Dialectic -- Jadal

Objection by Carrying to Legal Custom: Dialectical Method
Explains the objection of carrying to legal custom with a fiqh example and response methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection that a term must be interpreted according to legal custom and apply the two standard methods of responding to it.

Core Idea
Claiming a legal custom changes a term's meaning requires proof, not mere assertion.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), one common objection is that a term should be understood according to its legal 'urf rather than its linguistic origin. This lesson uses a real Maliki debate about whether pronouncing the basmalah is required for lawful slaughter. You will learn to recognise this objection and master the two classic methods of refuting it: either denying the existence of the legal custom or showing that the custom does not imply the claimed condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument and the disputed term
Find the original claim: Ibn al-Qassar argues that the Qur'anic verse (5:3) permits eating what is slaughtered even without basmalah. The key term is 'tadhkiya' (slaughter).
The original argument relies on the linguistic meaning of tadhkiya as 'slaughter'.
2
Recognise the objection using legal custom
Opponents object that 'tadhkiya' in the Law has a custom meaning that includes religious requirements like basmalah. They argue that intentionally omitting basmalah means the animal is not legally slaughtered.
The objection shifts the meaning from linguistic to legal custom.
3
Apply the first response: deny the custom and demand proof
The first method is to deny that the term has any legal custom distinct from its linguistic meaning. Demand that the objector provide evidence for the existence of such a custom.
This forces the objector to prove the custom, which may be difficult.
4
Apply the second response: show the custom does not include the condition
The second method is to accept a legal custom but argue that it does not require the condition. The author proves that tadhkiya means purification only, citing the hadith 'tanning of a hide is its tadhkiya' and the phrase 'pure soil'.
This undercuts the objection by showing the custom is broader than claimed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 - فأما الحمل على عرف الشرع، فمثاله : استدلال ابن القصار وغيره من بعض فقهاء المالكية على أن التسمية ليست بشرط في صحة الذبيحة، بقوله تعالى : وَمَا أَكَلَ السَّبُعُ إِلَّا مَا ذَكَيْتُمْ ...، ووجه الاستدلال أن هذا الذي لم يسمَّ عليه قد ذُكِّيّ، فوجب أن يباح أكله، وقد اعترض عليهم غيرهم من المالكية أيضًا بعدم التسليم بأن من ترك التسمية عامدًا يعتبر مذكيًا، لأن التذكية إذا أطلقت في الشرع فإنما يراد بها الذكاة الشرعية ومتى تعمد الذابح ترك التسمية لم يعتبر مذكيًا شرعًا. والطريق في الجواب على هذا النوع من الاعتراض، بأحد مسلكين : الأول : عدم التسليم بأن لفظة الذكاة لها عرف في الشرع غير ما وضعت له في اللغة وهو مطلق الذبح ومطالبة من يدعي وجود العرف الشرعي بالدليل على ذلك . الثاني : بيان أن الذكاة في الشريعة لا تقتضي التسمية، وإنما تقتضي التطييب فقط، والدليل على ذلك ما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : (دباغ الأديم ذكاته) ولا خلاف أنه لا يراد بذلك التسمية ؛ ثم إنه يقال تراب ذكي» إذا كان طيب الريح، وعليه لا دليل على اشتراط التسمية في الذكاة
1 - As for carrying to the legal custom, an example is the argument of Ibn al-Qassar and others from some Maliki jurists that the basmalah is not a condition for the validity of the slaughtered animal, by His statement, the Exalted: '...except what you have slaughtered...' The point of the argument is that this one upon which [the name] was not invoked has been slaughtered, so it is obligatory that its eating be permissible. And others from the Malikis also objected to them by not conceding that whoever intentionally omits the basmalah is considered a slaughterer, because when 'tadhkiya' is used absolutely in the Law, only the legal tadhkiya is meant, and whenever the slaughterer intentionally omits the basmalah, he is not considered a legal slaughterer. The way to answer this type of objection is by one of two approaches: First: not conceding that the word 'tadhkiya' has a custom in the Law other than what it was placed for in language, which is absolute slaughter, and demanding from the one who claims the existence of a legal custom evidence for that. Second: showing that tadhkiya in the Shari'a does not require the basmalah, but rather requires only purification [making good], and the evidence for that is what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'The tanning of a hide is its tadhkiya,' and there is no disagreement that the basmalah is not meant by that; then it is said 'pure soil' when it has a good smell, and based on this, there is no evidence for the condition of the basmalah in tadhkiya.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific type of dialectical objection in Islamic legal reasoning: the objection based on carrying a term to its legal custom ('urf al-shar'). The author presents an example from the Maliki school regarding the requirement of pronouncing the basmalah ("In the name of Allah") when slaughtering an animal. The original argument by Ibn al-Qassar and some Malikis cites the Qur'anic verse (5:3) which permits eating what is slaughtered, even if the basmalah is omitted. Their inference is that the animal has been made permissible (tadhkiya) and thus is lawful. The objectors, however, argue that the term 'tadhkiya' in the legal context is not simply the act of slaughter; it has a specific legal meaning that includes religious requirements. Therefore, if a slaughterer intentionally omits the basmalah, he is not performing legal tadhkiya, so the meat is not lawful. The author then explains two methods to counter this objection. The first method is to deny that the word 'tadhkiya' has a specialized legal custom different from its linguistic meaning, which is merely slaughter, and to demand proof from the one claiming such a custom. The second method is to demonstrate that in the Shari'a, tadhkiya does not entail the basmalah but only means purification (making the animal good to eat). Evidence is provided from the hadith that tanning a hide is its tadhkiya, which clearly does not involve the basmalah. Additionally, the Arabic word 'tadhkiya' is used for soil that is pure and pleasant-smelling, again without any basmalah. Therefore, there is no proof that the basmalah is required for tadhkiya. This illustrates how dialectical objections to apparent meanings can be addressed by either challenging the existence of a custom or providing counterevidence.

Key Terms
العرف الشرعي
التذكية
الحمل على العرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first method of responding to the objection that a term should be understood according to legal custom?
A) To argue that the basmalah is required in all cases.
B) To deny that the term has a legal custom and demand proof from the claimant.
C) To accept the custom but reinterpret it.
D) To cite the Qur'an directly without hadith.

Key Takeaway
The objection by carrying to legal custom can be answered either by denying the custom and demanding proof, or by showing the legal meaning does not incorporate the claimed condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the objection by carrying to legal custom in the following passage: 'The term 'salat' in the Quran means supplication, but objectors claim it must mean the ritual prayer due to legal custom.' Then state which of the two response methods could be used.
?
Construct a response using the second method (showing the custom does not require the condition) for the term 'hajj' if someone argues that legal custom requires visiting the Prophet's grave. Use evidence from Islamic sources to show that hajj originally means 'intention to visit' and does not include the grave.

The Linguistic Custom Objection in Dialectic
Explains the objection of carrying a term to its customary linguistic usage with a Hanafi example and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the objection of carrying a term to its linguistic custom in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Linguistic custom restricts a word's meaning unless evidence expands it.

This passage presents a classic dialectical move: the objector restricts the scope of a Qur'anic term by appealing to its customary usage. The Hanafi argues from the verse about 'coming from the toilet' (al-ghā'iṭ) to require ablution for any emission, including blood. The Maliki objects that linguistic custom has narrowed the term to mean only relieving oneself. You will learn how this objection works and how to respond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim and the term in dispute
The Hanafi uses the Qur'anic verse (4:43) as evidence that any emission (like blood) requires ablution, because the verse uses the word 'al-ghā'iṭ' without specifying. The term 'al-ghā'iṭ' is the focus of the debate.
The Hanafi's claim is that the verse covers all emissions, not just relieving oneself.
2
Locate the objector's move
The Maliki objects that the word 'al-ghā'iṭ' has a linguistic custom that restricts it to the act of relieving oneself. He argues that originally it meant a low place, but due to common usage for relieving oneself, it became customarily restricted.
The objection is not about the verse's authenticity but about the scope of its terms.
3
Analyze the objector's reasoning
The objector traces the semantic shift: originally 'al-ghā'iṭ' meant any low place, but because people used such places to relieve themselves, the word became customarily associated with that act. He asserts that a word with a linguistic custom must be interpreted according to that custom unless there is evidence for a broader meaning.
This is a standard principle in usul al-fiqh: al-ḥaml 'alā al-'urf al-lughawī.
4
Consider the arguer's possible responses
The source lists two responses: (1) deny that such a custom exists (difficult), or (2) produce evidence that the verse intends the original lexical meaning, thus moving beyond the custom.
The second response shifts the burden of proof to the arguer to provide external evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - وأما الحمل على عرف اللغة فمثاله : استدلال الحنفي على أن من خرج منه دم فعليه الوضوء، بقوله تعالى : ... أَو جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِّنكُم مِّنَ الْغَائِطِ ... فلم يفرق بين من خرج منه دم أو غيره . فيعترض عليه المالكي قائلا : إن هذه اللفظة كانت موضوعة لكل من أتى المكان المنخفض من الأرض سواء أحدث أو لم يُخدِث، إلا أنه جرى عرف استعمالها في اللغة لمن أتى قضاء الحاجة، لأنه لم تكن لهم مواضع يستترون فيها لقضاء الحاجة، فكان من أراد منهم ذلك أتى مكانًا منخفضًا من الأرض فاستتر فيه لقضاء حاجته، فكثر استعمالهم لهذه اللفظة بمعنى قضاء الحاجة حتى صار ذلك عرفا فيها واللفظة إذا كان لها عرف في اللغة حملت عليه ولم يتعد بها إلى غير موضوعها في كلام العرب إلا بدليل، وربما يجيب المستدل ذلك بأحد عن مسلکين : الأول : أن يمنع عرف اللغة إن استطاع ذلك ، وهو متعسر. والثاني : أن يبين الدليل الذي به عداه من المعنى المتعارف عليه إلى المعنى الموضوع له في أصل اللغة
2 - As for carrying to the linguistic custom, an example is the argument of the Hanafi that whoever has blood coming out of him must perform ablution, by His statement, the Exalted: '...or one of you comes from the toilet...' So he did not differentiate between one from whom blood comes out and others. So the Maliki objects to him saying: Indeed this word was placed for everyone who comes to an elevated place of the earth [i.e., a depression], whether he breaks wind or not, except that the custom of its usage in language continued for one who comes to relieve oneself, because they had no places to conceal themselves for relieving themselves, so whoever among them wanted that came to a low place of the earth and secreted himself there for his need, and their usage of this word for the meaning of relieving oneself increased until that became a custom in it. And if a word has a custom in language, it is carried upon it and one does not go beyond it to its non-original meaning in the speech of the Arabs except with evidence. And perhaps the arguer answers him by one of two approaches: First: to deny the linguistic custom if he can, and that is difficult. Second: to show the evidence by which he goes beyond it from the customary meaning to the original lexical meaning.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage explains the objector's move to carry a term to its linguistic custom ('urf al-lugha). The example is from Hanafi-Maliki debate about ablution upon the emission of blood. The Hanafi argues that the Qur'anic verse (4:43) mentions 'coming from the toilet' (al-ghā'iṭ) – a word originally meaning a low place – and uses it as a cause for ablution. He extends this to any emission like blood, since the verse does not differentiate. The Maliki objector argues that the word 'al-ghā'iṭ' originally meant a low place for any purpose, but gradually linguistic custom restricted it to the specific act of relieving oneself. Therefore, according to this custom, the verse only applies to the event of relieving oneself, not other forms of emissions like blood. The objector insists that a word with a well-established linguistic custom must be interpreted accordingly unless evidence shows it should be taken in its original broader sense. The arguer can respond in two ways: first, by attempting to deny the existence of such a linguistic custom, though this is difficult; second, by producing evidence that shows the word should be extended beyond its customary meaning back to its original lexical meaning. This illustrates the dialectical tension between literal and customary interpretations in Islamic legal argumentation.

Key Terms
العرف اللغوي
الغائط
المعنى الموضوع له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the linguistic custom objection, what is the Maliki objector's claim about the word 'al-ghā'iṭ'?
A) It was only ever used for relieving oneself.
B) It originally meant a low place but custom restricted it to relieving oneself.
C) It has no customary usage and should be taken literally.
D) It is a legal term with no linguistic origin.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic custom can restrict a term's meaning unless evidence supports a broader interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In the Hanafi-Maliki debate, identify the exact linguistic custom objection made by the Maliki, and then state the two possible responses from the Hanafi as given in the passage.
?
Task 2: Consider the word 'yad' (hand) in Qur'anic legal contexts. If a custom restricts its meaning to 'hand from wrist to fingertips', construct a similar objection against someone trying to apply it to the whole arm. Then suggest how the arguer might respond.

Hanafi-Maliki Debate on Linguistic Custom
Author presents the Hanafi-Maliki dialectical exchange over linguistic custom in interpreting shar'i terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between the Hanafi and Maliki over linguistic custom and identify the two possible rejoinders for the Hanafi.

Core Idea
Linguistic custom can override literal meaning unless evidence changes it.

This passage presents a classic debate in usul al-fiqh: how should a word be interpreted when its customary linguistic usage differs from its original lexical meaning? The Hanafi uses the verse about 'al-ghā'iṭ' to argue for ablution from any exit; the Maliki counters that the word has shifted meaning through custom. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to follow jadal arguments and apply the principle of 'urf al-lugha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi's claim and evidence
The Hanafi argues that blood requires wudu based on the verse 'or one of you comes from al-ghā'iṭ'. He reasons that the verse does not distinguish between different types of exit, so it covers all exits including blood.
This is a straightforward textual argument – the Hanafi takes the word at its apparent general meaning.
2
Understand the Maliki's objection
The Maliki objects by explaining that the word 'ghā'iṭ' originally meant 'low ground', but due to customary usage over time, it came to specifically mean 'relieving oneself'. Therefore, the verse applies only to that specific event, not to any exit like blood.
The basis of the objection is that the word has a dominant customary meaning that limits its scope.
3
Grasp the principle applied
The Maliki states that when a word has a customary usage in the language, it is to be interpreted according to that custom unless there is evidence to extend it beyond its custom.
This principle is key in usul al-fiqh: linguistic custom takes precedence over literal lexical meaning.
4
Explore the Hanafi's rebuttal options
The passage lists two approaches: either deny the existence of such a custom (which is difficult because the custom is well-established) or produce evidence that the word was used in its original sense beyond the custom.
The second option shifts the burden of proof – the Hanafi must bring evidence that the word retained its original meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - وأما الحمل على عرف اللغة فمثاله : استدلال الحنفي على أن من خرج منه دم فعليه الوضوء، بقوله تعالى : ﴿ أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنْكُمْ مِنَ الْغَائِطِ ﴾ فلم يفرق بين من خرج منه دم أو غيره . فيعترض عليه المالكي قائلا : إن هذه اللفظة كانت موضوعة لكل من أتى المكان المنخفض من الأرض سواء أحدث أو لم يُخدِث، إلا أنه جرى عرف استعمالها في اللغة لمن أتى قضاء الحاجة، لأنه لم تكن لهم مواضع يستترون فيها لقضاء الحاجة، فكان من أراد منهم ذلك أتى مكانًا منخفضًا من الأرض فاستتر فيه لقضاء حاجته، فكثر استعمالهم لهذه اللفظة بمعنى قضاء الحاجة حتى صار ذلك عرفا فيها واللفظة إذا كان لها عرف في اللغة حملت عليه ولم يتعد بها إلى غير موضوعها في كلام العرب إلا بدليل، وربما يجيب المستدل ذلك بأحد مسلكين : الأول : أن يمنع عرف اللغة إن استطاع ذلك ، وهو متعسر. والثاني : أن يبين الدليل الذي به عداه من المعنى المتعارف عليه إلى المعنى الموضوع له في أصل اللغة
2 - As for the imposition [of the meaning] based on the custom of the language, its example is: the Hanafi's inference that whoever has blood coming out of him must perform ablution, by His statement, the Exalted: 'Or one of you comes from the place of relief [al-ghā'iṭ]' – so he did not distinguish between one from whom blood emerges or something else. The Maliki objects to him, saying: This word was originally placed for everyone who came to a low place of the earth, whether he had an event or not, except that the customary usage of it in the language came to be for one who came to relieve himself, because they did not have places in which to conceal themselves for relieving themselves. So whoever among them wanted that would come to a low place of the earth and conceal himself therein for relieving himself. Thus their usage of this word increased in the sense of relieving oneself until it became a custom in it. And when a word has a custom in the language, it is imposed upon it and is not extended to other than its original meaning in the speech of the Arabs except with evidence. And perhaps the arguer responds to that by one of two approaches: the first: to deny the linguistic custom if he is able, and that is difficult. The second: to show the evidence by which he transferred from the customary meaning to the original lexical meaning.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange over the use of linguistic custom ('urf al-lugha) in interpreting legal texts. The Hanafi uses the verse 'or one of you comes from al-ghā'iṭ' to argue that any exit (including blood) necessitates ablution, since the verse does not specify. The Maliki counters that the word 'ghā'iṭ' originally meant any low ground, but customary usage in the language had shifted it to mean 'relieving oneself', because people used to go to low places for privacy. Therefore, the word must be understood in its customary sense unless there is evidence to extend it. The arguer (the Hanafi) can respond in two ways: either deny the existence of such a custom (difficult), or produce evidence that the term was used outside its custom. This illustrates a key principle in jadal: when a word has a dominant customary meaning, it is binding unless proven otherwise.

Key Terms
عرف اللغة
حمل على عرف اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The literal definition of the word
B) The customary usage in the language
C) A specific hadith
D) Qiyas

Key Takeaway
Linguistic custom can shift a word's meaning, and in usul al-fiqh, the customary meaning takes priority unless evidence shows otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which side has the stronger case and why? Use the principle of 'urf al-lugha.

Responding to Objections on Polysemy: The Case of 'Afw
Author gives the example of 'afw and the Maliki/Shafi'i vs. Hanafi dialectical exchange over two possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two juristic responses (frequency/apparency and grammatical context) to an objection based on a word with two possible meanings.

Core Idea
When a word has two meanings, the apparent and frequent one is preferred unless evidence shifts it.

In dialectical argument, opponents may exploit a word's multiple meanings to undermine a proof from revelation. This lesson unpacks a classic usul al-fiqh example: the Maliki/Shafi'i use of the verse on 'afw (pardon) to permit blood money without the killer's consent, and the Hanafi objection that 'afw could mean 'giving freely' (bazl). Two robust responses are offered, one based on lexical frequency and the other on grammatical coherence. Studying this exchange trains you to defend scriptural proofs by showing how apparent meanings and context resolve ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and its scriptural basis
The Maliki and Shafi'i argue that the verse 'faman 'ufiya lahu…' proves that if pardon from qisas is granted, blood money (diya) may be demanded without the killer's consent. Locate the verse and the reasoning.
The verse is from Surah al-Baqarah, 2:178.
2
Find the objection: what alternative meaning does the Hanafi propose?
The Hanafi objects that 'afw can also mean 'bazl' (giving freely), citing the verse 'khudh al-'afw' (take what is easy). This would change the meaning of the proof: the verse might refer to the killer voluntarily paying diya.
The objection challenges the certainty of the interpretation.
3
Analyze the first response: lexical frequency and apparency
The first response argues that 'afw is more frequently used for forgiveness/pardon (taubah wa safh), proven by multiple Quranic verses and a hadith. When a word has two meanings, the more apparent and frequent (aẓhar) is obligatory unless evidence shifts it.
This response invokes the principle 'al-haqiqah al-shar'iyyah or al-ghalibah.
4
Analyze the second response: grammatical context (siyaq)
The second response points out that 'faman 'ufiya lahu' refers back to the killer mentioned earlier in the verse ('al-hurru bil-hurr'), not to the guardian. Thus it cannot mean the killer giving diya; it must mean the guardian pardoning the killer.
Context resolves the ambiguity without relying on frequency.
5
Evaluate the overall dialectical outcome
The Hanafi objection is neutralized by two independent arguments. The Maliki/Shafi'i position stands. This illustrates how two distinct strategies can defend a proof against ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 - أن يكون اللفظ في اللغة موضوعًا لمعنيين وهو في أحدهما أظهر : ومثاله : استدلال المالكي والشافعي في جواز العفو عن القصاص على الدية من غير رضا الجاني، بقوله تعالى : ﴿ فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ مِنْ أَخِيهِ شَيْءٌ فَاتِّبَاعٌ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَأَدَاءٌ إِلَيْهِ بِإِحْسَانٍ ﴾ فدلت الآية على أنه إذا أعفي له جازت المطالبة بالدية . وهنا قد يعترض عليهما الحنفي بأن يقول : إن العفو في اللغة قد يراد به البذل، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿ خُذِ الْعَفْوَ ﴾ والمراد به : ما سهل أو ما تيسر، فيمكن أن يكون المراد بالآية الأولى : إذا بذل الجاني للولي الدية، اتبع بالمعروف . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض قد يأتي بأحد مسلكين : أحدهما : أن يبين أن العفو، وإن كان يستعمل في معنى البذل إلا أنه في التوبة والصفح أظهر ، لأن ورودها في الترك أكثر ، ومعناها به ألحق وأشبه، ولهذا قال عز وجل : ﴿ فَاعْفُوا وَأَصْفَحُوا ﴾ ، وقال : ﴿ وَاعْفُ عَنَّا وَاغْفِرْ لَنَا﴾ ، أي اترك لنا واصفح ومن ذلك أيضًا ما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : ( إني قد عفوت لكم عن صدقة الخيل والرقيق)، والمراد به : تركت لكم . واللفظ إذا احتمل معنيين وجب حمله على أظهرهما وأشهرهما، ولا يحمل على الآخر إلا بدليل . والثاني : أن يبين أن قوله عز وجل : ﴿ فَمَنْ عُفِيَ لَهُ ﴾ متعلق بما تقدم ذكره، وإنما تقدم ذكر القاتل في قوله ﴿ الْحُرُّ بِالْحُرِّ ﴾ من نفس الآية، ولم يجر للولي ذكر، فلا يجوز حمله على ما ذكروه
1 - That the word in the language is placed for two meanings, and it is more apparent in one of them: its example is: the Maliki's and Shafi'i's inference regarding the permissibility of pardon from retaliation (qisas) in exchange for blood money without the consent of the offender, by His statement, the Exalted: 'Then whoever is pardoned something by his brother, let him follow with kindness and pay with goodness.' So the verse indicates that when pardon is given, it is permissible to demand the blood money. Here the Hanafi may object to them by saying: 'Afw in the language may be intended to mean giving freely (bazl), and from it is His statement, the Exalted: 'Take the pardon (al-'afw)' meaning what is easy or available. So it is possible that the intended meaning in the first verse is: when the offender gives the blood money to the guardian, let him follow with kindness. The answer to this objection may come by one of two approaches: One: to show that 'afw, even if it is used in the sense of bazl, yet in the sense of forgiveness and pardon it is more apparent, because its occurrence [in the meaning of] leaving/pardoning is more frequent, and its meaning is more relevant and similar to it. Hence He, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'So forgive and pardon (fa'fwā waṣfaḥū)' and said: 'And pardon us and forgive us (wa'fu 'annā waghfir lanā)' meaning: leave for us and pardon. Also from that is what is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'I have pardoned you from the sadaqa of horses and slaves,' meaning: I have left for you. And when a word can bear two meanings, it is obligatory to take it in the more apparent and more famous of them, and it is not taken in the other except by evidence. Second: to show that His statement, Mighty and Majestic: 'Then whoever is pardoned him (faman 'ufiya lahu)' is connected to what preceded, and what preceded is the mention of the killer in His statement 'The free for the free' from the same verse, and the guardian is not mentioned, so it is not permissible to take it in what they mentioned.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange concerning a word that has two possible meanings, one of which is more apparent (aẓhar). The Maliki and Shafi'i argue from the verse about 'afw that pardon from retaliation implies the permissibility of taking blood money (diyah). The Hanafi objects that 'afw can also mean 'giving freely' (bazl), as in 'Take the pardon' (khudh al-'afw) meaning 'what is easy'. So the verse could mean the killer pays the blood money. Two responses are given: First, that 'afw is more commonly used in the sense of forgiveness and pardoning (taubah wa ṣafḥ), as attested by multiple Quranic verses and a hadith. Therefore, when a word has two meanings, it must be interpreted in its more apparent and famous sense unless there is evidence to shift it. Second, the grammatical context: the phrase 'faman 'ufiya lahu' refers to the killer mentioned earlier in the verse ('the free for the free'), not to the guardian, so it cannot mean the killer giving blood money. This illustrates the principles of interpreting polysemous words (mujmal/mushkil) in jadal.

Key Terms
العفو
أظهر المعنيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first response to the Hanafi objection that 'afw could mean 'bazl' (giving freely)?
A) The word 'afw is never used for bazl
B) The context of the verse shows it refers to the killer
C) The meaning of forgiveness is more apparent and frequent
D) There is a consensus on the meaning

Key Takeaway
Two independent strategies—appealing to lexical frequency and to grammatical context—can effectively neutralize objections based on a word's alternative meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the word 'yad' (hand) in the Quranic verse 'lil-rijaali nasiibun mimma kasabu wa lil-nisaai nasiibun mimma kasabna' (4:32). The First Type of Jadal Objection: Equally Probable Meanings
Defines the first type of jadal objection: word with two equally probable meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a word in a legal proof has two equally strong meanings and apply the dialectical response to such an objection.

Core Idea
An ambiguous word with equally strong meanings does not invalidate the proof; it extends the ruling to both.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one common objection is that the proof text contains a word that could mean something else. This lesson introduces the first type of such objection, where both meanings are equally probable. Through a Maliki example on hunting in the sanctuary, you will learn how to handle this situation: either both meanings apply if they do not conflict, or one must be preponderated by external evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested word
Find the key term in the proof that the objector claims has an alternative meaning. In the example, it is 'hurum' in the verse about hunting.
2
Determine the two possible meanings
List both interpretations and check if they are equally strong without preference. The Maliki says 'hurum' means 'those in the sanctuary'; the objector says it means 'those in ihram for Hajj or Umrah'.
3
Check for contradiction
If both meanings can apply simultaneously without contradiction, the ruling covers both contexts. Here, both 'being in the sanctuary' and 'being in ihram' are possible and not mutually exclusive.
4
Handle preponderance
If one meaning is stronger, evidence must be brought to justify that preference. The passage states that without such evidence, both meanings are equally valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون اللفظ موضوعًا لمعنيين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر: ومثاله: أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب الجزاء على من قتل الصيد في الحرم بقوله عز وجل: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا لَا تَقْتُلُوا الصَّيْدَ وَأَنْتُمْ حُرُمٌ وَمَنْ قَتَلَهُ مِنْكُمْ مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاءٌ مِثْلُ مَا قَتَلَ مِنَ النَّعَمِ فيعترض عليه بأن الآية تحتمل معنى ثانيًا غير الذي فسرتها به، وهو أن المراد بالحُرُم: المُحْرِمُون بالحج والعمرة. والجواب: أن الحُرُم وصف يوصف به الداخلون في الحَرَم، كما يوصف به المحرمون بالحج، وإذا كان اللفظ يحتملهما جميعًا معًا احتمالا واحدا، حملناه عليهما وأجرينا الحكم فيهما، لأنه لا تنافي بينهما. وإذا كان لأحدهما ترجيح لأحد المعنيين، وجب إيراد الحجة في هذا الترجيح.
That the word is assigned to two meanings with no preference for one over the other. Example: The Maliki argues for the obligation of a penalty for killing game in the sanctuary by the Almighty's saying: 'O you who believe, do not kill game while you are in a state of ihram (or sanctuary). And whoever of you kills it intentionally, then a penalty the like of what he killed from livestock...' Then he objects that the verse can bear a second meaning other than what you interpreted, which is that 'hurum' means those in ihram for Hajj or Umrah. The answer: 'Hurum' is an attribute used for those entering the sanctuary just as it is used for those in ihram for Hajj. If the word bears both equally, we apply it to both and execute the ruling concerning both, because there is no conflict between them. If one of the two meanings has a preponderance, then evidence must be brought for that preponderance.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of objection in jadal: when the textual evidence uses a word that has two equally possible meanings. The objector points out an alternative meaning, claiming the text is ambiguous. The proponent must either show why the alternative is not applicable or accept that both meanings are valid, as long as they do not contradict each other. Here, the Maliki argues that 'hurum' in the verse about hunting means 'those in the sanctuary', while the objector says it means 'those in ihram for Hajj'. The response is that the word can be used for both, so the ruling applies to both, unless one meaning is proven stronger by external evidence.

Key Terms
اللفظ
المزية
احتمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example, what is the alternative meaning of 'hurum' that the objector proposes?
A) Those in the sanctuary
B) Those in ihram for Hajj or Umrah
C) Those who are permissible
D) Those who are free

Key Takeaway
When a word in a proof text has two equally strong meanings and they are not contradictory, the ruling applies to both, and no objection holds unless one meaning is proven stronger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection from Disputed Assignment of a Word
Defines the second type of jadal objection: conflicting claims about a word's customary meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dialectical objection hinges on disputed conventional assignment of a word and reconstruct the argument and counter-argument.

Core Idea
A single word can be the battleground of an entire legal debate when its conventional meaning is disputed.

In this lesson, we explore the second type of jadal objection: when two parties disagree on the conventional assignment (wad') of a word. This is a powerful dialectical tool because it strikes at the very foundation of an opponent's interpretation. The passage provides a concrete example from a Maliki-Shafi'i dispute over the verse about the moons. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to spot such objections and analyze the linguistic reasoning involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed word and the two conflicting assignments
Look for the key word whose meaning is contested. In the example, the word 'and' (و) in the verse 'times for the people and the Hajj' is disputed: the Maliki claims it assigns all moons to both purposes, while the Shafi'i claims it assigns some moons to people and some to Hajj.
The dispute is not about the word's literal meaning but about its conventional assignment (wad') in this context.
2
Extract the claimant's argument based on his interpretation
The Maliki argues that since all moons are times for people without dispute, and the verse uses the same phrase for Hajj, all moons must be times for Hajj. Thus ihram for Hajj is valid any time.
The Maliki's interpretation treats 'and' as purely conjunctive, adding Hajj to the same set as people.
3
Identify the objector's challenge and how it relies on a different assignment
The Shafi'i objects that the apparent meaning (zahir) of the verse requires distribution: some moons for people, some for Hajj. He argues that the Maliki's interpretation violates this apparent meaning.
The Shafi'i's assignment treats 'and' as implying division (tab'id) between two different categories.
4
Analyze the response and assess the strength of each position
The Maliki counters that if God intended division, He would have explicitly said 'some... and some'. He also notes that all scholars agree all moons are times for people, so consistency demands they all are times for Hajj unless evidence restricts.
This response appeals to the default rule: the apparent meaning is inclusive unless there is a linguistic marker for distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون اللفظ مُتنازع الوضع فيدعي كل منهما أنه موضوع للمعنى الذي يدعيه. ومثاله: أن يستدل المالكي في أن الإحرام بالحج يصح في غير أشهر الحج بقوله تعالى: يَسْأَلُونَكَ عَنِ الْأَهِلَّةِ قُلْ هِيَ مَوَاقِيتُ لِلنَّاسِ وَالْحَجِّ فقد جعلها كلها مواقيت للحج، فصح أن يحرم في جميعها للحج. فيعترض عليه الشافعي بأن يقول: هذه الآية حجة عليكم، لا لكم، لأن الله تعالى قال: قُلْ هِيَ مَوَاقِيتُ لِلنَّاسِ وَالْحَجِّ، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون بعضها مواقيت للناس، وبعضها مواقيت للحج، فالقول باعتبار جميع الأهلة مواقيت للناس وجميعها مواقيت للحج مخالفة لظاهر الآية. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول المالكي: إن ظاهر الآية يقتضي كون جميعها مواقيت للناس وجميعها مواقيت للحج، ولو أراد الله عز وجل التبعيض لقال: بعضها مواقيت للناس وبعضها مواقيت للحج، والدليل على صحة هذا التأويل أن جميعها بلا خلاف مواقيت للناس، وليس في الأمة من يقول: إن بعضها مواقيت للحج ولا يصح أن يكون مواقيت للناس في بيوعهم ومشترياتهم.
That the word is disputed in assignment, each party claiming it is assigned to the meaning he advocates. Example: The Maliki argues that ihram for Hajj is valid outside the months of Hajj by the Almighty's saying: 'They ask you about the new moons. Say: They are times for the people and for the Hajj.' He made all of them times for Hajj, so it is valid to assume ihram for Hajj in all of them. Then the Shafi'i objects by saying: This verse is evidence against you, not for you, because Allah said '...times for the people and for the Hajj', and this requires that some of them are times for the people and some are times for the Hajj. Saying that all the moons are times for the people and all are times for the Hajj contradicts the apparent meaning of the verse. The answer to this objection is that the Maliki says: The apparent meaning of the verse requires that all of them are times for the people and all are times for the Hajj. If Allah had intended division, He would have said 'some of them are times for the people and some are times for the Hajj'. The proof of the validity of this interpretation is that all of them are times for the people without dispute, and there is no one in the Ummah who says that some of them are times for the Hajj, and it would not be valid for them to be times for the people in their sales and purchases.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of jadal objection: when two parties disagree about the conventional assignment (wad') of a word. The example involves the verse about the moons. The Maliki argues that 'times for the people and the Hajj' means all moons serve both purposes, so Hajj can be done in any month. The Shafi'i objects that this forces an unapparent meaning, because the structure 'for the people and the Hajj' usually implies distribution (some for one, some for the other). The Maliki counters that the apparent meaning is inclusive, and if God wanted distribution He would have said 'some... and some'. Moreover, all scholars agree that all moons are times for people's transactions, so logically they must all be times for Hajj as well, unless there is a specific restriction.

Key Terms
مُتنازع الوضع
الظاهر
التبعيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Maliki reject the Shafi'i's objection about the distribution of moons?
A) Because the Shafi'i misinterpreted the verse
B) Because God would have used 'some...some' to indicate distribution
C) Because all moons are unanimously times for people
D) Because the verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
The objection from disputed assignment uses linguistic ambiguity to challenge an opponent's argument; resolving it requires appeal to the apparent meaning (zahir) and linguistic defaults.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection from Legal Custom vs. Linguistic Meaning
Defines the third type of jadal objection: conflict between linguistic and legal custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the jadal objection that contests the apparent meaning by appealing to legal custom versus linguistic usage.

Core Idea
Legal custom can challenge the apparent meaning by redefining key terms.

In jadal (dialectical debate), a common move is to contest the apparent meaning (zahir) of a revealed text by arguing that the term should be understood according to its linguistic origin rather than its legal custom. This lesson unpacks the third type of such objection, using the Maliki argument on marrying slave women as a case study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
In the example, the Maliki uses the verse to prohibit marriage to a slave woman for a wealthy free man. The objector challenges this by redefining 'nikah'.
2
Distinguish linguistic from legal custom
The objector claims 'nikah' in language means intercourse, so the verse only permits intercourse with slaves if unable to afford free women for intercourse, not marriage.
3
Formulate the objector's objection
The objection is that the apparent meaning is not the legal custom but the linguistic meaning, thus the verse does not support the Maliki's ruling.
4
Examine the two responses
The defender can either prove that legal custom prevails (nikah means contract in sharia) or show contextual evidence (requirement of wealth, 'fearing hardship') that indicates marriage.
Both responses are valid rebuttals in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنازعة في الاستدلال بالظاهر من جهة العرف الشرعي. والمنازعة هنا تكون بمناكرة العرف وحمل اللفظ على مقتضاه في اللغة. مثاله: أن يستدل المالكي في أن الحر الموسر لا يجوز أن يتزوج الأمة، بقوله تعالى: وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلًا أَنْ يَنْكِحَ الْمُحْصَنَاتِ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ فَمِنْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ مِنْ فَتَيَاتِكُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ فقد أباحت الآية نكاح الأمة بشرط عدم طول الحرة. ويعترض على هذا الاستدلال بأن يقول المخالف: هذا لا حجة فيه، لأن النكاح في اللغة هو الوطء، فكأنه قال: ومن لم يستطع منكم أن يطأ الحرائر فليطأ بملك اليمين، وهذا إذا لم تكن عنده حرة غير مستطيع لوطئها، فلا يدخل في التحريم. والطريق في الجواب عن ذلك، أحد أمرين: الأول: أن يقول: إن النكاح في عرف الشرع عبارة عن العقد، والدليل عليه أن كل موضع ورد الشرع به، فالمراد به العقد. والثاني: أن يبين ما يمنع الحمل على ما ذكروه من الوطء، بأن يقول: قد قال في آخر الآية المستدل بها: ذَلِكَ لِمَنْ خَشِيَ الْعَنَتَ مِنْكُمْ وهذا لا يعتبره المخالف، وأيضًا فقد بين تبارك وتعالى أنه إنما أراد بالنكاح العقد بقوله: وَمَنْ لَمْ يَسْتَطِعْ مِنْكُمْ طَوْلًا والطول المال، وإنما يفتقر إليه العقد دون الوطء.
The objection concerning inference from the apparent meaning from the perspective of legal custom. The objection here is by contesting the custom and interpreting the word according to its linguistic requirement. Example: The Maliki argues that a free wealthy man is not permitted to marry a slave woman by the Almighty's saying: 'And whoever among you cannot afford wealth to marry believing free women, then from what your right hands possess of your believing slave girls.' The verse permitted marriage of slave women on condition of not having the means for a free woman. Then the opponent objects by saying: This is not evidence, because 'nikah' in language is intercourse, so it is as if He said: 'Whoever cannot afford to have intercourse with free women, let him have intercourse with what his right hand possesses.' This is when he does not have a free woman and is unable to have intercourse with her, so it does not fall under prohibition. The method of answering this is one of two things: First: He says that 'nikah' in the custom of the law is the contract, and the evidence is that wherever the law uses it, it intends the contract. Second: He shows what prevents interpreting it as they claim from intercourse, by saying: He said at the end of the verse: 'That is for whoever fears hardship among you' and the opponent does not consider this, and also the Blessed and Exalted One explained that He only intended by 'nikah' the contract by His saying: 'And whoever cannot afford wealth' and wealth is money, and it is the contract that requires money, not intercourse.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of jadal objection: when the apparent meaning is challenged based on the legal custom (al-'urf al-shar'i) versus linguistic meaning. The example is the verse about marrying slave women. The Maliki uses the verse to argue that a free man who can afford to marry a free woman is forbidden from marrying a slave. The objector claims that 'nikah' in language means intercourse, so the verse only permits intercourse with slave women if one cannot afford intercourse with free women, thus not a marriage ruling. The author then gives two ways to answer. First, prove that in legal custom 'nikah' means contract, citing that whenever the law uses it, it intends the marriage contract. Second, point to contextual evidence: the verse mentions 'wealth' (tawl) needed for the contract, not for intercourse, and also the phrase 'for whoever fears hardship' shows it is about marriage, not just intercourse.

Key Terms
العرف الشرعي
النكاح
الطول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the objector's linguistic interpretation of 'nikah' in the verse?
A) Marriage contract
B) Engagement
C) Intercourse
D) Affection

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection based on legal custom vs. linguistic meaning challenges the apparent meaning by redefining key terms, requiring a response that either establishes legal custom as primary or uses contextual evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Identify the debate map in the following scenario: A Hanafi argues that the hand in the verse of theft (5:38) refers to the wrist joint; a Shafi'i says it means the elbow. Formulate the objection based on linguistic versus legal custom.

Two Types of Objection by Indication: Implicit Element and Word Substitution
Two subtypes of objection to apparent meaning based on indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the two subtypes of objection based on indication (taqdir mahdhuf and iqamat lafz) and reconstruct the dialectical exchange in a usul al-fiqh debate.

Core Idea
Objections based on indication come in two forms: adding an implicit word or substituting a word.

In jadal (dialectical debate), a proponent often relies on the apparent meaning (al-zahir) of a text. The opponent can object by challenging the indication (dalalah) of that meaning. This lesson introduces two such objection types: claiming the argument only works if an omitted element is supplied (taqdir mahdhuf), or if a word is replaced (iqamat lafz). We'll explore a classic fiqh debate between Maliki and Shafi'i schools to see this in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and textual support
The Maliki argues that the two judges in the penalty for killing game cannot be the judged person. He uses verse 5:95, 'yahkanu bihi dhawu 'adlin minkum'.
The proponent's argument relies on the apparent meaning of the verse.
2
Find the objector's challenge
The Shafi'i objects: 'We do not accept that the verse means what you claim, because it is independent without assuming an omitted element.'
The objector denies the need for an implicit element, claiming the verse is self-sufficient.
3
Analyze the key move: the proponent's response
The Maliki replies that the verse requires an implied 'against you' (alaykum) because judging oneself is impossible. He draws a parallel with the witness verse (65:2).
This response shows the necessity of taqdir mahdhuf for the argument to hold.
4
Distinguish the second subtype
In C-2, the Maliki argues about touching the Quran using verse 56:79, but the text cuts off. The objection would be that 'touch' (yamasu) might mean 'understand', requiring a word substitution.
Here the debate revolves around whether a word should be replaced by another to support the claim.
5
Evaluate the debate according to jadal rules
The Maliki successfully defends his position by showing the implicit element is necessary. The Shafi'i's objection fails if he cannot refute the necessity of the taqdir.
In jadal, the proponent wins if he can rebut the objection with a sound argument from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - المنازعة في الاستدلال بالظاهر من جهة الدلالة: وهو نوعان : ج - 1 - ما لا يتم الدليل منه إلا بتقدير محذوف مضمر : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على أن الحاكم في جزاء قتل الصيد لا يكون المحكوم عليه، بقوله تعالى : ﴿... يَحْكُمُ بِهِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِنكُمْ ...﴾، ووجه الدلالة من الآية أن الحاكم يقتضي محكوما عليه، فكأنه تعالى قال : يحكم به ذوا عدل منكم عليكم، وهذا يوجب أن يكون الحاكمان غير المحكوم عليه، لأن الإنسان لا يحكم على نفسه. فيعترض عليه الشافعي قائلا : لا نسلم أن المراد بالآية ما أردتم، لأنه يستقل من غير تقدير محذوف ولا إضمار، وذلك أن قوله تعالى: ﴿... يَحْكُمُ بِهِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ منكم﴾ خطاب للمسلمين، فيجب أن يُحمل ذلك على كل رجل عدل من المخاطبين والمحكوم عليه من جملتهم، فصح أن يكون المحكوم عليه أحد الحكمين، ولا يمتنع أن يحكم الإنسان على نفسه. والجواب : أن يبين المستدل أن الكلام لا يستقل بنفسه إلا بالمضمر، ويتكلم على كلام المعترض بما يرده، وذلك أن يقول : قوله تعالى : ﴿... يَحْكُمُ بِهِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِّنكُمْ ...﴾ يقتضي أن يكون الحَكَمان غير المحكوم عليه، لأنه يستحيل أن يحكم الإنسان على نفسه، لأن معنى الحكم عليه القهر له والرد عن الباطل إلى الحق، وذلك يستحيل من الإنسان في نفسه كما يستحيل أن يأمرها وينهاها، فاقتضى ذلك أن يكون المحكوم عليه غير الحَكَمين، كما اقتضى ذلك في قوله تعالى: ﴿... وَأَشْهِدُوا ذَوَى عَدْلٍ مِّنكُم ...﴾ أن يكون ذوا العدل غير المشهود عليه. ج - 2 - ما لا يتم الاستدلال معه إلا بإقامة لفظ مكان لفظ : ومثاله : استدلال المالكي على أن المحدث لا يمس المصحف، بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَّرُونَ تَنزِيلٌ مِّن رَّبِّ الْعَلَمِينَ﴾، ووجه الاستدلال منه : أن لفظه لفظ ...
C - Objection in arguing by apparent meaning from the perspective of indication: It is of two types: C-1: That which the proof is not complete except by assuming an omitted element. Example: The Maliki argues that the judge regarding the penalty for killing game cannot be the same as the person judged, citing the verse: '...judged by two just men among you...' (Quran 5:95). The indication from the verse is that a judge requires a judged person, as if He said: judged by two just men among you against you. This requires that the two judges be other than the person judged, because a person cannot judge himself. The Shafi'i objects: We do not accept that the verse means what you claim, because it is independent without assuming an omitted element, i.e., His saying '...judged by two just men among you' is addressed to Muslims, so it must be applied to every just man from among those addressed, and the person judged is among them, so it is valid that the judged be one of the judges, and there is no impossibility in a person judging himself. The answer: The proponent must show that the speech is not independent except with the implied element, and discuss the objector's words to refute them, by saying: His verse '...judged by two just men among you...' requires that the two judges be other than the person judged, because it is impossible for a person to judge himself, since the meaning of judging against him is coercing him and turning him from falsehood to truth, and that is impossible from a person regarding himself as it is impossible for him to command and forbid himself. Hence it necessitates that the person judged be other than the two judges, as was necessitated in His verse '...and call to witness two just men among you...' (Quran 65:2) that the two witnesses be other than the person witnessed against. C-2: That which the argument is not complete except by substituting one word for another. Example: The Maliki argues that a person in a state of major ritual impurity cannot touch the Quran, citing the verse: 'None touch it except the purified, a revelation from the Lord of the worlds' (Quran 56:79-80). The indication from it: that its wording is... [incomplete]
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of munar'a (objection) against istidlal bi al-zahir (arguing by apparent meaning) from the perspective of dalalah (indication). The author divides this type into two subtypes. The first subtype (C-1) is when the proof is incomplete unless an implicit element (taqdir mahdhuf) is assumed. The author provides an example from fiqh: the Maliki argues that the two judges in the case of killing game cannot be the person who committed the act. He uses the verse 'judged by two just men among you' and infers that the judges must be other than the judged, as judging oneself is impossible. The Shafi'i objects by saying the verse is independent without any implicit element; the addressees are Muslims in general, so the judged could be among them. The Maliki then responds by arguing that the verse requires an implicit 'against you' (alaykum) to make sense, because the concept of judging inherently involves a distinct subject and object; a person cannot judge himself as it would be coercing oneself, which is absurd. He draws a parallel with the verse about witnesses. The second subtype (C-2) is when the argument requires substituting one word for another (iqamat lafz makan lafz). The example given is the Maliki's deduction that a person in a state of impurity cannot touch the Quran, based on the verse 'None touch it except the purified.' The Maliki interprets 'touch' (yamasu) literally as physical touch, but the opponent might argue that the intended meaning is 'understanding' or 'revealing'. The text cuts off, but the pattern is similar: the Maliki's argument relies on a specific substitution to support his claim, and the opponent would object by disputing that substitution.

Key Terms
تقدير محذوف (taqdir mahdhuf)
إقامة لفظ مكان لفظ (iqamat lafz makan lafz)
الدلالة (al-dalalah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of C-1, what implicit element does the Maliki argue is required to complete the verse?
A) 'against you' (عليكم)
B) 'for you' (لكم)
C) 'from among you' (منكم)
D) 'just' (عدل)

Key Takeaway
The two subtypes of objection based on indication challenge the completeness of a textual argument: one requires adding an omitted word, the other requires substituting a word; both are valid dialectical moves in jadal.

Responding to Objections in Dialectic: The Khabar vs Nahy Argument
Author presents an objection and response to a khabar-based argument about touching the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a dialectical objection and response in usul al-fiqh and evaluate which side prevails based on contextual evidence.

Core Idea
Contextual evidence can override the apparent meaning in jadal.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), an objector challenges an inference by insisting on the apparent meaning of a text. This lesson dissects such an exchange: the objector takes a verse as a mere report (khabar) while the proponent argues it carries a prohibition (nahy). You will learn how the proponent uses additional Quranic evidence to tip the scale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Find the proponent's initial inference: the verse 'لَا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَّرُونَ' means 'do not touch it unless pure', i.e., a prohibition in report form.
The proponent uses the claim that the wording is khabar but the meaning is nahy.
2
Extract the objector's point of attack
The objector insists on the zahir (apparent meaning): the verse is a straightforward report about the Preserved Tablet touched only by angels.
He supports this with another verse about purified scrolls handled by angels.
3
Analyze the proponent's response
The proponent invalidates the objector's interpretation by citing another verse (al-Waqi‘ah 56:80) showing the Quran is a revelation, so the reference is to the Mushaf, not the Preserved Tablet.
The response uses contextual evidence (qarīnah) to override the apparent meaning.
4
Evaluate who prevails
The proponent strengthens his case by providing a stronger contextual link (the Mushaf is revealed) and thereby defends the prohibition interpretation.
In jadal, the side with more or stronger evidence wins; here the proponent appears to succeed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فثبت أن لفظه لفظ الخبر، ومعناه النهي عن أن يمسه إلا طاهر. ووجه الاعتراض على هذا الاستدلال : أن يحمل المعترض اللفظ على ظاهره ويتأول ما ذكره من الدليل، فيقول : ﴿لَا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَرُونَ إخبار وليس بنهي، ولو كان نهيًا لقال : لا يمسسه ولا يمسه وإذا ثبت أنه إخبار، كان المراد به الإخبار عن اللوح المحفوظ، ولا يمسه إلا طاهر وهم الملائكة، كما قال تعالى : في صحف مكرمة مرفوعة مطهرة بأيدي سفرة كرام بررة. والطريق في الجواب عن ذلك : أن يبطل المستدل ما حمل عليه المعترض اللفظ ، بأن يبين أن المراد به ما ذكره هو، ويقوي دليله ويدفع اعتراض الخصم ويقول : مما يبين أن المراد به المصحف أن الله تعالى قال : تَنزِيلٌ مِّن رَّبِّ العالمين والصحف لم تنزل إلينا إلا وهي منزلة ويرجح بعض الترجيح
It is established that its wording is the wording of a report, and its meaning is the prohibition of touching it except while pure. And the manner of objection to this inference is: that the objector takes the wording at its apparent meaning and reinterprets the evidence he mentioned, saying: 'No one touches it except the purified' is a report, not a prohibition; and had it been a prohibition, He would have said: 'Let him not touch it' or 'do not touch it'. And when it is established that it is a report, then what is meant by it is the information about the Preserved Tablet, and none touches it except the pure, who are the angels, as the Exalted said: In honorable scrolls, exalted, purified, in the hands of scribes, noble, righteous. And the way to answer that is: that the proponent invalidates what the objector attributed to the wording, by showing that what is meant is what he himself mentioned, and strengthens his evidence and repels the opponent's objection, saying: Among what shows that what is meant is the Mushaf is that Allah the Exalted said: 'A revelation from the Lord of the worlds', and the scrolls were not revealed to us except as revelations, and he gives some preference.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (jadal) showing how an objection to an inference from a report (khabar) is made and then refuted. The original claim is that the verse 'لَا يَمَسُّهُ إِلَّا الْمُطَهَّرُونَ' is actually a prohibition (nahy) in the form of a report (khabar), meaning 'Do not touch it unless you are pure.' The objector (mu'tarid) insists on the apparent meaning: it is a straightforward report informing that only the purified (angels) touch it, referring to the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz). The objector supports this by citing another verse about angels handling purified scriptures. The proponent (mustadill) responds by arguing that the intended reference is to the Mushaf (physical copy of the Quran), not the Preserved Tablet, and uses another verse (al-Waqi'a 56:80) to show that the Quran is a 'revelation from the Lord of the worlds' and the Mushaf is the form in which it was revealed to us. Thus, the meaning is a prohibition, not mere information. The passage illustrates how a dialectician defends his interpretation by producing contextual evidence (qara'in) to outweigh the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
الخبر
النهي
الاعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) As a clear prohibition (nahy)
B) As a report about angels touching the Preserved Tablet
C) As a general statement about all pure persons
D) As an ambiguous (mujmal) statement

Key Takeaway
In jadal, contextual evidence (qarīnah) can override the literal wording, allowing a report to carry a prohibition meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a similar objection-response pair: Suppose someone argues that the verse 'أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ' (establish prayer) is a command (amr) in the form of a report (khabar). How would an objector challenge this using the apparent meaning? How would the proponent respond?

Objection to General Wording by Claiming Ambiguity
Author shows an objection that a general word is ambiguous (mujmal) and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze an objection based on ambiguity (ijmal) in a jadal exchange, and construct the appropriate response using takhsis.

Core Idea
General words survive ambiguity claims unless their legal meaning is undiscoverable without external clarification.

In jadal (dialectical debate), one key objection is to argue that a general word is actually ambiguous (mujmal), thus defeating its use as a proof. The Shafi'i uses this against the Maliki's argument from the Qur'anic command on fasting. Let's dissect the exchange and see how the Maliki responds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the general word
The Maliki uses the verse 'كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ' (fasting is prescribed for you) as a general proof that any act called 'sawm' fulfills the obligation, so one intention suffices for the whole month. The general word here is 'السدهم' (al-siyam).
2
Extract the objection (i'tirad)
The Shafi'i objects that 'sawm' is mujmal because it refers to legal fasting (sawm shar'i), which has conditions (time, niyyah) not clear from the word alone. Therefore, the word cannot be used as a general proof.
The objection uses the concept of ijmal (ambiguity) to block the inference from umum.
3
Analyze the analogy used in the objection
The Shafi'i analogizes to the verse 'وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ' (and give its due on the day of its harvest), where the word 'haqq' (due) is mujmal because its amount and kind are not specified. In both cases, the meaning is not apparent from the wording alone.
The objector claims that just as the harvest verse requires external clarification (bayan), so does 'sawm'.
4
Examine the response (jawab)
The Maliki (mustadill) denies the ambiguity: 'sawm' in Arabic means 'imsak' (abstention), which is clear. Legal conditions (like time and intention) are external specifications (takhsis) that do not make the word itself ambiguous. The analogy fails because in the harvest verse, the due amount is inherently unknown, whereas 'sawm' has a clear lexical meaning.
The response distinguishes between linguistic ambiguity (ijmal) and legal restriction (takhsis).
5
Evaluate the validity
The Maliki's response is strong: since the core meaning of 'sawm' is clear, the objection fails. The word remains general and can be used as a proof unless specific evidence restricts it. The opponent's claim of ijmal is rebutted by showing that legal details are not part of the word's definition.
This illustrates a key principle: a word is mujmal only if its essential meaning is unknowable without external clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - الاعتراض على إفادة اللفظ العموم بدعوى الإجمال: مثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على جواز صوم رمضان كله بنية واحدة في أوله بقوله تعالى : كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ ... ، وهذا اللفظ عام في كل ما يسمى صياما ، فمتى أتى المكلف بما يقع عليه الاسم فقد أتى بما كتب عليه وبرئت ذمته منه إلا ما خصه الدليل. فيعترض عليه الشافعي بقوله : إن الصيام مجمل لأن المراد به صوم شرعي وذلك لا يعلم من ظاهره، بل يفتقر في معرفته إلى بيان، فصار في الإجمال كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِه ... ، ولما لم يعلم المكلف المأمور جنس الحق من ظاهر اللفظ وافتقر في البيان إلى غيره كان ذلك اللفظ مجملا ولم يصح الاحتجاج به على قدر الحق ولا جنسه، وكذلك هاهنا . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض يكون ببيان المستدل أن هذا ليس من الإجمال في شيء، وإنما الصوم هو الإمساك في كلام العرب، إلا أن الشرط قد ورد بتخصيص بعض الأوقات واعتبار شرائط، فيجب أن يحمل على كل صوم إلا ما خصه الدليل، وليس من هذا الباب قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ، لأنه لا يتمكن من امتثال الأمر به إلا إذا عرف مقدار الحق، فبطل ما تعلقوا به (2) .
A - The objection to the wording's indication of generality by claiming ambiguity: its example is: that the Maliki argues for the permissibility of fasting the whole of Ramadan with one intention at its beginning by the word of the Exalted: 'Fasting is prescribed for you...', and this wording is general to everything that is called fasting. So whenever the obligated person does what the name applies to, he has done what was prescribed for him and his liability is cleared from it except what the evidence specifies. So the Shafi'i objects to him by saying: Fasting is ambiguous (mujmal) because what is meant by it is a legal fasting (sawm shar'i), and that is not known from its apparent meaning, but requires clarification to know it. So it becomes like the word of the Exalted: 'And give its due on the day of its harvest...', and since the obligated person does not know the kind of the due from the apparent wording and relies on clarification from someone else, that wording is ambiguous and it is not valid to argue with it about the amount of the due nor its kind. And so here. And the answer to this objection is by the arguer (mustadill) showing that this is not ambiguity at all, and that fasting is simply abstention (imsak) in the language of the Arabs, except that the condition has come to specify certain times and require conditions. So it must be applied to every fast except what the evidence specifies. And the verse 'And give its due on the day of its harvest' is not of this type, because it is not possible to comply with the command except by knowing the amount of the due, so what they clung to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents a specific type of objection (i'tirad) in jadal: challenging the generality (umum) of a word by claiming it is ambiguous (mujmal). The example involves the Maliki using the general command 'كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ' to argue that a single intention at the start of Ramadan suffices for the whole month, because the word 'sawm' is general and covers any act of fasting. The Shafi'i objector says the term is ambiguous: in the Qur'an, 'sawm' means legal fasting (sawm shar'i), which has detailed conditions (like time, intention) not clear from the word alone. The objector analogizes to the verse about giving the due (haqq) on harvest day, where the amount and kind of due are unknown without clarification. Therefore, the word 'sawm' is mujmal and cannot support the Maliki's claim. The Maliki (mustadill) responds by denying the ambiguity: 'sawm' in Arabic simply means 'abstention' (imsak), and the legal specifications (like time and conditions) are separate restrictions (takhsis) that do not make the word itself ambiguous. Unlike the harvest verse, where the due is inherently undefined, the act of fasting is clear as abstaining, and any restrictions are external. Thus, the Maliki's argument holds unless there is specific evidence to exclude certain acts of abstention. This illustrates a key distinction in usul al-fiqh between linguistic ambiguity (ijmal) and legal specification (takhsis).

Key Terms
العموم
الإجمال
المجمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Shafi'i objector claim that 'sawm' (fasting) in the verse is mujmal?
A) Because 'sawm' has multiple meanings in Arabic
B) Because the legal meaning of 'sawm' is not known from the apparent wording alone
C) Because the verse is abrogated
D) Because the word is general in a different way

Key Takeaway
A word is mujmal only if its very meaning is undiscoverable without external clarification; legal specifications (takhsis) do not render the word itself ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In the debate about whether the general command 'أقيموا الصلاة' (establish prayer) covers all forms of prayer, a Mu'tazili objects that 'salat' is mujmal because its legal meaning (including conditions like qibla, raka'at) is not obvious from the word. Construct the Shafi'i response using takhsis, similar to the fasting example.
?

Objection and Response on General vs. Ambiguous: The Fasting Example
Author presents a Maliki argument for general fasting command and Shafi'i objection of ambiguity, with response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term is genuinely ambiguous (mujmal) versus general ('amm) in a legal debate and construct a response to an objection of ambiguity.

Core Idea
A word remains general if its core meaning is known; specification by evidence does not make it ambiguous.

In this passage, a Maliki argues that the command to fast applies to any act of fasting unless evidence specifies otherwise. Al-Shafi'i objects that 'sawm' is ambiguous because the true religious fasting is unknown from the word alone, comparing it to a Quranic verse about an unspecified harvest due. The Maliki responds by distinguishing between the clear linguistic meaning of 'sawm' (abstention) and the additional Shar'i conditions, arguing that the word itself is not ambiguous—only its scope is narrowed by evidence. This exchange illustrates a key principle in usul al-fiqh for determining whether a term is general or ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The Maliki asserts that the command to fast is general; anyone who performs an act of fasting fulfills the obligation unless evidence specifies an exception. This is the position to be defended.
2
Locate the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'i argues that 'sawm' is mujmal (ambiguous) because it refers to a specific religious fasting, not known from the word alone. He analogizes to the verse about the harvest due where the amount is unspecified.
The objection aims to undermine the generality claim by shifting the category to ambiguity.
3
Analyze the response
The Maliki counters that 'sawm' linguistically means abstention; Shar'i conditions do not change the core meaning. The term remains general, only its application is restricted by evidence. He distinguishes the harvest verse because in that case the amount is unknown, whereas the act of fasting is known.
The response hinges on the distinction between linguistic core and religious specification.
4
Evaluate the debate
Assess whether the response successfully refutes the objection. The key is whether the analogy holds: in the harvest verse, the quantity of the due is not indicated by the word 'haqq', while 'sawm' clearly indicates abstention. Thus the analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صياما ، فمتى أتى المكلف بما يقع عليه الاسم فقد أتى بما كتب عليه وبرئت ذمته منه إلا ما خصه الدليل. فيعترض عليه الشافعي بقوله : إن الصيام مجمل لأن المراد به صوم شرعي وذلك لا يعلم من ظاهره، بل يفتقر في معرفته إلى بيان، فصار في الإجمال كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ ﴾، ولما لم يعلم المكلف المأمور جنس الحق من ظاهر اللفظ وافتقر في البيان إلى غيره كان ذلك اللفظ مجملا ولم يصح الاحتجاج به على قدر الحق ولا جنسه، وكذلك هاهنا . والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض يكون ببيان المستدل أن هذا ليس من الإجمال في شيء، وإنما الصوم هو الإمساك في كلام العرب، إلا أن الشرط قد ورد بتخصيص بعض الأوقات واعتبار شرائط، فيجب أن يحمل على كل صوم إلا ما خصه الدليل، وليس من هذا الباب قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ ﴾، لأنه لا يتمكن من امتثال الأمر به إلا إذا عرف مقدار الحق، فبطل ما تعلقوا به.
Fasting, so whenever the legally responsible person performs what is covered by the name, he has performed what was prescribed upon him and his liability is cleared, except what evidence specifies. So al-Shafi'i objects by saying: 'Fasting is ambiguous because what is meant is Shar'i fasting, which is not known from its apparent meaning, rather its knowledge requires clarification, so it becomes like His statement: "And give its due on the day of its harvest." Since the commanded person does not know the type of the due from the apparent wording and needs clarification from elsewhere, that wording is ambiguous and cannot be used as evidence for the amount or type of the due, and similarly here.' The answer to this objection is that the arguer explains that this is not ambiguity at all; rather, 'sawm' is abstention in the speech of the Arabs, but the Law has come specifying some times and considering conditions, so it must be applied to every fasting except what evidence specifies. And His statement: 'And give its due on the day of its harvest' is not of this category, because one cannot comply with the command unless one knows the amount of the due. So what they clung to is invalidated.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage illustrates a classic jadal exchange in usul al-fiqh. The Maliki argues that the command to fast is general ('amm) and applies to any act of fasting, with exceptions only where evidence specifies. Al-Shafi'i objects that the word 'sawm' is ambiguous (mujmal) because it refers to a specific religious fasting unknown from the word alone, analogous to the verse about the harvest due where the amount is unknown. The response distinguishes between linguistic meaning (abstention) and Shar'i conditions: the word itself remains unambiguous; only application is restricted by evidence. The harvest verse is rejected as analogous because in that case the quantity is unknown, whereas in fasting the core act is known. This shows how disputants deploy the categories of 'amm and mujmal to support or refute legal arguments.

Jadal in Usul: Objecting to a Term's Coverage (Tayammum with Pebbles)
Author gives example of objection that pebbles are not covered by 'sa'id' and the response using linguistics and maqasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange between the Maliki and Shafi'i over the term 'sa'id' and evaluate the Maliki's response using linguistic and maqasid arguments.

Core Idea
Jadal combines linguistic precision and teleological reasoning to resolve semantic disputes.

This passage illustrates a classic jadal move: the objector concedes the generality of a term but denies that the specific case falls under it. The Maliki's response shows how to deploy linguistic consensus and maqasid al-shari'ah to defend the term's coverage. Understanding this exchange equips you to handle similar semantic objections in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputants and their positions
The Maliki claims pebbles are included in 'sa'id' and thus tayammum with pebbles is valid. The Shafi'i objects, restricting 'sa'id' to dust only.
2
Extract the Maliki's initial argument
The Maliki cites the Quranic verse (5:6) and argues that 'sa'id' covers pebbles by its general meaning.
3
State the Shafi'i's objection
The Shafi'i does not concede that pebbles are part of 'sa'id'; he claims 'sa'id' specifically means dust.
4
Analyze the Maliki's response
The Maliki provides linguistic evidence (consensus of linguists as reported by al-Zajjaj, and derivation from 'tasa'ud') and teleological evidence (the law intends ease and removing hardship; restricting to dust would cause hardship).
This two-pronged response strengthens the argument.
5
Assess the strength of the response
The linguistic proof establishes the literal inclusion; the maqasid proof aligns with the broader objectives of shari'ah, making it difficult for the objector to refute without denying either consensus or the clear purpose of the law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - التسليم بدعوى العموم مع الاعتراض على تناول اللفظ موضع الخلاف : مثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على جواز التيمم بالحصى بقوله تعالى : فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيدًا طَيِّبًا ... ، والحصى من الصعيد، فوجب حمل ذلك على عمومه . فيعترض عليه الشافعي بعدم التسليم بأن الحصى من الصعيد، وإنما الصعيد اسم للتراب خاصة . وطريق المالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن هذا الاعتراض أن يبين أن الاسم يصدق على الحصى، حتى يصح له المراد وذلك أن يقول : إن هذا ما ذكره أهل اللغة؛ من ذلك قول أبي إسحاق الزجاج : لا خلاف بين أهل اللغة في أن اسم الصعيد ينطلق على جميع أجزاء الأرض من الحصى والتراب وغير ذلك»، كما يدل عليه أيضًا أن الصعيد مأخوذ من : تصاعد الشيء، إذا علا ، فما علا على وجه الأرض فهو صعيد، فإذا ثبت ذلك دخل الحصى وغيره تحت اللفظ ، ثم إن عموم الله عز وجل إنما أراد التيسير والتوسعة على عباده والتفضل عليهم، فعلق التيمم بجميع أنواع الأرض، ليكون موجودًا على كل حال، ولو علقه بالتراب وحده لكان ذلك تضييقا عليهم، وهذا ضد الظاهر من حال الرخصة والتوسعة.
B - Acknowledging the claim of generality while objecting to the term's covering of the disputed case: Example: The Maliki argues for the permissibility of tayammum with pebbles by His statement: 'And perform tayammum with clean earth...', and pebbles are part of earth, so it must be applied in its generality. So al-Shafi'i objects by not conceding that pebbles are part of earth; rather, 'sa'id' is a name specifically for dust. The Maliki's method in answering this objection is to show that the term applies to pebbles, so that his intended meaning is valid. He says: This is what the linguists have mentioned; from that is the saying of Abu Ishaq al-Zajjaj: 'There is no disagreement among linguists that the name "sa'id" applies to all parts of the earth, including pebbles, dust, and other things.' Also evidence that 'sa'id' is derived from 'tasa'ud' (to rise), so whatever rises on the face of the earth is 'sa'id'. If that is established, pebbles and others fall under the term. Furthermore, the generality of Allah intends ease and spaciousness for His servants and favor upon them, so He attached tayammum to all types of earth so that it would be available in every situation. If He had attached it only to dust, that would be a restriction upon them, and this is contrary to the apparent situation of permission and ease.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage demonstrates a common type of jadal: the disputant acknowledges that the wording is general but denies that the specific case (here, pebbles) falls under that general term. The Shafi'i objects that 'sa'id' means only dust, so pebbles are excluded. The Maliki response uses two arguments: first, linguistic evidence — quoting Abu Ishaq al-Zajjaj that linguists agree 'sa'id' applies to all earth parts, and the derivation from 'rise' indicates anything on the earth's surface; second, teleological reasoning — the law intends ease, and restricting tayammum to dust would cause hardship, contradicting the clear purpose of the legislation. This shows how usul al-fiqh arguments combine language and maqasid (objectives) to determine the scope of legal terms.

تضييق
