Dialectical Use of Mujmal: The Takbir Example
Author defines mujmal and gives Maliki argument that takbir is obligatory because Quranic command is mujmal explained by Prophet's action, with objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical argument that uses the claim of ambiguity (ijmal) to make a Prophetic action binding, and identify the first objection to such an argument.

Core Idea
Claiming a text is ambiguous lets you use the Prophet's action as an obligatory explanation.

In this passage, we see a classic dialectical move in usul al-fiqh: the Maliki scholar argues that saying 'Allahu Akbar' in prayer is obligatory by claiming that the Quranic command 'Establish prayer' is mujmal (ambiguous). The Prophet's action of starting with takbir is then presented as an obligatory explanation. The objector, however, denies the ambiguity, arguing that 'salat' in Arabic means supplication and is not made ambiguous by added conditions. Let's walk through this exchange step by step.

Guided Path
1
Define mujmal
Identify the definition provided: a word that indicates two equally possible meanings with no preference. This is the key premise of the Maliki argument.
The definition is from usul al-fiqh.
2
Reconstruct the Maliki argument
Outline the argument: The verse 'Establish prayer' is mujmal; the Prophet explained it by his action (starting with 'Allahu Akbar'); the explanation of a mujmal is obligatory; therefore saying 'Allahu Akbar' is obligatory.
This is a composite argument from Quran and Sunnah.
3
Identify the first objection
The objector denies the premise: the verse is not mujmal but 'amm (general). The linguistic meaning of 'salat' is supplication; adding conditions does not create ambiguity, as in the case of purity for touching the mushaf.
Analogous example from the same field.
4
Assess the dialectical impact
The objector has shifted the burden back to the Maliki: if the verse is not mujmal, then the Prophet's action is not an obligatory explanation of an ambiguous command, so the argument fails.
The debate is ongoing; the Maliki must respond to maintain his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع: الاعتراض على الاستدلال بالمجمل يعرف الأصوليون اللفظ المجمل بأنه : ما لَهُ دلالة على أحد أمرين لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر بالنسبة إليه» فالمستدل قد يستدل بالآية ويدعي فيها الإجمال لحاجته إلى ذلك، وهو أن يرى المستدل أن أفعال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام لا تدل بنفسها على الوجوب، لكن إذا أخرجها النبي الله مخرج البيان كانت دالة على الوجوب . مثال ذلك : أن يستدل من يقول بهذه القاعدة من علماء المالكية على أن الإحرام بقوله الله الأكبر، لا يجوز ولا يجزئ من ذلك إلا الله أكبر»، فيستدل بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ ) ، ويدعي في ذلك الإجمال وأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فسر هذا المجمل بفعله والذي كان يفتتح به الله أكبر، وبيان المجمل الواجب هو على الوجوب، فوجب أن يكون هذا اللفظ هو الواجب فلا يجزي غيره. وهذا النوع من الاستدلال إنما يكون بمجموع الآية والسنة . والاعتراض عليه إنما يكون من وجهين : الأول : أن يمنع المعترض كون الآية مجملة، فيقول : إنها ليست بمجملة، بل عامة، لأن الصلاة في كلام العرب هي الدعاء وإنما أضيف إليه شروط، وذلك لا يخرجه عن موضوعه كما لا يخرج اشتراط الطهارة لمس المصحف
Section Four: Objection to using the ambiguous [text] as evidence. The usuliyyun define the ambiguous wording (al-lafz al-mujmal) as: that which has indication to one of two matters without either having superiority over the other with respect to it. So the arguer may use a verse and claim it is ambiguous due to his need for that. And that is when the arguer sees that the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) do not in themselves indicate obligation, but if the Prophet produced them as an explanation, they indicate obligation. Example: Those who hold this principle from the Maliki scholars argue that the ihram [by saying] 'Allahu Akbar' is not permissible and nothing suffices except 'Allahu Akbar'. They use the verse: 'Establish prayer' and claim that it is ambiguous and that the Prophet explained this ambiguous [command] by his action, which was to open with 'Allahu Akbar'. The explanation of the ambiguous is obligatory, so this wording becomes obligatory and nothing else suffices. This type of argumentation is by combining the verse and the Sunnah. The objection to it is from two aspects: First: The objector denies that the verse is ambiguous, saying: It is not ambiguous but rather general, because 'salat' in the speech of the Arabs means supplication (du'a) and conditions have been added to it, but that does not remove it from its subject, just as requiring purity for touching the mushaf does not remove it.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage introduces a distinct argumentative strategy: using the claim of ambiguity (ijmal) to make a Prophetic action binding. The usul al-fiqh definition of mujmal is given: a word pointing to two equally possible meanings. The Maliki example: the verse 'Establish prayer' is claimed to be mujmal because 'salat' could mean supplication or the ritual prayer; the Prophet's action of starting with 'Allahu Akbar' is presented as an obligatory explanation. The first objection denies the ambiguity itself: 'salat' is not mujmal but general, as its linguistic meaning (supplication) is known, and added conditions do not change that — analogous to requiring purity for touching the mushaf, which does not make the mushaf ambiguous. This illustrates how the dispute over a word's ambiguity hinges on whether the Shar'i addition alters the core linguistic meaning.

Key Terms
مجمل (mujmal): Wording indicating two equally possible meanings with no preference; used here to argue that the Quranic command needs prophetic explanation. [Note: Standard definition across usul works.]
بيان (bayan): Explanation; here the Prophet's action is considered an obligatory explanation of the mujmal. [Note: Stable term in usul and mantiq.]
إجمال (ijmal): Ambiguity; the property of being mujmal. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The first objection to a mujmal-based argument is to deny that the text is actually ambiguous, showing that the linguistic meaning remains.

Defending Ambiguity: Analogy with the Harvest Verse
Responder's strategy for countering ambiguity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the analogical argument used to defend a claim of textual ambiguity in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Ambiguity is proven by showing a term's legal meaning differs from its linguistic meaning.

In this lesson, we examine how a dialectician affirms that a command verse is ambiguous (mujmal) by comparing it to another verse whose ambiguity is agreed upon. This analogy strengthens the claim that the verse needs explanation (bayan) from the Prophetic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being defended
The verse about prayer (salah) is ambiguous. Find where the responder states this.
2
Locate the evidence
The term 'salah' in language means supplication, but in law it refers to specific actions – the intended meaning is unclear.
3
Find the analogical support
The Quranic verse 6:141 'Give its due on the day of harvest' is also ambiguous; the community agreed on its ambiguity.
4
Assess the force of the analogy
If ambiguity is accepted for the harvest verse, consistency demands it for the prayer verse.
5
Note the dialectical move
The responder does not prove ambiguity from scratch but reinforces it by pointing to a parallel case that is uncontested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطريق في الجواب على هذا الوجه من الاعتراض؛ أن يقرر المستدل ما ادعاه من الإجمال وينصره، بأن يقول: إن الصلاة في كلام العرب هي الدعاء، والمراد به في الشرع غير ما وضع له من أفعال وركوع وسجود، فلا يعلم المراد بها من اللفظ، وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره وهذا بمنزلة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ﴾، لما لم يفهم المراد من لفظه وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره، اتفق على إجماله، فكذلك في مسألتنا مثله. والوجه الثاني من الاعتراض: أن يمنع كون الخبر بيانا للآية، بأن يقول: لا أسلم أن ما ورد عن النبي بيان للآية، بل يجوز أن يكون بيانا، ويجوز أن يكون فعلا مبتدأ على وجه الاستحباب، وإذا احتمل الأمرين بطلت دعوى البيان، ولو كان بيانا للأمر لوجب جميع أفعاله لأنه خرج على مخرج واحد، ولما لم يجب الجميع دل أنه ليس على وجه البيان. والمسلك بالنسبة للمستدل في الجواب عن هذا الوجه من الاعتراض أن يقول: إذا ثبت بما ذكرنا أن الآية مجملة وأنها مفتقرة إلى البيان وفعل ما فعله باسم أن يكون بيانا للصلاة المجملة المأمور بها، ولا يلزم أن يكون الصلاة، وجب جميع ما فعله واجبًا لأننا لو تركنا ذلك الظاهر لحملنا الكل على الوجوب، ولكنه دل الدليل على العدول عن الظاهر في بعض الأفعال، وبقي الباقي يحمل على ظاهره.
The method of answering this type of objection is that the arguer affirms and supports his claim of ambiguity, saying: 'Prayer (al-salah) in the Arabic language is supplication (al-du'a), and what is meant by it in the Sacred Law is different from its linguistic meaning of actions, bowing, and prostration. Thus its intended meaning is not known from the wording, and it depends on something else for its explanation. This is like the verse: “And give its due on the day of its harvest.” Since the intended meaning was not understood from its wording and it depended on something else for its explanation, they agreed on its ambiguity. Similarly in our case.' The second type of objection is to deny that the report (khabar) is an explanation (bayan) of the verse, saying: 'I do not accept that what came from the Prophet is an explanation of the verse. Rather, it could be an explanation, or it could be an independent act of recommendation (istihbab). If it admits both possibilities, the claim of explanation is invalidated. If it were an explanation of the command, all of his acts would be obligatory because they issued from a single source. Since they are not all obligatory, this indicates that it is not on the basis of explanation.' The course for the arguer in answering this type of objection is to say: 'If it is established by what we mentioned that the verse is ambiguous and requires explanation, and that the Prophet's action was to be an explanation of the ambiguous commanded prayer, it does not follow that all of his acts are obligatory. If we left that apparent meaning, we would have interpreted everything as obligatory, but evidence indicates deviation from the apparent meaning in some acts, while the remainder continues to carry its apparent meaning.'
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical strategy for responding to two specific objections regarding textual ambiguity. The first objection claims that a command verse is ambiguous (mujmal) and therefore cannot specify particular obligations without further evidence. The responder counters by arguing that the term 'salah' is indeed ambiguous because its Islamic legal meaning differs from its linguistic meaning, similar to the verse about 'giving its due on the day of harvest' which scholars agree is ambiguous. The second objection denies that the Prophet's reported action serves as an explanation (bayan) of the command, suggesting it might be merely recommended (mustahabb). The responder refutes this by arguing that once ambiguity is established, the Prophet's action necessarily functions as an explanation; otherwise, we would have to interpret all his actions as obligatory, which is not the case. The passage demonstrates the classical method of dealing with claims of ambiguity in legal dialectic.

Key Terms
الإجمال (al-ijmal): Ambiguity in a text where the intended meaning is not clear from the wording alone, requiring external explanation (bayan). [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ijmal is a standard concept; here it is used in a jadal context as a basis for objection.]
البيان (al-bayan): Explanation that removes ambiguity, often through the Prophet's statements or actions. [Note: The term is consistent across usul and jadal texts.]
الاستحباب (al-istihbab): Recommendation, a level of legal obligation less than wajib; used here to challenge the status of the Prophet's action as bayan. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for claiming that the verse about prayer is ambiguous?
A) The word 'salah' has multiple linguistic meanings.
B) The legal meaning of 'salah' differs from its linguistic meaning.
C) The Prophet's actions are unclear.
D) The verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
To defend a claim of ambiguity, the dialectician can use analogy with a text whose ambiguity is uncontested.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, the objector says that the term 'zakat' in the Quran is not ambiguous because its meaning is clear from the context. Construct a response using the method of analogy with another ambiguous term (e.g., 'salah' or 'hajj').

Responding to the Denial of Bayan: Default Obligation
Responder's strategy for countering the objection that the Prophet's act is not bayan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of 'haml 'ala al-wujub' (interpreting as obligation) when defending an act as explanatory.

Core Idea
Once ambiguity is established, the Prophet's action must be an explanation; otherwise, all actions would be obligatory.

Here we tackle the second objection: the objector denies that the Prophet's reported actions are explanatory (bayan) of the ambiguous command, suggesting they might be merely recommended (mustahabb). The responder counters using the principle that if an act is not needed as explanation, then the default is that all similar acts are obligatory unless evidence indicates otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector denies that the Prophet's action is a bayan; it could be an independent recommended act.
2
Understand the objection's logic
If the act could be either explanation or recommendation, then the claim of explanation is uncertain and invalid.
3
Notice the responder's move
He argues that once ambiguity is proven, the Prophet's action must be an explanation, because to leave it as ambiguous would mean all his actions are obligatory (since we would take the apparent general command).
4
Examine the rebuttal
The responder says evidence shows deviation from the apparent in some acts, but the rest remain on the apparent (obligation).
5
Evaluate the strength
This argument establishes that the default is obligation unless a specific rebutting evidence exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطريق في الجواب على هذا الوجه من الاعتراض؛ أن يقرر المستدل ما ادعاه من الإجمال وينصره، بأن يقول: إن الصلاة في كلام العرب هي الدعاء، والمراد به في الشرع غير ما وضع له من أفعال وركوع وسجود، فلا يعلم المراد بها من اللفظ، وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره وهذا بمنزلة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَءَاتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ﴾، لما لم يفهم المراد من لفظه وافتقر في بيانه إلى غيره، اتفق على إجماله، فكذلك في مسألتنا مثله. والوجه الثاني من الاعتراض: أن يمنع كون الخبر بيانا للآية، بأن يقول: لا أسلم أن ما ورد عن النبي بيان للآية، بل يجوز أن يكون بيانا، ويجوز أن يكون فعلا مبتدأ على وجه الاستحباب، وإذا احتمل الأمرين بطلت دعوى البيان، ولو كان بيانا للأمر لوجب جميع أفعاله لأنه خرج على مخرج واحد، ولما لم يجب الجميع دل أنه ليس على وجه البيان. والمسلك بالنسبة للمستدل في الجواب عن هذا الوجه من الاعتراض أن يقول: إذا ثبت بما ذكرنا أن الآية مجملة وأنها مفتقرة إلى البيان وفعل ما فعله باسم أن يكون بيانا للصلاة المجملة المأمور بها، ولا يلزم أن يكون الصلاة، وجب جميع ما فعله واجبًا لأننا لو تركنا ذلك الظاهر لحملنا الكل على الوجوب، ولكنه دل الدليل على العدول عن الظاهر في بعض الأفعال، وبقي الباقي يحمل على ظاهره.
The method of answering this type of objection is that the arguer affirms and supports his claim of ambiguity, saying: 'Prayer (al-salah) in the Arabic language is supplication (al-du'a), and what is meant by it in the Sacred Law is different from its linguistic meaning of actions, bowing, and prostration. Thus its intended meaning is not known from the wording, and it depends on something else for its explanation. This is like the verse: “And give its due on the day of its harvest.” Since the intended meaning was not understood from its wording and it depended on something else for its explanation, they agreed on its ambiguity. Similarly in our case.' The second type of objection is to deny that the report (khabar) is an explanation (bayan) of the verse, saying: 'I do not accept that what came from the Prophet is an explanation of the verse. Rather, it could be an explanation, or it could be an independent act of recommendation (istihbab). If it admits both possibilities, the claim of explanation is invalidated. If it were an explanation of the command, all of his acts would be obligatory because they issued from a single source. Since they are not all obligatory, this indicates that it is not on the basis of explanation.' The course for the arguer in answering this type of objection is to say: 'If it is established by what we mentioned that the verse is ambiguous and requires explanation, and that the Prophet's action was to be an explanation of the ambiguous commanded prayer, it does not follow that all of his acts are obligatory. If we left that apparent meaning, we would have interpreted everything as obligatory, but evidence indicates deviation from the apparent meaning in some acts, while the remainder continues to carry its apparent meaning.'
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage explains the dialectical strategy for responding to two specific objections regarding textual ambiguity. The first objection claims that a command verse is ambiguous (mujmal) and therefore cannot specify particular obligations without further evidence. The responder counters by arguing that the term 'salah' is indeed ambiguous because its Islamic legal meaning differs from its linguistic meaning, similar to the verse about 'giving its due on the day of harvest' which scholars agree is ambiguous. The second objection denies that the Prophet's reported action serves as an explanation (bayan) of the command, suggesting it might be merely recommended (mustahabb). The responder refutes this by arguing that once ambiguity is established, the Prophet's action necessarily functions as an explanation; otherwise, we would have to interpret all his actions as obligatory, which is not the case. The passage demonstrates the classical method of dealing with claims of ambiguity in legal dialectic.

Key Terms
الإجمال (al-ijmal): Ambiguity in a text where the intended meaning is not clear from the wording alone, requiring external explanation (bayan). [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ijmal is a standard concept; here it is used in a jadal context as a basis for objection.]
البيان (al-bayan): Explanation that removes ambiguity, often through the Prophet's statements or actions. [Note: The term is consistent across usul and jadal texts.]
الاستحباب (al-istihbab): Recommendation, a level of legal obligation less than wajib; used here to challenge the status of the Prophet's action as bayan. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if the Prophet's action is not accepted as an explanation (bayan) of the ambiguous verse?
A) The verse remains ambiguous.
B) The Prophet's action becomes recommended.
C) All similar actions would have to be considered obligatory.
D) The argument is abandoned.

Key Takeaway
In defending an act as explanatory, the dialectician uses the default rule that ambiguity implies obligation unless specific evidence lightens the ruling.

Objection by Claiming Participation in Evidence
Defines the participation objection and its first subtype with a legal example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of an objection by participation in evidence and apply its structure to a juristic dispute.

Core Idea
Participation lets the objector turn the opponent's evidence against him without denying its validity.

In legal dialectic, one common strategy is to accept the opponent's evidence but reinterpret it to support your own position. This objection, called al-musharaka (participation), requires acknowledging the evidence's apparent meaning and then showing that the same text favors your view. The author defines this objection, sets its conditions, and illustrates its first subtype with a classic dispute over the interpretation of a Qur'anic verse.

Guided Path
1
Define the objection
Start by grasping the core meaning: the objector takes the very evidence used by the original arguer and claims it supports his own contention. This is not a denial of the evidence but a reinterpretation.
2
Identify the conditions
The objector must accept the 'wajh al-dalala' (aspect of indication) of the evidence. He cannot later claim ambiguity (ijmal) because participation requires mutual understanding of the text's apparent meaning.
Ambiguity would imply the text cannot be relied upon for any clear ruling.
3
Recognize the first type
The first type occurs when both disputants rely on the apparent meaning (zahir) of the same text, with no extra merit for either side. Within this, the first subtype (A) involves a word that is mushtarak (shared between two meanings), and each party interprets it according to his own doctrine.
The author contrasts this with other subtypes not fully covered here.
4
Analyze the legal example
Work through the Maliki vs. Hanafi/Shafi'i dispute over Qur'an 2:237. The Malikis argue that 'the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage' is the guardian (wali), so he can forgive the dower. The Hanafis/Shafi'is respond by claiming the same verse supports the husband. Both sides accept the verse's apparent meaning; they differ on who the phrase refers to.
The passage ends abruptly, but the structure of participation is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث الاعتراض بدعوى المشاركة في الدليل ومعناه: أن يجعل المعترض ما استدل به المستدل دليلا له في المسألة التي اعترض عليها. ولا يصح يقع الاعتراض بدعوى المشاركة إلا ممن يسلم بوجه دلالة المستدل منها، كما لا يحسن أن يتعقب دعوى المشاركة دعوى الإجمال، فإن المشاركة إنما تكون بعد ثبوت الدلالة التي أقر بمشاركته فيها، وبعد الإقرار بالدلالة لا تجوز دعوى الإجمال، لأن المجمل ما لم يعقل معناه من نطقه، والدلالة لا تكون إلا بمعقول. وهو نوعان: المطلب الأول: أن يستدل كل واحد منهما بدليل من جهة الظاهر لا مزية لأحدهم على الآخر فيه وهو على ضربين: أ - أن يكون لفظا واحدا مشتركا بين معنيين فيحمله كل واحد منهما على المعنى الذي يذهب إليه ومثاله: أن يستدل أصحاب مالك على أن الولي يجوز أن يعفو عن الصداق قبل الدخول وبعد الطلاق بقوله تعالى: ... إِلَّا أَن يَعْفُونَ أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ عُقْدَةُ النِّكَاحِ ... والذي بيده عقدة النكاح الولي، وأما الزوج والزوجة فلكل واحد منهما اسم يختص به، وهذا ليس فيه مدخل إلا أن بيده عقدة النكاح، فإذا ثبت أن الولي هو الذي بيده عقدة النكاح، جاز عفوه عن الصداق بحق الظاهر. وهنا يعترض الشافعي أو الحنفي بأن يقول: هذه الآية حجة لنا، لأن الذي بيده عقدة النكاح هو الزوج، لأنه أملك للعقد وأحق به من الولي، فكانت نسبته
Third Section: The Objection by Claiming Participation in the Evidence. Its meaning is that the objector makes the evidence used by the arguer a proof for his own position in the issue under objection. This objection by claiming participation is only valid when the objector accepts the aspect of evidence (wajh dalala) from which the arguer proceeds. Also, it is not proper for a claim of participation to be followed by a claim of ambiguity, because participation only occurs after the evidence is established, which the objector agrees to share. After admitting the evidence, it is not permissible to claim ambiguity, because ambiguity is what cannot be understood from its wording, and evidence exists only through understanding. It is of two types: The First Demand: that each of the two [disputants] argues by evidence from the apparent meaning (zahir), with no advantage for one over the other in it. This is of two kinds: A) That it is a single word shared (mushtarak) between two meanings, and each one carries it to the meaning he holds. An example: the Malikis argue that the guardian (wali) may forgive the dower (sadaq) before consummation and after divorce by God's saying: “Except if they forgive, or he forgives in whose hand is the knot of marriage.” The one in whose hand is the knot of marriage is the guardian. As for the husband and wife, each has a specific name. This text has no entry except that the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage [is the guardian]. So if it is established that the guardian is the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage, his forgiveness of the dower is permitted by the apparent meaning. Here, the Shafi'i or Hanafi objects by saying: 'This verse is our proof, because the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage is the husband, because he is more entitled to the contract and has more right to it than the guardian.' So his relative ...

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of objection in legal dialectic: 'objection by claiming participation in the evidence' (al-i'tirad bi da'wa al-musharaka fi al-dalil). The objector takes the very evidence used by the original arguer and interprets it to support his own view. The author emphasizes that this objection requires the objector to first accept the underlying indication (dalala) of the evidence; it cannot be combined with a claim of ambiguity, because ambiguity undermines the shared understanding of the evidence. Then the passage divides participation into two types. The first type occurs when both parties rely on the apparent meaning (zahir) of a text, with no extra merit for either. Within this, the first subtype (A) involves a single word that is shared (mushtarak) between two meanings, each side interpreting it according to its own position. The example concerns the Qur'anic verse about forgiveness of the dower: the Malikis interpret 'the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage' as the guardian (wali), while the Shafi'is and Hanafis interpret it as the husband. The passage illustrates how the same textual evidence is used by both sides. The text breaks off at the end of the page, but the example clearly shows the mechanics of participation.

Participation in evidence is a powerful objection because it concedes the proof's validity while reinterpreting it to another conclusion.

Objection by Claiming Participation in Evidence: Definition and Conditions
Defines the third type of objection in jadal and its validity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid objection by claiming participation in evidence in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
In jadal, an objection by participation concedes the evidence's implication to attack from within.

In this lesson, we examine the third type of objection in dialectical debate (jadal): the objection by claiming participation in the evidence. This strategy involves the objector turning the arguer's own evidence against him, but strict conditions apply for its validity. The passage clarifies the prerequisite of acknowledging the evidence's implication and the prohibition of later claiming ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of the objection
The objector claims that the same evidence used by the arguer actually supports his own case, not the arguer's. This is not simply denying the evidence but co-opting it.
The Arabic term is 'al-iʿtiraḍ bi-daʿwa al-mushāraka'.
2
Identify the condition for validity
The objector must first acknowledge (yaslamu bi) the implication (dalāla) of the evidence for the arguer's claim. Without this concession, the objection is not a participation objection but a denial of the evidence's relevance.
This condition ensures the objector is not contradicting himself.
3
Recognize the prohibition of subsequent ambiguity claim
After acknowledging the implication, the objector cannot later claim the evidence is ambiguous (mujmal). Implies understanding precedes participation.
Ambiguity would negate the established implication.
4
Prepare for the two types
The passage ends by stating there are two types of this objection, which will be explained next. This lesson focuses on the definition and conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: الاعتراض بدعوى المشاركة في الدليل. ومعناه: أن يجعل المعترض ما استدل به المستدل دليلا له على المسألة التي اعترض عليها. ولا يصح أن يقع الاعتراض بدعوى المشاركة إلا ممن يسلم بوجه دلالة المستدل منها، كما لا يحسن أن يتعقب دعوى المشاركة دعوى الإجمال، فإن المشاركة إنما تكون بعد ثبوت الدلالة التي أقر بمشاركته فيها، وبعد الإقرار بالدلالة لا تجوز دعوى الإجمال، لأن المجمل ما لم يعقل معناه من نطقه، والدلالة لا تكون إلا بمعقول. وهو نوعان:
The third topic: The objection by claiming participation in the evidence. Its meaning: that the objector makes what the arguer used as evidence an evidence for himself on the issue he objected to. And it is not valid for the objection by claiming participation to occur except from someone who acknowledges the aspect of implication of the arguer from it; just as it is not proper that the claim of participation be followed by a claim of ambiguity, for participation only occurs after the establishment of the implication which he acknowledged his participation in, and after acknowledging the implication, the claim of ambiguity is not permissible, because the ambiguous is that whose meaning is not understood from its utterance, and implication is only by what is understood. And it is of two types:
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of objection in jadal: objection by claiming participation in the evidence. The objector takes the same evidence used by the original arguer and claims it supports his own position instead. The passage then states a condition: this objection is only valid if the objector first acknowledges that the evidence implies the original claim; otherwise it would be a denial of implication. Then it distinguishes this objection from a subsequent claim of ambiguity (ijmal): once the implication is acknowledged, one cannot later claim the evidence is ambiguous. The passage ends by stating there are two types of this objection, which will be explained next.

Responding to Shared-Word Objections in Jadal
Explains and illustrates the first subcategory of type one with the marriage verse and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a mushtarak objection is being made and construct at least one valid response using the strategies of linguistic fit, discourse context, or structural completeness.

Core Idea
A shared word is a double-edged sword; the arguer must show why his interpretation is uniquely correct.

This lesson dives into the first subcategory of objection by participation: when both sides use the same apparent evidence, specifically a word with multiple meanings (mushtarak). Using the famous Maliki-Shafi'i dispute over Q. 2:237, you will learn how to recognize this objection and how the original arguer can defend his position with multiple strategies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared evidence
Locate the single apparent evidence both parties rely on. In the example, it is the Qur'anic phrase 'the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage' (Q. 2:237). Both Malikis and Shafi'is/Hanafis use this same phrase to support opposite conclusions.
The evidence must be 'apparent' with no inherent advantage for either side.
2
Determine each party's interpretation
Malikis interpret the phrase as referring to the guardian (wali); Shafi'is/Hanafis interpret it as the husband. Each claims the phrase supports their position on who can forgive the dowry.
The objector does not deny the text; he offers a different meaning.
3
Analyze the objector's claim of no advantage
The objector asserts that both sides are equal in the apparent reading. The Maliki arguer must show that his interpretation is stronger. The passage provides three ways to do this.
The objector's participation claim can be refuted by showing his interpretation is weaker.
4
Categorize the three response strategies
The three responses are: (1) linguistic appropriateness – the guardian is more fittingly called 'the one with the knot' because he has only the contract; (2) discourse shift – God addresses husbands directly then uses third person, indicating a different referent; (3) comprehensive enumeration – the verse covers three categories (wives, one with knot, husbands), proving the one with knot is distinct from the husband.
These strategies show how to defend against participation objections by demonstrating interpretive superiority.

المطلب الأول : أن يستدل كل واحد منهما بدليل من جهة الظاهر لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر فيه وهو على ضربين : أ - أن يكون لفظا واحدًا مشتركا بين معنيين فيحمله كل واحد منهما على المعنى الذي يذهب إليه ومثاله : أن يستدل أصحاب مالك على أن الولي يجوز أن يعفو عن الصداق قبل الدخول وبعد الطلاق بقوله تعالى : ... إِلَّا أَن يَعْفُونَ أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ، عُقْدَةُ النِّكَاح ... والذي بيده عقدة النكاح الولي ، وأما الزوج والزوجة فلكل واحد منهما اسم يختص به، وهذا ليس فيه مدخل إلا أن بيده عقدة النكاح، فإذا ثبت أن الولي هو الذي بيده عقدة النكاح، جاز عفوه عن الصداق بحق الظاهر وهنا يعترض الشافعي أو الحنفي بأن يقول : هذه الآية حجة لنا، لأن الذي بيده عقدة النكاح هو الزوج، لأنه أملك للعقد وأحق به من الولي، فكانت نسبته إليه أولى فيكون المعنى إلا أن يعفو الزوجات أو يعفو الذي بيده عقدة النكاح وهو الزوج . ويمكن للمستدل هنا أن يجيب بأكثر من جواب . فيمكنه أن يجيب بأن يبين أن الذي بيده عقدة النكاح هو الولي، وأن هذا الاسم به أليق، وذلك أن الزوج له حظ من جهة العقد بالزوجية، والولي ليس له حظ في النكاح إلا العقد، فكانت نسبته إليه أولى ولهذا يقال له ولي، وإن كان الزوج أيضًا وليا لنفسه لما انفرد بالولاية وكانت حظه من... كما يمكنه أن يجيب بأن الله تعالى قال: ﴿وَإِن طَلَّقْتُمُوهُنَّ مِن قَبْلِ أَن تَمَسُّوهُنَّ ... ﴾ ، فواجه الأزواج بالخطاب ثم قال : . . . أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ عُقدَةُ النِّكَاح ... ، فخاطب غائبا، ولو أراد الأزواج لذكرهم بخطاب المواجهة . كما أن له أن يجيب بطريق ثالث ، وهو أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى ذكر العفو من جميع الجهات واستوعب جميع مواضعه، فلما كانت جهة الزوج يصح فيها العفو من الاثنين قال . .. إِلَّا إلا أَن يَعْفُونَ ... يريد الزوجات، و ... أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ، عُقدَةُ النِّكَاح .... يريد الولي، ثم قال ... وَأَن تَعْفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى ... يريد الأزواج، ولو كان الذي بيده عقدة النكاح هو الزوج لكان قد أخل ببعض الأقسام وهو خطاب الولي وكرر خطاب الزوج، وحمل الخطاب في قوله : ... أَوْ يَعْفُواْ الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ، عُقدَةُ النِّكَاح ... على فائدة غير ما حمل عليه ... وَأَن تَعْفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى ... أولى حمله على مجرد التكرار والتأكيد.
The first demand: that each one of them uses as evidence a proof from the apparent aspect, with no advantage for one over the other in it. And it is of two types: A) That it be a single word shared between two meanings, so each one of them interprets it according to the meaning he adopts. Its example: The followers of Malik argue that the guardian may forgive the dowry before consummation and after divorce by His statement the Exalted: '...except that they forgive or the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage forgives...' And the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage is the guardian. As for the husband and wife, each of them has a name specific to him, and there is no entry here except that he has the knot of marriage. So if it is established that the guardian is the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage, his forgiveness of the dowry is permissible by the apparent right. Here the Shafi'i or Hanafi objects by saying: This verse is a proof for us, because the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage is the husband, because he is more entitled to the contract and more worthy of it than the guardian, so its attribution to him is more appropriate. Thus the meaning is: except that the wives forgive, or the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage forgives, and he is the husband. And the arguer here can respond with more than one answer. He can respond by clarifying that the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage is the guardian, and that this name is more fitting for him, because the husband has a share from the aspect of the contract by marriage, and the guardian has no share in the marriage except the contract, so its attribution to him is more appropriate, and for this he is called wali, even though the husband is also a wali for himself, because he is unique in guardianship and his share is... Also he can respond that God the Exalted said: 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them...' so He addressed the husbands with direct speech, then He said: '...or the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage forgives...' so He addressed a third person, and if He intended the husbands, He would have mentioned them with direct address. Also he can respond in a third way: that the Blessed and Exalted mentioned forgiveness from all aspects and encompassed all its places. Since the aspect of the husband permits forgiveness from the two, He said '...except that they forgive...' meaning the wives, and '...or the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage forgives...' meaning the guardian, then He said '...and that you forgive is closer to piety...' meaning the husbands. If the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage were the husband, then He would have omitted some divisions, namely addressing the guardian, and repeated the address to the husband. Interpreting the address in His statement '...or the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage forgives...' as having a different benefit from what '...and that you forgive is closer to piety...' is interpreted as is better than interpreting it as mere repetition and emphasis.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the first type of objection by participation: both parties use the same apparent evidence without one having an advantage over the other. It then elaborates on the first subcategory: when a single word is shared between two meanings (mushtarak), each side interprets it to support its own view. The example is a well-known fiqhi dispute: the Maliki school argues that the guardian (wali) may forgive the dowry after divorce, based on the Qur'anic phrase 'the one in whose hand is the knot of marriage' (Q. 2:237). The Malikis say this refers to the guardian. The Shafi'i or Hanafi objectors claim the phrase refers to the husband, thus it supports their view that only the husband can forgive. The passage then provides three responses from the Maliki side. First, a linguistic argument: the guardian is more fittingly called 'the one in whose hand is the knot' because he has only the contract, while the husband has a share in the marriage itself. Second, a discourse shift argument: God addresses the husbands directly (second person plural) then speaks of a third person, indicating the phrase refers to someone else. Third, a comprehensive enumeration argument: God mentions forgiveness from wives, from the one with the knot, and from the husbands (ya'fuu), showing three separate categories. If the one with the knot were the husband, there would be repetition and omission of the guardian. These responses illustrate how the original arguer can defend his interpretation against a participation objection.

Key Terms
لفظ مشترك
حجة
المطلب الأول

A) The word 'wali' is more appropriate for the guardian.
B) God addressed the husbands directly elsewhere in the same verse.
C) The verse mentions three categories: wives, the one with the knot, and husbands, so it cannot be the husband.
D) The husband has no right to forgive the dowry.

Key Takeaway
A shared-word objection (lafz mushtarak) can be rebutted by showing that one interpretation is stronger linguistically, contextually, or structurally, using strategies of appropriateness, discourse shift, or comprehensive enumeration.

Second Subcategory: Each Side Uses a Different Expression from the Verse
Introduces the second subcategory of type one with the game expiation verse example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the second subcategory of participation objection (tashik) where each party relies on a different expression from the same evidence.

Core Idea
Even when opponents cling to different words in the same verse, they still share the same evidence – that is what makes it a participation objection.

In jadal (dialectical debate), a participation objection (tashik) occurs when both sides claim the same evidence supports their position. The first subcategory involves each side using the same word. The second subcategory, introduced here, is subtler: each side relies on a different word or phrase from the same verse, yet both are still making a participation claim because the evidence itself – the verse – is identical. This passage illustrates this with a real fiqh debate between the Maliki and Shafi'i schools over Quran 5:95 concerning the expiation for killing game in ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared evidence
Find the single Qur'anic verse that both sides are using. In this example, it is Quran 5:95, which discusses the penalty for killing game while in ihram.
The verse mentions 'a sacrificial offering reaching the Kaaba' and 'an expiation of feeding the poor'.
2
Determine each side's chosen expression
The Maliki focuses on the word 'expiation' (kafara) to argue that the penalty is a kaffara. The Shafi'i presumably focuses on the phrase 'a sacrificial offering' (hady) or the judgment by two just persons, though his objection is cut off.
The texts says 'the Shafi'i objects saying: This verse is only a proof for me...' but the argument is incomplete.
3
Understand why this is still a participation objection
Even though each side attaches to a different word, they both base their arguments on the same verse. The participation objection is valid because the evidence (the verse) is the same, regardless of which specific word they emphasize.
This subcategory shows that participation is about the commonality of the evidence, not the specific wording.
4
Assess the incomplete objection
The passage cuts off before the Shafi'i completes his argument. In analyzing the dialectical exchange, note that the full reasoning is missing. How would the Shafi'i likely argue that the verse supports him? What would the Maliki's rejoinder be?
This incompleteness is a reminder that real debate texts often require reconstruction.

This passage introduces the second subcategory of the first type of participation objection: each party attaches to a different word from the same verse. The example is again from fiqh: the Maliki school uses the verse about killing game (Quran 5:95) to argue that the penalty for killing game in ihram is an expiation (kaffara). The Maliki focuses on the word 'expiation' (kafara) to support his view. The Shafi'i objects, claiming the verse is a proof for him, presumably focusing on the phrase 'a sacrificial offering reaching the Kaaba' or the judgment by two just persons. The passage cuts off before the Shafi'i completes his objection. This subcategory shows that even when the sides rely on different expressions within the same textual evidence, it is still a valid participation objection because the evidence itself is the same verse.

Key Terms

Multiple Independent Answers in Jadal
Multiple responses to an objection about the marriage knot.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct multiple independent responses to a single objection in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
In jadal, multiple independent answers strengthen the case; each answer alone should suffice.

In dialectical debates (jadal), a skilled arguer must be ready to defend his position from multiple angles. This passage from a usul al-fiqh work shows how the mustadill (evidence presenter) answers an objection about the meaning of 'the one in whose hand is the marriage knot' in the Qur'an. The objection challenges the interpretation that this refers to the guardian (wali) rather than the husband. The mustadill provides three separate, self-sufficient arguments. This lesson will walk you through these arguments and the strategy behind offering multiple answers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection is about the identity of 'the one in whose hand is the marriage knot' in Quran 2:237. The opponent likely claims it refers to the husband. The mustadill must prove it refers to the guardian (wali).
2
Extract the first answer (linguistic)
The mustadill argues that the term is more fitting for the guardian because the guardian's only connection to marriage is the contract ('aqd), whereas the husband has additional rights. Thus, the guardian is more appropriately called 'the one in whose hand is the marriage knot'.
This answer is based on the meaning and connotations of the phrase.
3
Extract the second answer (textual context)
The mustadill notes that in the verse, Allah addresses the husbands directly ('If you divorce them...') but then refers to the pardoner in the third person ('or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot'). If it meant the husband, He would have continued with direct address. The shift to third person indicates a different referent (the guardian).
This answer relies on the consistency of address (iltifat) in the text.
4
Extract the third answer (comprehensive distribution)
The mustadill analyzes the entire verse: wives are addressed in one place, husbands in another, and the 'one who holds the knot' in between. If the 'one who holds the knot' were the husband, the distribution would be redundant (husbands mentioned twice) and omit the guardian. Thus, it must refer to the guardian.
This answer uses the structure of the verse to show completeness.
5
Generalize the strategy
Recognize that each answer is independent and sufficient. In jadal, preparing multiple arguments shows depth and prevents a single point of failure. This strategy is used in advanced usul al-fiqh and kalam debates.
The opponent must refute all three arguments to win the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إليه أولى فيكون المعنى إلا أن يعفو الزوجات أو يعفو الذي بيده عقدة النكاح وهو الزوج. ويمكن للمستدل هنا أن يجيب بأكثر من جواب. فيمكنه أن يجيب بأن يبين أن الذي بيده عقدة النكاح هو الولي، وأن هذا الاسم العقد في به أليق، وذلك أن الزوج له حظ من جهة العقد بالزوجية، والولي ليس له حظ النكاح إلا العقد، فكانت نسبته إليه أولى ولهذا يقال له ولي، وإن كان الزوج أيضًا وليا لنفسه لما انفرد بالولاية وكانت حظه من. كما يمكنه أن يجيب بأن الله تعالى قال: وَإِن طَلَّقْتُمُوهُنَّ مِن قَبْلِ أَن تَمَسُّوهُنَّ ... فواجه الأزواج بالخطاب ثم قال: أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ عُقْدَةُ النِّكَاح ... فخاطب غائبا، ولو أراد الأزواج لذكرهم بخطاب المواجهة. كما أن له أن يجيب بطريق ثالث، وهو أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى ذكر العفو من جميع الجهات واستوعب جميع مواضعه، فلما كانت جهة الزوج يصح فيها العفو من الاثنين قال: إِلَّا أَن يَعْفُونَ ... يريد الزوجات، و أَوْ يَعْفُوا الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ عُقْدَةُ النِّكَاح ... يريد الولي، ثم قال: وَأَن تَعْفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى ... يريد الأزواج، ولو كان الذي بيده عقدة النكاح هو الزوج لكان قد أخل ببعض الأقسام وهو خطاب الولي وكرر خطاب الزوج، وحمل الخطاب في قوله: أَوْ يَعْفُواْ الَّذِي بِيَدِهِ عُقْدَةُ النِّكَاح ... على فائدة غير ما حمل عليه وَأَن تَعْفُوا أَقْرَبُ لِلتَّقْوَى ... أولى حمله على مجرد التكرار والتأكيد.
To him is more appropriate, so the meaning is unless the wives pardon or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot pardons, and he is the husband. And it is possible for the one presenting the evidence (mustadill) here to answer with more than one answer. He can answer by showing that the one in whose hand is the marriage knot is the guardian (wali), and that this name is more fitting for the contract, because the husband has a share from the contract of marriage, while the guardian has no share of the marriage except the contract, so his relation to it is more appropriate, and therefore he is called wali, even if the husband is also a wali for himself because he alone has guardianship and his share is from... He can also answer that Allah Almighty said: 'And if you divorce them before you have touched them...' so He addressed the husbands directly, then He said: 'or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot pardons', so He addressed an absent party, and if He intended the husbands He would have mentioned them with direct address. Also he can answer in a third way: that the Blessed and Exalted mentioned pardon from all sides and encompassed all its positions. Since from the husband's side pardon is possible from both parties, He said: 'unless they (the wives) pardon' meaning the wives, and 'or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot pardons' meaning the guardian, then He said: 'and that you pardon is nearer to piety' meaning the husbands. And if the one in whose hand is the marriage knot were the husband, then He would have omitted some categories, namely addressing the guardian, and repeated addressing the husband. To assign the address in His statement 'or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot pardons' to a meaning other than that assigned to 'and that you pardon is nearer to piety' is more appropriate than assigning it to mere repetition and emphasis.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical example showing how the mustadill (the one presenting evidence) can answer an objection using multiple lines of argument. The objection is about the identity of 'the one in whose hand is the marriage knot' in the Qur'anic verse on divorce (al-Baqara 2:237). The mustadill gives three distinct answers. The first answer is purely linguistic: the term 'the one in whose hand is the marriage knot' is more fitting for the guardian because the guardian's only involvement in the marriage is the contract, whereas the husband has additional rights and duties from the marriage itself. The second answer uses textual context: in the verse, Allah addresses the husbands directly ('if you divorce them') but then refers to the pardoner in the third person ('or the one in whose hand is the marriage knot'), which indicates a different referent (the guardian). The third answer is a comprehensive analysis of the entire verse: Allah mentions pardon from three parties—wives ('they pardon'), guardian ('the one in whose hand is the marriage knot'), and husbands ('you pardon'—addressed to husbands). If the 'one in whose hand is the marriage knot' were the husband, the distribution would be redundant and incomplete. Hence, it must refer to the guardian. This demonstrates the depth of argumentation required in jadal: the mustadill must be prepared with multiple independent proofs, each sufficient to refute the objection.

الولي
العقد

Key Takeaway
Providing multiple independent arguments in jadal makes a position robust and hard to refute.

Clinging to Different Words: The Game Penalty Debate
Author illustrates the method where each disputant clings to a different word of the same verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical move where the Maliki and Shafi'i use different words of the same verse, analyze the objection and response, and apply the method to other usuli debates.

Core Idea
When two scholars cite different words of the same Quranic verse, the stronger jadal move is to reinterpret the opponent's word to support your own position.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usuli debate: is the penalty for hunting game in ihram an expiation (kaffarah) or a substitute (jaza')? The Maliki and Shafi'i both rely on the same verse (al-Ma'ida 5:95) but seize on different phrases. By following their dialectic, you will learn how to handle objections that cite a different part of your proof text—a common maneuver in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The Maliki claims the penalty is an expiation (kaffarah). The Shafi'i claims it is a substitute (jaza') for the game killed.
2
Locate the verse and the two phrases
Both rely on al-Ma'ida 5:95. The Maliki cites 'or an expiation: feeding the poor' (أو كفارة طعام مساكين). The Shafi'i cites 'a penalty like the animal he killed' (فجزاء مثل ما قتل من النعم).
3
Analyze the objection (i'tirad)
The Shafi'i objects that the word 'jaza'' implies a direct substitute, not an expiation. This challenges the Maliki's interpretation.
4
Examine the Maliki's response
The Maliki reinterprets 'jaza'' to mean 'equivalent' (al-'adl), i.e., the estimation of the expiation. Thus the verse means: the expiation is an animal equivalent to the game. This preserves the Maliki's evidence.
The response neutralizes the objection by redefining the key term.

ب - أن يتعلق كل واحد منهما من الآية بلفظ غير ما يتعلق به الآخر ويتناول لفظه: مثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي على أن ما يخرجه قاتل الصيد هو كفارة بقوله تعالى: ﴿يَحْكُمُ بِهِ ذَوَا عَدْلٍ مِّنكُمْ هَدْيًا بَالِغَ الْكَعْبَةِ أَوْ كَفَّارَةٌ طَعَامُ مَسَاكِينَ﴾. فيقول الشافعي معترضا: هذه الآية إنما هي حجة لي، وذلك أن الله تعالى قال: ﴿فَجَزَاءٌ مِّثْلُ مَا قَتَلَ مِنَ النَّعَمِ﴾، وهذا تصريح بأنه جزاء عن نفس الصيد وبدل منه لا كفارة. وطريق المالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول: إن الجزاء معناه العدل، ولذلك فإن المراد بالجزاء هاهنا تقدير الكفارة، فكأنه قال: إن القاتل للصيد متعمدا كفارته أن يهدي هديًا يعدل مثل ما قتل من الصيد، فليس في مانع من كون المخرج على وجه الكفارة. ومثاله أيضًا: استدلال الشافعي أو الحنبلي في بطلان النكاح بغير ولي بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا تَعْضُلُوهُنَّ أَن يَنكِحْنَ أَزْوَاجَهُنَّ﴾، فلو لم يكن تزويجها إلى الأولياء لما نهاهم عن العضل، إذ لا يتصور العضل عندهم؛ فيعترض الحنفي قائلا: في الآية ما يدل على أن النكاح بيد المرأة ولا دخل للولي فيه، وذلك قوله تعالى ﴿أَن يَنكِحْنَ﴾، إذ أضاف النكاح إليهن، فدل ذلك على أن لهن أن يعقدن، ونحن أرجح استدلالاً بها، لأن العقد إذا أطلق اقتضى العقد الصحيح، والعضل قد يكون بحق وبغير حق. والجواب: أن يسقط المستدل دليل المعترض ليسلم له ما تعلق به.
B - That each of the two clings to a different word from the verse and each takes his own word. Example: The Maliki argues that what the killer of game (while in ihram) gives out is an expiation (kaffarah) by the Almighty's statement: '...judged by two just men among you, a sacrificial animal reaching the Ka'bah or an expiation: feeding the poor.' Then the Shafi'i objects saying: This verse is actually an argument for me, because Allah Almighty said: 'a penalty (jaza') like the animal he killed from the cattle' and this is an explicit declaration that it is a penalty for the game itself and a substitute for it, not an expiation. The Maliki's way of answering this objection is to say: 'Jaza' means the equivalent, and therefore what is meant by jaza' here is the estimation of the expiation, as if He said: The killer of the game deliberately, his expiation is to offer a sacrificial animal equal to the game he killed. So there is no hindrance in considering the payment as an expiation. Another example: The Shafi'i or Hanbali argument that marriage without a guardian is invalid, based on the Almighty's statement: 'So do not prevent them from marrying their husbands.' If their marriage were not entrusted to guardians, He would not have forbidden them from preventing, because prevention from them is inconceivable. Then the Hanafi objects saying: In the verse there is indication that marriage is in the woman's hand and the guardian has no say, namely His statement 'from marrying', since He attributed the marriage to them, proving that they can contract. We are more justified in using this as evidence, because when the contract is mentioned absolutely it implies a valid contract, and prevention may be with right or without right. The answer: The mustadill should invalidate the objector's evidence to preserve his own dependency on it.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method (ب) of dialectical argument where each disputant clings to a different word or phrase within the same Quranic verse. The author gives two examples. The first example is from the Maliki–Shafi'i debate on the penalty for hunting game in ihram (al-Ma'ida 5:95). The Maliki argues that the penalty is a kaffarah (expiation), citing the phrase 'or an expiation: feeding the poor'. The Shafi'i counters by citing another phrase in the same verse: 'a penalty (jaza') like the animal he killed', insisting it is a direct substitute, not an expiation. The Maliki answers by reinterpreting 'jaza'' to mean 'equivalent' or 'estimation', thereby reconciling the two phrases; the 'jaza'' is the estimation of the expiation, so it is still an expiation. The second example is from the Shafi'i/Hanbali–Hanafi debate on marriage without guardian. The Shafi'i/Hanbali uses the verse 'do not prevent them from marrying their husbands' (al-Baqara 2:232) to infer that guardians have the power to prevent, proving that marriage requires a guardian. The Hanafi objects by pointing out that the verse attributes the act of marrying to the women ('that they marry'), implying they have the authority to contract marriage themselves. The Hanafi claims his evidence is stronger because the term 'marry' when used absolutely indicates a valid contract. The author then states the answer: the mustadill must invalidate the objector's evidence to secure his own. This is a classic jadal maneuver: undermining the opponent's relied-upon text. The passage illustrates how close reading of Qur'anic phrasing is central to usuli dialectic.

Key Terms
الاعتراض
الجزاء
الكفارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example, how does the Maliki answer the Shafi'i's objection based on the word 'jaza''?
A) He argues that 'jaza'' means 'expiation' itself.
B) He reinterprets 'jaza'' as 'equivalent', making it the estimation of the expiation.
C) He denies that the verse contains the word 'jaza''.
D) He says the Shafi'i's verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
When an opponent cites a different word from your proof verse, you can reinterpret that word to make it consistent with your own evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another verse where two scholars use different words to support opposing rulings. Write out the objection (i'tirad) and the response using the method illustrated in this lesson.

The Marriage Guardian Debate: Different Words from the Same Verse
Author illustrates the method where each disputant clings to a different word of the same verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the dialectical exchange on marriage guardianship between Hanafis and Shafi'is/Hanbalis, identify how each uses a different word from al-Baqara 2:232, and apply the general jadal principle of invalidating the opponent's evidence.

Core Idea
The same verse can be used to argue both for and against guardianship in marriage—the debate hinges on which word is emphasized and how 'prevention' is interpreted.

This lesson explores the second example from the passage: the debate on whether marriage requires a guardian. The Shafi'i/Hanbali uses the phrase 'do not prevent them' (fala ta'ḍulūhunna) to argue that guardians exist who can prevent, while the Hanafi uses 'that they marry' (an yankiḥna) to argue that women have the authority to contract marriage themselves. By analyzing this exchange, you will understand how to undermine your opponent's reliance on a different part of the same textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Shafi'i/Hanbali: marriage without a guardian is invalid. Hanafi: marriage is valid without a guardian.
2
Locate the verse and the two phrases
Both rely on al-Baqara 2:232: 'So do not prevent them from marrying their husbands' (فلا تعضلوهن أن ينكحن أزواجهن). The Shafi'i/Hanbali emphasizes 'do not prevent them' (فلا تعضلوهن). The Hanafi emphasizes 'that they marry' (أن ينكحن).
3
Analyze the Shafi'i/Hanbali's argument
If marriage were not entrusted to guardians, why would Allah forbid them from preventing? Prevention (عضل) is only conceivable from those who have the power to prevent, i.e., guardians. Thus, the verse implies guardianship is required.
4
Analyze the Hanafi's objection
The Hanafi objects that the verse attributes the act of marrying to women ('أن ينكحن'), proving they have the right to contract. Also, 'prevention' can be with or without right, so the verse does not necessarily indicate guardians have exclusive power.
5
Understand the response
The passage states: 'The answer: The mustadill should invalidate the objector's evidence to preserve his own dependency on it.' The Shafi'i/Hanbali must show that the Hanafi's interpretation is weak or that their phrase actually supports the mustadill's position.
The specific response is not detailed; the principle alone is given.

Example: The Maliki argues that what the killer of game (while in ihram) gives out is an expiation (kaffarah) by the Almighty's statement: '...judged by two just men among you, a sacrificial animal reaching the Ka'bah or an expiation: feeding the poor.' Equally General Evidence: The Second Demand of Jadal
Author describes the second method: both disputants argue from equally general evidence with no advantage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two disputants rely on equally general evidence and explain how the debate shifts to external criteria for preference (tarjih).

Core Idea
When evidence is equally general, victory lies not in the text but in tarjih principles.

In jadal, a disputant often argues from a general text without specification. But what happens when both sides have equally general evidence? This lesson introduces the second method: each using general evidence with no inherent superiority. You will learn to recognize this situation and understand that the debate then moves to other grounds for preference, such as analogy (qiyas) or presumption of continuity (istishab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of evidence
Determine if both disputants are appealing to general texts (عموم) with no specific advantage (مزية). The passage states: 'with no advantage for one over the other in it.'
The absence of mazyah means the texts are equally strong on the face of it.
2
Locate the common ground
Note that both accept the generality of the text; the dispute is not about the text's scope but about its application to the specific case (e.g., free vs. slave women).
The Maliki uses the verse without differentiation.
3
Check for external factors
Since the texts are equally general, the resolution must come from outside the texts, e.g., qiyas, istishab, or maslaha. This is the core of tarjih.
The passage cuts off, but typical jadal would proceed to external preference.
4
Analyze the Maliki example
See how the Maliki uses the general divorce verse (al-Baqara 2:229-230) to argue that divorce rules apply equally to free and slave women because the verse does not differentiate. Consider what objection the opponent (likely Hanafi) might raise.
The opponent might argue that other evidence qualifies the general rule for slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني: أن يستدل كل واحد منهما بدليل من جهة العموم لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر فيه. ومثاله: أن يستدل المالكي في أن اعتبار الطلاق بالرجال، بقوله تعالى: ﴿الطَّلَاقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكٌ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحٌ بِإِحْسَانٍ﴾ إلى قوله ﴿فَإِن طَلَّقَهَا فَلَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِن بَعْدُ حَتَّى تَنكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ﴾ فجعل لكل زوج أن يطلق زوجته ثلاثا، ولم يفصل بين أن تكون الزوجة حرة أو أمة، فوجب أن يحمل ذلك...
The second demand: That each of the two argues with an evidence from the aspect of generality, with no advantage for one over the other in it. Its example: The Maliki argues that divorce is attributed to men, by the Almighty's statement: 'Divorce is twice, then either retention in kindness or release with goodness' up to His statement: 'And if he divorces her, then she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband.' So He made it permissible for every husband to divorce his wife three times, and He did not differentiate between whether the wife is free or a slave, so it is necessary to apply that...
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new categorical method in the dialectical manual, called 'the second demand' (al-matlub al-thani). Here, both disputants rely on text that is general in scope, and neither has an inherent advantage over the other. The author gives an example from Maliki jurisprudence concerning divorce: the Maliki argues that the general Qur'anic verses on divorce (al-Baqara 2:229-230) apply equally to free and slave women because they do not differentiate. The verse says 'divorce is twice' and 'if he divorces her she is not lawful until she marries another', without specifying the woman's status. The Maliki concludes that the ruling applies to all women. The passage cuts off, but the intended contrast is likely that the opponent (possibly the Hanafi) would argue that the general rule is qualified by other evidence restricting some divorces. The key point is that when evidence is equally general, the preference (tarjih) must be determined by an external factor (like qiyas or istishab). This type of jadal requires the disputants to argue from the same level of generality, forcing the debate to move to other principles.

Key Terms
المطلب
العموم
المزية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the verses specifically mention slave women.

D) Because the Maliki relies on a hadith instead of the verse.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, when both disputants present equally general evidence, the debate must move to external preference methods such as qiyas or istishab.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(Example: the debate on whether a sale contract is valid without specification of price—both sides may appeal to general 'aya on trade. What external principle would resolve it?)

Analyzing a Dialectical Refutation in Usul al-Fiqh
Author presents a Maliki response to an objection about the verse on hunting penalty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dialectical structure of a Maliki response to an objection about the hunting penalty verse and explain how redefining a key term resolves the dispute.

Core Idea
In jadal, redefining a key term can transform an opponent's premise into support for your own position.

In this passage, an objector uses the Quranic verse on hunting during ihram to argue that the penalty is a substitute for the game, not an expiation. The Maliki jurist responds by reinterpreting the word 'jaza'' (penalty) to mean 'equivalence' (adl), thereby showing it actually denotes an expiation. This dialectical move—redefining a key term—is a powerful technique in usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (i'tiraad)
Find the claim the objector makes: the penalty is a substitute, not an expiation. Look for the phrase that cites the verse and concludes it is a badal.
The objector uses the explicit wording of the verse to argue for substitution.
2
Locate the Maliki's response (jawab)
The Maliki responds by redefining 'penalty' as 'equivalence' and then showing it is an expiation. Identify the sentence beginning with 'إن الجزاء معناه العدل'.
3
Analyze the term redefinition (tahrir al-mahall)
How does shifting the meaning of 'jaza'' from 'substitute' to 'equivalence' change the argument? The Maliki reinterprets the verse as prescribing a valued expiation rather than a direct replacement.
This type of definitional move is common in jadal to reconcile proofs with a school position.
4
Evaluate the strength of the response
Does the redefinition hold within the verse? What does it achieve for the Maliki school? Consider whether the linguistic argument is plausible and how it refutes the objection.
The strength lies in the Arabic usage of 'jaza'' to mean both penalty and equivalence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : هو ... فَجَزَاهُ مِثْلُ مَا قَتَلَ مِنَ النَّعَمِ ... ، وهذا تصريح بأنه جزاء عن نفس الصيد وبدل منه لا كفارة. ذلك وطريق المالكي في الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إن الجزاء معناه العدل، ولذلك فإن المراد بالجزاء هاهنا تقدير الكفارة، فكأنه قال: إن القاتل للصيد متعمدا كفارته أن يهدي هديًا يعدل مثل ما قتل من الصيد، فليس في مانع من كون المخرج على وجه الكفارة
He said: '... then a penalty like what he killed of livestock...' This is an explicit statement that it is a penalty for the game itself and a substitute for it, not an expiation. That (or: then) the Maliki's way in answering this objection is to say: The meaning of penalty is equivalence, therefore what is meant by penalty here is an estimation of the expiation, as if He said: 'The one who kills game intentionally, his expiation is to offer a sacrificial animal equivalent to what he killed of the game,' so there is no obstacle to it being considered an expiation.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical technique from usul al-fiqh. The author quotes an objection that uses the verse about hunting during ihram (Quran 5:95) to argue that the penalty is a substitute, not an expiation. The Maliki jurist responds by reinterpreting 'penalty' to mean 'equivalence' (adl), thereby showing that the penalty is actually an expiation (kaffarah), not a substitute. This is a key distinction: the objector tried to separate the two, but the Maliki reconciles them by defining the penalty as a valued expiation. The response clarifies the intended meaning of the verse, allowing the Maliki to maintain his position.

Key Terms
اعتراض (i'tiraad): An objection raised by an opponent against a proof, in this case objecting that the hunt penalty is a substitute. [Note: In jadal, i'tiraad is a standard step; this text uses it in the context of usul al-fiqh disputes.]
جواب (jawaab): The answer given by the proponent to refute the objection, here the Maliki interpretation. [Note: No significant shift.]
جزاء (jaza'): Penalty or compensation; the Maliki defines it as 'adl (equivalence) to argue it is an expiation. [Note: In fiqh, jaza' can mean both penalty and expiation; the Maliki here narrows it to equivalence.]
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation; the Maliki argues the hunted animal penalty is a kaffarah, not a mere substitute. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Maliki response to the hunting penalty objection, what does the Maliki argue the word 'jaza'' (جزاء) means in this context?
A) Punishment without equivalence
B) A substitute for the game without expiation
C) Equivalence (adl) and thus an expiation
D) A fine paid to the treasury

Key Takeaway
The Maliki's redefinition of 'jaza'' as 'adl' turns the proof for a substitute into a proof for expiation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the Maliki response, identify the exact phrase where the Maliki redefines 'jaza''. (Answer: 'إن الجزاء معناه العدل')
?

A Jadal Exchange on the Role of the Guardian in Marriage
Author gives an example of a jadal exchange between Shafi'i/Hanbali and Hanafi on marriage without guardian.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three steps of a jadal exchange (istidlal, i'tiraad, jawab) in a real usul al-fiqh debate and evaluate who has the stronger argument.

Core Idea
Jadal is a structured debate where each side uses the same evidence but different interpretations.

In Islamic legal theory, dialectical exchanges (jadal) are not just arguments; they follow a rigorous structure. This lesson walks you through a classic example between the Shafi'i/Hanbali and Hanafi schools regarding whether a marriage without a guardian is valid. The debate hinges on the interpretation of a single Quranic verse. By understanding this exchange, you will see how jadal rules govern the use of textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's initial argument (istidlal)
Find the Shafi'i/Hanbali's use of the verse. He uses the prohibition of prevention (عضل) to argue that guardians must be involved, because if they had no authority, the prohibition would be meaningless.
The proponent's claim is that marriage without guardian is invalid.
2
Identify the objector's counter-argument (i'tiraad)
The Hanafi uses the same verse but points to the phrase 'أن ينكحن' (that they marry) to argue that women have the right to contract.
He also adds that the unqualified contract implies valid contract, and prevention can be with or without right.
3
Analyze the objector's reasoning
The Hanafi claims his interpretation is stronger because the attribution of the verb to women directly supports their agency. He also uses a linguistic point about the absolute contract.
The objector tries to reverse the proponent's evidence.
4
Identify the proponent's response (jawab)
The proponent should 'drop the objector's argument' (يسقط دليل المعترض) to preserve his own proof. This means he dismisses the objection as irrelevant to his own argument.
In jadal, this is a valid move: the proponent does not need to disprove the objection; he only needs to show it does not affect his evidence.
5
Evaluate the outcome
The author does not explicitly declare a winner, but by presenting the jawab as the standard response, he implies the proponent can defend his position effectively.
The debate shows how jadal allows for multiple valid interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله أيضًا : استدلال الشافعي أو الحنبلي في بطلان النكاح بغير ولي بقوله تعالى : . . . فَلَا تَعْضُلُوهُنَّ أَن يَنكِحْنَ أَزْوَاجَهُنَّ . . . ، فلو لم يكن تزويجها إلى الأولياء لما نهاهم عن العضل، إذ لا يتصور العضل عندهم؛ فيعترض الحنفي قائلا : في الآية ما يدل على أن النكاح بيد المرأة ولا دخل للولي فيه، وذلك قوله تعالى (أَنْ يَنكِحْنَ)، إذ أضاف النكاح إليهن، فدل ذلك على أن لهن أن يعقدن، ونحن أرجح استدلالاً بها، لأن العقد إذا أطلق اقتضى العقد الصحيح، والعضل قد يكون بحق وبغير حق(3) والجواب : أن يسقط المستدل دليل المعترض ليسلم له ما تعلق به
And an example of that also: The Shafi'i or Hanbali argument for the invalidity of marriage without a guardian by His statement '... then do not prevent them from marrying their husbands...', for if their marriage were not up to the guardians, He would not have prohibited prevention, since prevention is inconceivable from them. So the Hanafi objects saying: In the verse there is evidence that marriage is in the woman's hand and the guardian has no role, and that is His statement 'that they marry', for He attributed marriage to them, so that indicates that they can contract, and we are more correct in reasoning by it, because the contract when used absolutely implies the valid contract, and prevention may be with right or without right. And the answer: that the proponent should drop the objector's argument to keep what he relies upon.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example of a jadal exchange between two madhhabs. The Shafi'i/Hanbali uses the verse (Surah al-Baqarah 2:232) that forbids guardians from preventing women from marrying their husbands, arguing that if guardians had no authority, the prohibition would be meaningless. The Hanafi objector turns the argument around: the verse attributes marriage (ankaha) to the women themselves, indicating they have the right to contract. The Hanafi claims his interpretation is stronger because the unqualified contract implies valid contract, and prevention can be with or without right. Finally, the answer (jawab) is given: the proponent should refute the objection by dropping the objector's proof to preserve his own. This is a standard method in jadal: the proponent shows that the objector's argument is irrelevant or not decisive.

Key Terms
استدلال
اعتراض
جواب
بطلان النكاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'لا تعضلوهن' (do not prevent them)
B) 'أن ينكحن' (that they marry)
C) 'أزواجهن' (their husbands)
D) 'عضل' (prevention)

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the objector often uses the same evidence as the proponent but with a different interpretation, and the proponent's response is to neutralize the objection without necessarily engaging it fully.

Then have a Shafi'i object using a different verse or reasoning. Then provide the Maliki's response. Use the three-step structure: istidlal, i'tiraad, jawab.
