Jadal Using a General Text: The Maliki Responds to the Hanafi on Divorce and Slavery
Author presents a jadal case where both parties use the same general text, with the Maliki responding to the Hanafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a jadal exchange where both parties cite the same general text and evaluate the respondent's strategy for excluding the opponent's extension.

Core Idea
When both sides use the same general text, victory goes to the one who can justify excluding the opponent's case.

This lesson examines a classic jadal scenario: both the proponent and the objector appeal to the same Quranic verse on divorce. The Maliki argues that the verse's generality includes slave wives, while the Hanafi objects that it also includes slave husbands. The Maliki's response shows how to break the symmetry by restricting the scope of the address. Understanding this move is essential for any student of Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
What is the Maliki's initial claim? What is the Hanafi's objection? The Maliki claims that a free man divorcing a slave wife gets three divorces from the verse. The Hanafi objects that the same verse would give a slave husband three divorces, which the Malikis deny.
2
Locate the common text
Which Quranic verse is cited? It is Surah al-Baqarah 2:229-230. Both sides appeal to its generality: the verse does not distinguish between free and slave in either the husband or the wife.
3
Analyze the symmetry
Why does the Hanafi's objection create a deadlock? Because both sides have an equal claim to the text's generality. The Maliki must break the symmetry to defend his position.
4
Examine the Maliki's two-fold answer
First, he adopts the principle that absolute address (khitab mutlaq) does not include slaves without evidence. Second, he argues that the address is to free men (rijal), and slavery has been established to affect divorce rulings, so the verse only applies to free men.
Both arguments aim to exclude the slave husband from the general ruling.
5
Evaluate the dialectical effectiveness
Does the Maliki's answer succeed? It provides reasons, but a Hanafi could challenge the principles. The outcome depends on the strength of the usul principles used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : أن يستدل كل واحد منهما بدليل من جهة العموم لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر فيه ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي في أن اعتبار الطلاق بالرجال، بقوله تعالى : الطَّلَقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكُ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحُ بِإِحْسَنِ ...)، إلى قوله ... فإن طَلَّقَهَا فَلَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ مِنْ بَعْدُ حَتَّى تَنكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَةُ ... فجعل لكل زوج أن يطلق زوجته ثلاثا، ولم يفصل بين أن تكون الزوجة حرة أو أمة، فوجب أن يحمل ذلك على عمومه واقتضى ذلك أن الحر إذا كانت تحته أمة أن طلاقها ثلاث، وعندهم لا يملك إلا طلقتين . فيعترض عليه الحنفي بأن يقول : هذه الآية حجة لنا، وذلك أنه جعل لكل زوج أن يطلق زوجته ثلاثا ولم يفصل بين أن يكون الزوج حرًا أو عبدا، وهذا يقتضي أنه إذا كان عبدا وتحته حرة أنه يملك ثلاث تطليقات وعندكم لا يملك إلا تطليقتين . لا فكل واحد منهما - أي كل من المعترض والمستدل – تعلق بعموم الآية . والطريق في الجواب عن ذلك أن يبين المالكي وهو المستدل هنا، أنه دخول للعبد في الآية، وذلك من وجهين : أحدهما أن يسلك طريق من يقول : إن الخطاب المطلق لا يدخل فيه العبد إلا بدليل . والثاني : أن يقول : إن المخاطب بهذه الآية هم الرجال، فالظاهر أنهم إنما يخاطبون بأحكامهم وما يعتبر بصفاتهم، ولو كان معتبرًا بصفات النساء لخوطبن به، وقد ثبت أن للعبودية تأثيرًا في الطلاق، فوجب أن يكون ذلك في الرجال المخاطبين
The second demand: that each of them argues with a proof from the aspect of generality, with no advantage for one over the other in it. And an example of that: The Maliki argues, regarding that divorce is based on the husband, by His statement: 'Divorce is twice, then hold with kindness or release with goodness...' up to His saying: '... and if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him afterwards until she marries another husband...' He made it for every husband to divorce his wife three times, and he did not distinguish between the wife being free or a slave, so it is necessary to interpret it according to its generality, and that necessitates that if a free man has a slave wife, his divorcing her is three, while according to them he only has two divorces. So the Hanafi objects by saying: This verse is an argument for us, because He made it for every husband to divorce his wife three times and did not distinguish between the husband being free or a slave, and this necessitates that if a slave has a free wife, he has three divorces, while according to you he only has two. So each of them - i.e., the objector and the proponent - holds onto the generality of the verse. And the way of answering that is that the Maliki, who is the proponent here, should explain that the inclusion of the slave in the verse is not the case, and that by two aspects: one is to follow the path of those who say: absolute address does not include the slave except with evidence. And the second: to say that those addressed by this verse are the free men, so apparently they are only addressed regarding their rulings and what is considered according to their attributes, and if it were considered according to the attributes of women, they would have been addressed; and it has been established that slavery has an effect on divorce, so it must be that [the ruling] is regarding the free men addressed.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents a second type of jadal situation: when both the proponent and objector use the same general textual evidence, and one side has no advantage over the other in terms of the proof's generality. The example given is about divorce: the Maliki argues from the Quranic verses on divorce (Surah al-Baqarah 2:229-230) that the husband can divorce his wife three times, and since the verse does not distinguish between free and slave wives, the free husband divorcing a slave wife should also be three times, though the Hanafis say he only has two. The Hanafi objects by turning the argument around: the verse also does not distinguish between free and slave husbands, so a slave husband divorcing a free wife should also have three divorces, though the Malikis say he only has two. Now both sides hold onto the generality. The Maliki's answer (jawab) is twofold: first, he adopts the position that absolute address does not include slaves without evidence; second, he argues that the address is specifically to free men (rijal), not slaves, and that slavery impacts divorce rulings. Thus, the generality does not include slaves. This is a sophisticated dialectical move: the Maliki does not simply assert his view but provides reasons to exclude the opponent's extension.

Key Terms
عموم
خطاب مطلق
دخول
اعتبار الطلاق بالرجال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The verse is abrogated
B) The absolute address does not include slaves without evidence
C) The Hanafi's argument is based on analogy, not text
D) The verse was revealed about free men only

Key Takeaway
When both sides cite the same general text, the respondent can win by providing principles that exclude the opponent's extension.

(4:23) includes marriage to a woman's sister by milk-relationship, citing generality. The Hanafi objects that the verse also includes a man's own sister by milk-relationship. Write the Shafi'i's two-fold answer.

Restricting Umum with Attributes: The Divorce Debate
Hanafi objects to Maliki's use of generality; Maliki responds with two exclusion methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of the objector and responder in a jadal exchange and evaluate the responder's methods for restricting a general text.

Core Idea
A general Quranic text can be restricted by the attributes of the addressee.

In this passage, we witness a classic jadal exchange between a Hanafi and a Maliki jurist. Both cling to the generality of the same verse, yet they reach opposite rulings. The Maliki must show why a slave is excluded from that generality. The tools used here—absolute address and attribute-based restriction—are fundamental in usul al-fiqh for reconciling seemingly contradictory applications of a general text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial positions
What does the Maliki claim based on the verse's generality? What is the Hanafi's objection using the same generality? Write down each party's claim.
The Maliki says a free man with a slave wife can give three divorces; the Hanafi says a slave man with a free wife should also have three.
2
Recognize the symmetrical argument
Notice that both the objector (mu'tarid) and the responder (mustadill) rely on the same verse and its generality. This is a common tactic in jadal: using the opponent's own evidence against them.
3
Analyze the first method of response
The Maliki's first method: he adopts the position that the absolute address (khitab mutlaq) does not include slaves without evidence. How does this method exclude the slave?
If the address is absolute, it defaults to free persons unless a separate proof brings in the slave.
4
Analyze the second method of response
The second method: the verse addresses men based on their attributes. Slavery is an attribute that affects the divorce ruling, so the slave's ruling is different. How does this restrict generality?
The generality is not overridden but interpreted in light of the attribute (slavery) that changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على عمومه واقتضى ذلك أن الحر إذا كانت تحته أمة أن طلاقها ثلاث، وعندهم لا يملك إلا طلقتين . فيعترض عليه الحنفي بأن يقول : هذه الآية حجة لنا، وذلك أنه جعل لكل زوج أن يطلق زوجته ثلاثا ولم يفصل بين أن يكون الزوج حرًا أو عبدا، وهذا يقتضي أنه إذا كان عبدا وتحته حرة أنه يملك ثلاث تطليقات وعندكم لا يملك إلا تطليقتين . فكل واحد منهما - أي كل من المعترض والمستدل – تعلق بعموم الآية . والطريق في الجواب عن ذلك أن يبين المالكي وهو المستدل هنا، أنه دخول للعبد في الآية، وذلك من وجهين : أحدهما أن يسلك طريق من يقول : إن الخطاب المطلق لا يدخل فيه العبد إلا بدليل . والثاني : أن يقول : إن المخاطب بهذه الآية هم الرجال، فالظاهر أنهم إنما يخاطبون بأحكامهم وما يعتبر بصفاتهم، ولو كان معتبرًا بصفات النساء لخوطبن به، وقد ثبت أن للعبودية تأثيرًا في الطلاق، فوجب أن يكون ذلك في الرجال المخاطبين
Based on its generality, that implies that if a free man has a female slave under him, her divorce is three, while according to them he only has two divorces. The Hanafi objects by saying: This verse is an argument for us, because it made every husband able to divorce his wife three times and did not distinguish between whether the husband is free or a slave, and this implies that if a slave has a free woman under him, he has three divorces, while according to you he only has two divorces. So each of them—i.e., the objector and the arguer—clung to the generality of the verse. The way to answer that is for the Maliki, who is the arguer here, to show the exclusion of the slave from the verse, and that is in two ways: one of them is to follow the method of those who say that the absolute address does not include the slave except by evidence. The second is to say: Those addressed by this verse are men, and the apparent meaning is that they are addressed concerning their rulings and what is considered based on their attributes, and if it were considered based on women's attributes they would have been addressed; and it has been established that slavery has an effect on divorce, so it must be that this applies to the addressed men.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange in jadal between a Hanafi and a Maliki regarding the interpretation of a Quranic verse on divorce. The Maliki initially argues that the generality of the verse implies that a free man married to a slave woman can give three divorces, but the Hanafis hold that a slave owner only has two divorces. The Hanafi objects by using the same generality to argue that if a slave man is married to a free woman, he should have three divorces according to the Maliki's logic, but the Maliki denies this. The author then explains the method of response: the Maliki must show why the slave is excluded from the generality of the verse. Two methods are given: first, the absolute address (khitab mutlaq) does not include the slave without evidence; second, the addressees of the verse are men, and their rulings are based on their attributes, and slavery is an attribute that affects the divorce ruling, so the slave's ruling differs. The commentary follows the author's order: first presenting the objection, then the response with two justifications. All examples are from Islamic jurisprudence.

Key Terms
عموم
خطاب مطلق
عبودية

B) He claims that the verse is abrogated by another verse.
C) He claims that the verse applies only to free men and not to slaves.
D) He claims that the Hanafi's objection is invalid because the verse is clear.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a general text can be restricted by showing that the attribute of the addressee (e.g., slavery) affects the ruling, thus excluding them from the generality.

Objection Using Different Qira'at in Jadal
Hanafi objects to wudu from 'lamastum' using 'lamastum' meaning intercourse; responses reconcile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a dialectical exchange where an objector uses a different qira'a, and evaluate the two standard responses: demonstrating semantic flexibility or reconciling the readings.

Core Idea
Different qira'at are not contradictions but complementary evidence; reconciliation is preferred.

This lesson examines a classic jadal scenario: a Hanafi objects to a ruling based on one qira'a by citing another qira'a. You will learn how an usuli can defend his position by either showing the word's semantic range or by harmonising both readings. This is a core skill in usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim and evidence
Find the original arguer's position: that wudu is obligatory from touching women. What is their evidence? They cite the Quranic qira'a 'aw lamastum' (touched) and claim it literally means hand touch.
The initial arguer is from Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali school.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The Hanafi objector cites a different qira'a: 'aw lamastum' (mutual touching). He argues this indicates intercourse, not hand touch, because the form fā‘alta (mufā‘ala) requires two participants.
The objection uses a grammatical argument based on Arabic morphology.
3
Analyse the grammatical reasoning
The Hanafi claims that 'lamastum' (from fā‘alta) is a reciprocal form, so it must mean an action between two persons, i.e., sexual intercourse. Touching with the hand is done by one person, so it wouldn't take this form.
This is a strong argument if accepted; it forces the defender to respond.
4
Examine the first response: semantic flexibility
The defender shows that 'mulamasa' can also mean hand touch by citing the hadith of bai' al-mulamasa, where it means handling the item by hand. This refutes the claim that the word exclusively means intercourse.
This response relies on actual usage in authoritative sources.
5
Examine the second response: reconciliation
The defender reconciles both qira'at: adopt wudu from hand touch from 'lamastum', and ghusl from intercourse from 'lamastum'. This follows the usuli principle that reconciling evidences is better than discarding some.
Reconciliation (jam') is a preferred method in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع الاعتراض باختلاف القراءات يكون من وجهين اثنين : المطلب الأول : أن يستدل المستدل بقراءة فيعارضه المعترض بقراءة أخرى ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على وجوب الوضوء بلمس النساء بقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَوْ لَمَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ ﴾؛ إذ أن اللمس حقيقة في المماسة باليد . فيعترضه الحنفي بأن المراد بالآية ليس مجرد اللمس، وإنما الجماع، والدليل عليه أنه قُرئ: أَوْ لَامَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ ، وهذا لا يستعمل إلا في الجماع، لأنه من فاعلت، وذلك لا يكون إلا في الجماع لأنه من اثنين، فأما اللمس باليد فإنه من فاعل واحد، فلا تستعمل فيه المفاعلة. والجواب على هذا الاعتراض يكون بأحد مسلكين : الأول : أن يبين المستدل أن ذلك لا يقتضي الجماع، فإن الملامسة قد تستعمل في اللمس باليد ولهذا روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن بيع الملامسة، والمراد بذلك اللمس باليد . والثاني : أن يقول المستدل : أنا أجمع بين القراءتين، فأوجب الوضوء من مجرد اللمس باليد عملا بقراءة من قرأ أو لمستم، وأوجب الغسل بالجماع بقراءة من قرأ أو لامستم لأن الجمع بين الأدلة - إن أمكن – أولى من إسقاط بعضها واستعمال بعضها الآخر
Fourth topic: Objection by difference in recitations is in two aspects: First: That the arguer uses a recitation and the objector opposes him with another recitation. Example: The Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues for the obligation of wudu from touching women by the Almighty's saying: '... or you have touched women'; since touching is literal in touching with the hand. So the Hanafi objects to him by saying that what is meant by the verse is not mere touching, but rather intercourse, and the evidence for it is that it was recited: 'or you have touched (lamastum) women', and this is not used except for intercourse, because it is from the form fā‘alta, and that only occurs in intercourse because it is from two persons; as for touching with the hand, it is from one agent, so the reciprocal form is not used for it. The answer to this objection is by one of two approaches: First: That the arguer shows that this does not necessitate intercourse, for 'mulamasa' may be used for touching with the hand, and for this reason it is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, prohibited the sale of mulamasa, and by that is meant touching with the hand. Second: That the arguer says: I reconcile between the two recitations, so I make wudu obligatory from mere touching with the hand in accordance with the recitation of those who read 'aw lamastum', and I make ghusl obligatory for intercourse in accordance with the recitation of those who read 'aw lamastum', because reconciliation between evidences – if possible – is better than dropping some and using others.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange regarding an objection based on different qira'at (Quranic readings). The author presents a example: a Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali uses the qira'a 'lamastum' (touched) to argue that wudu is required from touching women, as 'lamastum' literally means touching with the hand. The Hanafi objects by citing the alternative qira'a 'lamastum' (mutual touching) which indicates intercourse, arguing that the verse actually refers to intercourse, not mere hand touching, because the form 'lamastum' (from fā‘alta) implies two participants. The response gives two approaches to rebut this objection. First, the arguer can show that 'mulamasa' can also mean touching with the hand, citing the hadith prohibiting the sale of mulamasa, which refers to handling the item by hand. Second, the arguer can reconcile the two readings: adopt the ruling of wudu from hand touching based on the 'lamastum' reading, and adopt ghusl from intercourse based on the 'lamastum' reading, prioritizing reconciliation over discarding some evidence. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each part of the exchange and the two strategies.

Objection by Different Qira'a: Second Example
Author presents second example of objection using different qira'at on menstrual purity verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and respond to a mu'āraḍah objection in Islamic legal dialectic using the example of different qira'at.

Core Idea
Opposing qira'at can be harmonized; they do not necessarily invalidate the original argument.

In this passage, the author presents a key dialectical technique: when an arguer relies on a specific qira'a, the objector can counter with a different qira'a to block the inference. But the astute arguer knows two powerful responses—demonstrating harmony between readings, or adding the missing condition from the other reading. Understanding this move strengthens your ability both to object and respond in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim and evidence
The Maliki argues that intercourse with a menstruating woman is prohibited until she performs ghusl, citing 'ḥattā yaṭṭahharna' (with shaddah) from verse 2:222, which means purification with water.
The arguer's evidence is a specific qira'a.
2
Identify the objector's mu'āraḍah
The opponent presents the alternative reading 'ḥattā yaṭhurna' (without shaddah) and also 'fa-idhā taṭahharna' to show that only cessation of blood is required for permissibility, blocking the arguer's inference.
Mu'āraḍah is presenting a counter-dalil from the same source.
3
Extract the two responses
First: the arguer shows that the objector's reading also implies two conditions—cessation of blood and purification—since 'taṭahharna' means ghusl. Second: even if the objector's reading only implied one condition, the arguer can add the condition of ghusl from his own reading, treating the readings like two separate verses.
Both responses aim to show that the objector's reading does not contradict the arguer's.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
The arguer's responses effectively neutralize the objection by harmonizing the qira'at or treating them as complementary. The objector's challenge does not defeat the original argument; instead, the arguer demonstrates that both readings support the need for ghusl.
The response preserves the strength of the original dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : أن يستدل المستدل بقراءة فيعارضه المعترض بقراءة أخرى ليوقف استدلاله بالقراءة التي استدل بها مثاله؛ استدلال المالكي على أن الحائض إذا انقطع دمها لم يجز وطؤها حتى تغتسل، بقوله تعالى : ... وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهَّرْنَ . . . (1) – بالتشديد - وأصله يتطهرن، فأبدل التاء طاء وأدغمها في الطاء الثانية، والتطهر هو الاغتسال بالماء . فيعترضه المخالف بأن هذه القراءة معارضة بالقراءة الأخرى وهي ... وَلَا نَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ . . . - بالتخفيف - و . . . فَإِذَا تَطَهَرْنَ فَأْتُوهُنَ ...، فعلق إباحة الوطء على غاية وهي انقطاع الدم مما يدل على جواز الوطء بعد هذه الغاية .. ويمكن للمستدل بالآية الأولى أن يجيب عن هذا الاعتراض بأحد وجهين : الأول : أن يبين أن القراءتين ليس بينهما تناف أو تضاد، وذلك أن القراءة التي تمت بها المعارضة تدل هي الأخرى على نفس المعنى، حيث علقت إباحة الوطء على شرطين : انقطاع الدم والتطهر بالماء، وهو نفس ما تدل عليه الآية التي تم بها الاستدلال أول مرة . والثاني : أن يقول المستدل في الجواب : أنه لو لم تقتض تلك القراءة إلا شرطًا واحدا، وهو الطهر من الحيض، لأضفنا إليه التطهر بقراءتنا، لأن حكم القراءتين حكم الآيتين، فجاز أن يراد بإحداهما حكم غير ما تقتضي القراءة الأخرى.
The second demand: That the arguer argues using a reading, and the objector opposes him with another reading to stop his argument with the reading he used. Its example: The Maliki's argument that a menstruating woman, if her blood ceases, it is not permissible to have intercourse with her until she performs ghusl, by His statement the Exalted: '... and do not approach them until they are purified (yaṭṭahharna)...' (1) – with shaddah (emphasis), and its origin is yataṭahharna, so the ta was replaced with ṭa and assimilated into the second ṭa, and purification here is ghusl with water. So the opponent objects by saying that this reading is opposed by the other reading: '... and do not approach them until they become pure (yaṭhurna)...' – with takhfīf (without emphasis) – and '... then when they have purified themselves (taṭahharna), come to them...' Thus permissibility of intercourse is attached to an endpoint, which is the cessation of blood, indicating permissibility of intercourse after that endpoint. The arguer of the first verse can answer this objection by one of two aspects: First: to show that the two readings are not contradictory or conflicting, because the reading used in objection also indicates the same meaning, since it attaches permissibility of intercourse to two conditions: cessation of blood and purification with water, which is the same as indicated by the first verse. Second: That the arguer say in the answer: If that [other] reading required only one condition, which is purity from menstruation, we would add to it purification by our reading, because the ruling of two readings is like the ruling of two verses, so it is permissible that one of them intends a ruling different from what the other reading requires.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is another dialectical example of objection using differing qira'at. The author presents the second form: the objector uses the alternative reading not just to counter, but to completely block the arguer's inference. The example: The Maliki argues that a menstruating woman cannot be approached until she performs ghusl, citing the verse 'ḥattā yaṭṭahharna' (with shaddah), which derives from 'yataṭahharna', meaning to wash with water. The objector counters with the alternative reading 'ḥattā yaṭhurna' (without shaddah) and also the phrase 'fa-idhā taṭahharna', arguing that permissibility is attached solely to the cessation of blood (ṭuhr), implying that once blood stops, intercourse is allowed even without ghusl. The author provides two responses available to the Maliki arguer. First, he can argue that the two readings are not contradictory: the objector's reading also implies two conditions—cessation of blood and purification—as 'taṭahharna' explicitly mentions purification with water. Thus both readings agree. Second, if the objector's reading only implied one condition (purity from blood), the arguer can add the condition of ghusl from his own reading, treating the two readings like two separate verses that together yield the complete ruling. This demonstrates the principle of combining evidence and respecting the strength of multiple qira'at.

In jadal, an objection using a different qira'a can be answered by showing harmony between readings or by treating them as complementary conditions.

Objection by Citing a Different Qira'ah
Author explains and exemplifies objection by citing a rival qira'ah to halt an argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the dialectical move of objecting to a Qur'anic argument by citing a rival qira'ah, and assess the responses that reconcile the two recitations.

Core Idea
A different qira'ah can halt an argument but must be reconciled, not simply rejected.

In Islamic dialectics, an objector may respond to a Qur'an-based argument by citing a variant recitation (qira'ah) that seems to contradict the first. This does not necessarily mean the first argument fails; rather, the two qira'at can often be harmonized. This lesson uses the example of the Malikite's argument about post-menstrual intercourse to show how such an objection is made and how it can be answered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's initial proof
Find the verse and recitation (qira'ah) the Malikite uses to argue that ghusl is required after menstruation before intercourse. Note the specific wording with shaddah (yattahhirn).
2
Identify the objector's counter-proof
The objector cites a different qira'ah (yathurna) and another verse (tatahharna) to argue that only cessation of blood is needed. Identify the apparent contradiction.
3
Analyze the first response: reconciliation
The first response claims the two qira'at are not in conflict, as they both actually imply the same conditions: cessation of blood and purification. Verify this by examining the objector's verse's implication.
4
Analyze the second response: conditional addition
The second response concedes that the objector's verse might imply only one condition, but argues that the first verse adds a second condition because multiple qira'at are like multiple verses—each can contribute a ruling. Assess the validity of this approach.
5
Determine the outcome of the debate
Based on the responses, evaluate whether the Malikite's argument stands. The objector's challenge is defused, and the original ruling is preserved because the qira'at are reconciled or combined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : أن يستدل المستدل بقراءة فيعارضه المعترض بقراءة أخرى ليوقف استدلاله بالقراءة التي استدل بها. مثاله؛ استدلال المالكي على أن الحائض إذا انقطع دمها لم يجز وطؤها حتى تغتسل، بقوله تعالى : ... وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهَّرْنَ . . . – بالتشديد - وأصله يتطهرن، فأبدل التاء طاء وأدغمها في الطاء الثانية، والتطهر هو الاغتسال بالماء. فيعترضه المخالف بأن هذه القراءة معارضة بالقراءة الأخرى وهي ... وَلَا نَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ . . . - بالتخفيف - و . . . فَإِذَا تَطَهَرْنَ فَأْتُوهُنَ ...، فعلق إباحة الوطء على غاية وهي انقطاع الدم مما يدل على جواز الوطء بعد هذه الغاية. ويمكن للمستدل بالآية الأولى أن يجيب عن هذا الاعتراض بأحد وجهين : الأول : أن يبين أن القراءتين ليس بينهما تناف أو تضاد، وذلك أن القراءة التي تمت بها المعارضة تدل هي الأخرى على نفس المعنى، حيث علقت إباحة الوطء على شرطين : انقطاع الدم والتطهر بالماء، وهو نفس ما تدل عليه الآية التي تم بها الاستدلال أول مرة . والثاني : أن يقول المستدل في الجواب : أنه لو لم تقتض تلك القراءة إلا شرطًا واحدا، وهو الطهر من الحيض، لأضفنا إليه التطهر بقراءتنا، لأن حكم القراءتين حكم الآيتين، فجاز أن يراد بإحداهما حكم غير ما تقتضي القراءة الأخرى.
The second requirement: That the arguer argues with a recitation, then the objector objects with another recitation to halt his argument based on the recitation he used. Example: The Malikite's argument that if the menstruating woman's blood stops, it is not permissible to have intercourse with her until she performs ghusl, by His statement the Exalted: '...and do not approach them until they become purified (yattahhirn)' – with shaddah – and its origin is yatatahharun, so the ta was replaced with a ta and it was assimilated into the second ta, and purification is washing with water. Then the opponent objects that this recitation is opposed by the other recitation which is '...and do not approach them until they become purified (yathurna)' – with takhfif – and '...and when they have purified themselves (tatahharna), then come to them...' – so the permissibility of intercourse is attached to a limit, which is the cessation of blood, indicating the permissibility of intercourse after this limit. The arguer with the first verse can respond to this objection in one of two ways: First: To show that the two recitations are not in conflict or contradiction, because the recitation used for objection indicates the same meaning, where the permissibility of intercourse is attached to two conditions: cessation of blood and purification with water – the same as indicated by the verse used initially. Second: The arguer can say in response: Even if that recitation implied only one condition – that is, purity from menstruation – we would add the purification from our recitation, because the ruling of the two recitations is like the ruling of two verses, so it is permissible that one of them intends a ruling other than what the other recitation implies.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage describes a dialectical method where an objector uses a different qira'ah (variant recitation) to stop an opponent's argument. The example is a fiqh dispute about intercourse with a menstruating woman after her blood stops. The Malikite relies on the shaddah recitation of 'yattahhirn' meaning washing with water; the objector counters with the takhfif recitation 'yathurna' and the phrase 'tatahharna', suggesting that only cessation of blood is required. The author then presents two possible responses for the Malikite: first, to harmonize the qira'aat by showing both require both conditions; second, a conditional argument that even if only one condition were implied by the objector's verse, the Malikite's verse adds the second condition, as multiple qira'aat are treated like multiple verses which can each target different rulings. This illustrates the principle that qira'aat can be reconciled, and that a difference in recitation does not necessarily entail a contradiction in ruling.

Key Takeaway
Variant qira'at are not necessarily contradictory; the dialectician must either reconcile their implications or combine them to derive the full legal ruling.

Responding to Objection by Explicit Claim of Naskh
First type: objector explicitly claims naskh with an example involving fasting and fidyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an explicit naskh objection and formulate a response by showing that the alleged abrogation is limited in scope.

Core Idea
Explicit naskh claims require proving the abrogation applies to the specific case.

The first type of objection by claiming naskh occurs when the objector explicitly states that the opponent's proof verse has been abrogated. In the example, the Hanafi directly claims that the fidyah verse is abrogated by the later fasting verse. The defender must then demonstrate that the abrogation only applies to those not in the specific situation (pregnancy/breastfeeding) being argued. This lesson will teach you how to handle such explicit abrogation claims in dialectical exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Extract the original proof
Identify the verse and ruling the first party (e.g., Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) uses as evidence. Here, they use the fidyah verse to argue that pregnant/breastfeeding women must pay fidyah if they break the fast out of fear for the child.
The verse is about fidyah for those who are able but not fasting.
2
Pinpoint the explicit naskh claim
Locate where the objector (Hanafi) explicitly states abrogation. He says: 'This verse is abrogated by the verse [from Surah Baqarah] commanding those who witness the month to fast.'
The objector directly claims naskh without suggesting reconciliation.
3
Analyze the scope of abrogation
Notice that the defender limits the abrogation: the fasting verse only abrogates the fidyah requirement for those who are not in the specific situation of pregnancy/breastfeeding. These women have the choice to fast or pay fidyah, so the fidyah verse still applies to them.
The defender uses evidence that these women are mukhayyara (have a choice).
4
Apply the reconciliation principle
By limiting the scope, the defender reconciles the two verses: the fasting verse applies to ordinary Muslims, the fidyah verse applies to pregnant/breastfeeding women. Thus, no contradiction remains and the naskh objection fails.
This shows that jam' (reconciliation) is preferred over naskh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: الاعتراض بدعوى النسخ. ويقع الاعتراض بدعوى النسخ من أربعة أوجه: المطلب الأول: أن ينقل المعترض النسخ صريحًا. ومثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على إيجاب الفدية على الحامل والمرضع إذا أفطرتا في رمضان خوفًا على الجنين أو الرضيع، بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ ...) . فيعترض الحنفي بأن يقول : هذه الآية منسوخة بقوله عز وجل : . . فَمَن شَهدَ مِنكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْةُ . . وهنا على المستدل أن يثبت أن النسخ إنما حصل في حق من كان منه الإفطار من حمل ولا رضاع، بدليل أنهما مخيرتان بين الصوم وعدمه، فتكون الآية متجهة إلى غيرهما. المطلب الثاني: أن يدعي المعترض النسخ بآية متأخرة. من ذلك : استدلال المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي في جواز المن والفداء على أسرى المشركين بقوله تعالى : . . . فَإِمَّا مَنَّنا بَعْدُ وَإِمَّا فِدَاءٌ . . . فيعترض الحنفي بدعوى أن هذه الآية منسوخة بآية أخرى متأخرة عنها، وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿ ... فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ . . . ) من سورة براءة التي هي آخر سورة نزلت. والجواب على هذا الاعتراض : أن الجمع بين الآيتين ممكن، وما دام الجمع ممكنا فهو أولى من ادعاء النسخ.
The fifth chapter: Objection by claiming abrogation. The objection by claiming abrogation occurs in four aspects: The first requirement: That the objector transmit the abrogation explicitly. Its example: That the Malikite, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues for the obligation of fidyah on the pregnant and breastfeeding woman if they break the fast in Ramadan out of fear for the fetus or infant, by His statement the Exalted: 'And upon those who are able [to fast], a fidyah [of] feeding a poor person...'. Then the Hanafi objects by saying: This verse is abrogated by His statement the Exalted: '...so whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it...'. And here the arguer must prove that the abrogation only occurred for those whose breaking of the fast is due to pregnancy or breastfeeding, with the evidence that they have the choice between fasting or not, so the verse is directed to others. The second requirement: That the objector claims abrogation by a later verse. Among that: The argument of the Malikite, Shafi'i, or Hanbali for the permissibility of bestowing grace or ransom on the prisoners of the polytheists by His statement the Exalted: '...then either set them free (mann) as a favor or ransom...'. Then the Hanafi objects by the claim that this verse is abrogated by another later verse, which is His statement the Exalted: '...then kill the polytheists...' from Surah Bara'ah which was the last surah revealed. The answer to this objection: That reconciliation between the two verses is possible, and as long as reconciliation is possible, it is preferable to claiming abrogation.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents two types of objection by claiming naskh (abrogation) in jadal. The first type (muthalab al-awwal) occurs when the objector explicitly claims that the verse used by the opponent is abrogated by another. The example: a Malikite, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues from the verse about fidyah for those unable to fast (interpreted as pregnant/breastfeeding) that they must pay fidyah if they break the fast. The Hanafi objects that this verse is abrogated by the later verse commanding those who witness the month to fast. The defender must then prove that the abrogation only applies to those not in the specific situation of pregnancy/breastfeeding, since those women have a choice. The second type (muthalab al-thani) is when the objector claims abrogation by a later verse, specifically a verse that seems to contradict the first. The example: the defender argues from the verse about prisoners (mann or fida') that it is permissible to release or ransom them. The Hanafi objects that this is abrogated by the 'kill the polytheists' verse from the last revealed surah. The answer given is that reconciliation (jam') between the verses is possible, and when possible, it takes precedence over claiming abrogation. Both examples illustrate a common dialectical move in usul al-fiqh debates, where one party tries to nullify the opponent's proof by alleging naskh, and the defender responds by showing that the verses can be harmonized or that the naskh is limited.

Key Terms
نسخ
جمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of naskh objection, what is the Hanafi's claim and how does the defender respond?
A) The Hanafi claims the fidyah verse is abrogated by the fasting verse; the defender says the abrogation only applies to those not pregnant/breastfeeding.
B) The Hanafi claims the fasting verse is abrogated; the defender says it applies to all.
C) The Hanafi claims the fidyah verse is abrogated; the defender admits it is abrogated.
D) The Hanafi claims the verse about prisoners is abrogated; the defender says it is not.

Key Takeaway
When an objector explicitly claims naskh, the defender can show that the abrogation does not apply to the specific case, thus reconciling the verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a jadal exercise, one student presents the verse 'If any of you witnesses the month, let him fast it' as proof for obligatory fasting on all Muslims. Another objects: 'This verse is abrogated by the verse: Fasting is prescribed for you as it was prescribed for those before you.' How would you respond using the principle of jam'? Provide a reconciliation that distinguishes between obligation and recommendation.

Responding to Objection by Claim of Naskh with a Later Verse
Second type: objector claims naskh by a later verse, with an example of prisoner treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to respond to an objection that a later verse abrogates an earlier one by demonstrating that reconciliation (jam') is possible.

Core Idea
A later verse does not automatically abrogate; we must try to reconcile first.

The second type of naskh objection claims that a later revealed verse abrogates the opponent's proof verse. The example involves the verse on prisoner treatment (mann/fida') and the later verse in Surah Bara'ah commanding to kill polytheists. The correct response is to attempt reconciliation before accepting naskh. This lesson will show you how to apply the principle of jam' in such cases, a crucial skill in dialectical debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earlier proof verse
The defender uses the verse: 'Then either set them free as a favor or ransom' to argue that it is permissible to release or ransom captives.
This verse is from Surah Muhammad or similar, but the exact reference is not needed for the logical step.
2
Identify the later claimed abrogating verse
The objector (Hanafi) points to the verse from Surah Bara'ah (9:5): 'Kill the polytheists wherever you find them', which was revealed later and seems to command killing without exception.
Surah Bara'ah is known as the last surah revealed, so it is chronologically later.
3
Assess the possibility of reconciliation
Ask: Can these two verses be applied to different contexts? The defender suggests that the 'kill' verse applies to polytheists who are actively hostile, while the 'mann/fida' verse applies to captives who are under control. There is no genuine contradiction.
Reconciliation is possible by specifying the applicability of each verse to different situations.
4
Conclude that jam' is possible and naskh is not accepted
Since the verses can be reconciled, the objection of naskh is invalid. The defender can continue using his original proof.
This illustrates the usuli principle: al-jam' awla min al-naskh (reconciliation is preferable to abrogation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: الاعتراض بدعوى النسخ. ويقع الاعتراض بدعوى النسخ من أربعة أوجه: المطلب الأول: أن ينقل المعترض النسخ صريحًا. ومثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على إيجاب الفدية على الحامل والمرضع إذا أفطرتا في رمضان خوفًا على الجنين أو الرضيع، بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ ...) . فيعترض الحنفي بأن يقول : هذه الآية منسوخة بقوله عز وجل : . . فَمَن شَهدَ مِنكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْةُ . . وهنا على المستدل أن يثبت أن النسخ إنما حصل في حق من كان منه الإفطار من حمل ولا رضاع، بدليل أنهما مخيرتان بين الصوم وعدمه، فتكون الآية متجهة إلى غيرهما. المطلب الثاني: أن يدعي المعترض النسخ بآية متأخرة. من ذلك : استدلال المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي في جواز المن والفداء على أسرى المشركين بقوله تعالى : . . . فَإِمَّا مَنَّنا بَعْدُ وَإِمَّا فِدَاءٌ . . . فيعترض الحنفي بدعوى أن هذه الآية منسوخة بآية أخرى متأخرة عنها، وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿ ... فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ . . . ) من سورة براءة التي هي آخر سورة نزلت. والجواب على هذا الاعتراض : أن الجمع بين الآيتين ممكن، وما دام الجمع ممكنا فهو أولى من ادعاء النسخ.
The fifth chapter: Objection by claiming abrogation. The objection by claiming abrogation occurs in four aspects: The first requirement: That the objector transmit the abrogation explicitly. Its example: That the Malikite, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues for the obligation of fidyah on the pregnant and breastfeeding woman if they break the fast in Ramadan out of fear for the fetus or infant, by His statement the Exalted: 'And upon those who are able [to fast], a fidyah [of] feeding a poor person...'. Then the Hanafi objects by saying: This verse is abrogated by His statement the Exalted: '...so whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it...'. And here the arguer must prove that the abrogation only occurred for those whose breaking of the fast is due to pregnancy or breastfeeding, with the evidence that they have the choice between fasting or not, so the verse is directed to others. The second requirement: That the objector claims abrogation by a later verse. Among that: The argument of the Malikite, Shafi'i, or Hanbali for the permissibility of bestowing grace or ransom on the prisoners of the polytheists by His statement the Exalted: '...then either set them free (mann) as a favor or ransom...'. Then the Hanafi objects by the claim that this verse is abrogated by another later verse, which is His statement the Exalted: '...then kill the polytheists...' from Surah Bara'ah which was the last surah revealed. The answer to this objection: That reconciliation between the two verses is possible, and as long as reconciliation is possible, it is preferable to claiming abrogation.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents two types of objection by claiming naskh (abrogation) in jadal. The first type (muthalab al-awwal) occurs when the objector explicitly claims that the verse used by the opponent is abrogated by another. The example: a Malikite, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues from the verse about fidyah for those unable to fast (interpreted as pregnant/breastfeeding) that they must pay fidyah if they break the fast. The Hanafi objects that this verse is abrogated by the later verse commanding those who witness the month to fast. The defender must then prove that the abrogation only applies to those not in the specific situation of pregnancy/breastfeeding, since those women have a choice. The second type (muthalab al-thani) is when the objector claims abrogation by a later verse, specifically a verse that seems to contradict the first. The example: the defender argues from the verse about prisoners (mann or fida') that it is permissible to release or ransom them. The Hanafi objects that this is abrogated by the 'kill the polytheists' verse from the last revealed surah. The answer given is that reconciliation (jam') between the verses is possible, and when possible, it takes precedence over claiming abrogation. Both examples illustrate a common dialectical move in usul al-fiqh debates, where one party tries to nullify the opponent's proof by alleging naskh, and the defender responds by showing that the verses can be harmonized or that the naskh is limited.

نسخ
جمع

Checkpoint
In the second example of naskh objection, what is the Hanafi's claim and how does the defender respond?
A) The Hanafi claims the verse about fidyah is abrogated; the defender says it applies only to the elderly.
B) The Hanafi claims the verse about prisoners (mann/fida') is abrogated by the verse to kill polytheists; the defender says reconciliation is possible.
C) The Hanafi claims the verse about fasting is abrogated; the defender says it is still valid.
D) The Hanafi claims the verse about prisoners is abrogated; the defender admits it is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
When an objector claims naskh by a later verse, the defender can reconcile the verses by showing they apply to different contexts, thereby preserving the original proof.

Objection by Explicit Claim of Abrogation
Classifies and illustrates the objection by explicit abrogation claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection by explicit abrogation and construct a response that restricts the scope of the abrogation.

Core Idea
Abrogation objections must be met by restricting their scope to specific cases.

In dialectical debate (jadal), one powerful move is to claim that the opponent's proof text has been abrogated. But not every abrogation claim defeats the argument. In this lesson, we examine the first type of abrogation objection: the objector explicitly cites a later verse to abrogate the earlier one. Learn how the proponent can defend by narrowing the abrogation's application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's evidence
Find the verse the proponent uses to support his legal claim. In the example, it is the ransom verse (al-Baqarah 2:184) used to require fidyah for pregnant/nursing women who break fast.
The proponent is a Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali.
2
Identify the objector's abrogation claim
The Hanafi objector explicitly states that the ransom verse is abrogated by a later verse commanding fasting (al-Baqarah 2:185). This is a direct claim of naskh.
The objector must be explicit; implicit abrogation is another type.
3
Analyse the proponent's response
The proponent must show that the abrogation only applies to those who can fast without hardship, not to those with excuses like pregnancy or nursing. He provides evidence that such women have a choice between fasting and not fasting, so the ransom verse still applies to them.
This response restricts the scope of abrogation.
4
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
If the proponent successfully proves the restriction, the objection is rebutted. The abrogation claim does not defeat the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل
المبحث الخامس
الاعتراض بدعوى النسخ
ويقع الاعتراض بدعوى النسخ من أربعة أوجه:
المطلب الأول: أن ينقل المعترض النسخ صريحًا
ومثاله؛ أن يستدل المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي على إيجاب الفدية على الحامل والمرضع إذا أفطرتا في رمضان خوفًا على الجنين أو الرضيع، بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَلَى الَّذِينَ يُطِيقُونَهُ فِدْيَةٌ طَعَامُ مِسْكِينٍ ...﴾
فيعترض الحنفي بأن يقول: هذه الآية منسوخة بقوله عز وجل: ... فَمَن شَهدَ مِنكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْةُ ...
وهنا على المستدل أن يثبت أن النسخ إنما حصل في حق من كان منه الإفطار من حمل ولا رضاع، بدليل أنهما مخيرتان بين الصوم وعدمه، فتكون الآية متجهة إلى غيرهما.
The fifth chapter: Objection by claiming abrogation. The objection by claiming abrogation occurs from four aspects. The first sub-section: That the objector explicitly transmits the abrogation. An example is when the Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali argues for the obligation of ransom on a pregnant or nursing woman if they break fast in Ramadan out of fear for the fetus or infant, by His statement: 'And upon those who are able [to fast, but not able to do so], a ransom of feeding a poor person...' So the Hanafi objects by saying: This verse is abrogated by His statement: '...So whoever among you is present during the month, let him fast it...' Here it is incumbent upon the proponent to prove that the abrogation occurred only concerning the one who broke fast due to pregnancy or nursing, with evidence that they are given a choice between fasting and not fasting, so the verse is directed to others besides them.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is a classification and illustration from a work on jadal (dialectical argumentation). It opens by stating that the objection by claiming abrogation has four types, then details the first type: the objector explicitly claims abrogation of a specific verse by another. The example given is a dispute between a Malik/Shafi'i/Hanbali and a Hanafi. The first argues for the obligation of fidyah (ransom) for pregnant/nursing women who break fast, citing a Quranic verse about ransom. The Hanafi objector claims that verse is abrogated by the later verse commanding fasting for those present in Ramadan. The proponent must then respond by showing that the abrogation only applies to those who can fast without hardship, not to women with valid excuses, and that this is supported by evidence that such women have a choice. This illustrates the method of restricting the scope of abrogation in dialectical exchange.

An objection by explicit abrogation can be rebutted by proving that the abrogation's scope excludes the specific case under debate.

Refuting an Abrogation Claim with Reconciliation
Illustrates the objection where a later verse is claimed to abrogate an earlier one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute a claim of abrogation (naskh) by showing that reconciliation (jam’) between the two verses is possible.

Core Idea
Reconciliation (jam’) takes priority over abrogation (naskh) in dialectical reasoning.

In jadal, an objector may claim that a later revealed verse abrogates an earlier one. The respondent can counter by harmonizing the two texts, avoiding the need to invoke naskh. This lesson walks through a classic example involving prisoners of war, showing how the principle of jam’ is applied to preserve both rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim (mudda’i)
Locate the position the objector is challenging. In the example, a Maliki/Shafi’i/Hanbali argues that the verse ‘...then either set them free as a favour or for ransom’ allows mann (grace) or fida’ (ransom) for prisoners.
The original claim relies on the apparent generality of the verse.
2
Identify the objector’s claim (mu’tarid)
The Hanafi objector says this verse is abrogated by a later verse: ‘Kill the polytheists’ from Surah Bara’ah (the last revealed surah). This is a claim of naskh bi-aya muta’akhira.
The objector uses chronological priority to argue for abrogation.
3
Check if reconciliation (jam’) is possible
The respondent argues that both verses can be reconciled: one applies when the imam sees interest in killing, the other when he sees interest in grace/ransom. This removes the contradiction.
Jam’ is the preferred method in usul al-fiqh before resorting to naskh or tarjih.
4
Apply the dialectical rule against naskh
Since jam’ is possible, the abrogation claim fails. The rule: ‘as long as reconciliation is possible, it is not permissible to resort to abrogation.’
This is a key principle in jadal: textual conflict must first be resolved through harmonization.
5
Understand the reconciliation method
The reconciliation is based on circumstances or the imam’s discretion (maslahah). Each verse governs a different scenario, so both remain operative.
This shows that jam’ can be achieved by contextual specification (takhsis) or by conditional interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني: أن يدعي المعترض النسخ بآية متأخرة
من ذلك: استدلال المالكي أو الشافعي أو الحنبلي في جواز المن والفداء على أسرى المشركين بقوله تعالى: ... فَإِمَّا مَنَّنا بَعْدُ وَإِمَّا فِدَاءٌ ...
فيعترض الحنفي بدعوى أن هذه الآية منسوخة بآية أخرى متأخرة عنها، وهي قوله تعالى: ﴿... فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ ...﴾ من سورة براءة التي هي آخر سورة نزلت.
والجواب على هذا الاعتراض: أن الجمع بين الآيتين ممكن، وما دام الجمع ممكنا فلا يجوز أن نصير إلى النسخ، ويتم الجمع بين هاتين الآيتين، بأن تحمل إحداهما على قتلهم في حالة ما إذا رأى الإمام المصلحة في ذلك، وأن تحمل الأخرى على المن عليهم وقبول الفداء منهم إذا ما رأى الإمام المصلحة في ذلك أيضا.
The second sub-section: That the objector claims abrogation by a later verse. An example is the argument of the Maliki, Shafi'i, or Hanbali regarding the permissibility of mann (grace) and fida' (ransom) on polytheist prisoners by His statement: '...Then either set them free as a favour or for ransom...' So the Hanafi objects by claiming that this verse is abrogated by another later verse, namely His statement: '...Kill the polytheists...' from Surat Bara'ah, which was the last revealed surah. The answer to this objection is that reconciliation between the two verses is possible, and as long as reconciliation is possible, it is not permissible to resort to abrogation. The reconciliation between these two verses is achieved by interpreting one as pertaining to killing them when the imam sees the interest in that, and interpreting the other as pertaining to showing grace to them and accepting ransom from them if the imam also sees the interest in that.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of objection by claim of abrogation: the objector argues that a later verse abrogates an earlier one. The example involves a dispute over the treatment of polytheist prisoners: a Maliki/Shafi'i/Hanbali cites a verse allowing either favor or ransom, while the Hanafi objector claims this verse is abrogated by the later command 'Kill the polytheists' from Surah Tawbah (Bara'ah). The response is a dialectical principle: if the two verses can be reconciled (jamaʿ), abrogation is not allowed. The reconciliation is explained by suggesting that each ruling applies in different circumstances based on the imam's discretion regarding public interest (maslahah). This illustrates the method of harmonization (jamʿ) as a defense against abrogation claims.

Key Terms
المنّ والفداء
النسخ بآية متأخرة
الجمع

B) The two verses can be reconciled, so abrogation is not needed.
C) The earlier verse is abrogated and the objector is correct.
D) The Hanafi must prove the verses are contradictory.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic dialectic, the principle of jam’ (reconciliation) prevents premature resort to naskh when two texts can be harmonized.

Responding to an Objection of Abrogation from Previous Law
Author presents a dialectical exchange: objection claiming a verse is from abrogated previous law, with three rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct three rebuttals to an objection that a verse belongs to abrogated previous law.

Core Idea
Three rebuttals defeat the claim that a verse is abrogated from previous law.

In usul al-fiqh debates, an opponent may argue that a verse cited as evidence is from the law of a previous prophet and thus abrogated. This lesson examines a classic example from a Maliki-Hanafi exchange and presents three rebuttals. You will learn how to counter such objections using textual universality, the principle of continuity of previous laws, and prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (i'tirad)
Find the Hanafi's claim: the verse was addressed to Moses, so it is not part of Islamic law. This is the objection to be rebutted.
2
Extract the first rebuttal (universality)
The Maliki argues that the verse's address is universal, not limited to Moses. Check if the verse's context supports general application.
3
Extract the second rebuttal (principle of continuity)
Invoke the usuli principle that laws of previous prophets remain valid unless abrogated. This shifts the burden of proof to the objector.
This principle is accepted by many madhabs, but not all; its acceptance is a key point in the dialectic.
4
Extract the third rebuttal (prophetic precedent)
Show that the Prophet himself cited the same verse to rule on missed prayers. This demonstrates the verse is still operative (muhkamah) in Islamic law.
5
Evaluate the dialectical strength
Determine which rebuttal is strongest based on evidence: the third rebuttal uses Sunnah directly, making it hard to refute. The first requires linguistic analysis; the second relies on accepting a foundational principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثالث: أن يدعي أن مضمون الآية من شرع من قبلنا وأن شرعنا قد نسخها ومثاله: أن يستدل المالكي على وجوب قضاء الفوائت في الأوقات المنهي عن الصلاة فيها بقوله عز وجل: هو ... وَأَقِمِ الصَّلَاةَ لِذِكْرِي، فيقول الحنفي معترضا: هذا ليس بشرع لنا لأنه خوطب به موسى عليه السلام، وقد نسخت شريعته. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض من وجوه: أحدها: أن يبين المستدل - إن أمكنه - أن ذلك ليس بمتوجه إلى موسى وحده، بل هو متوجه إلى جميع العباد. والثاني: أن يقول: إن شرع من قبلنا شرع لنا ما لم يثبت نسخه، وهذا أصل عندنا ونحن نبني عليه فروعنا. والثالث: أن يبين أن هذه الآية محكمة في شرعنا معمول بها، والدليل على ذلك ما روي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: (من نسي صلاة فليصلها إذا ذكرها لا كفارة لها إلا ذلك، فإن الله تعالى يقول: وَأَقِمِ الصَّلَاةَ لِذِكْرِي)، فجعلها حجة ودليلا على قضاء الصلاة عند الذكر، وهذا دليل على أنها محكمة، ولو كانت منسوخة لم يكن لذكرها في هذا الموضع فائدة.
The third demand: That he claims that the content of the verse is from the law of those before us and that our law has abrogated it. Its example: That the Maliki argues for the obligation of making up missed prayers during the prohibited times for prayer by His statement, Exalted and Majestic: '... and establish prayer for My remembrance.' So the Hanafi objects saying: This is not a law for us because it was addressed to Moses, peace be upon him, and his law has been abrogated. The answer to this objection is from several aspects: First: That the arguer should show – if he can – that this is not directed to Moses alone, but rather is directed to all servants. Second: That he says: The law of those before us is a law for us as long as its abrogation has not been established, and this is a principle with us and we build our branches upon it. Third: That he shows that this verse is firmly established in our law, acted upon, and the evidence for that is what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Whoever forgets a prayer, let him pray it when he remembers it; there is no expiation for it except that, for Allah the Exalted says: Establish prayer for My remembrance.' So he made it a proof and evidence for making up prayer upon remembrance, and this is evidence that it is firmly established, and had it been abrogated, its mention in this context would have no benefit.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange from a work on jadal (disputation) in usul al-fiqh. It presents a type of objection (i'tirad) where an opponent argues that a verse cited as evidence belongs to the law of a previous prophet (shar' man qablana) and is therefore abrogated by the shari'ah of Islam. The author provides an example: the Maliki jurist uses the verse from Surah Taha (20:14) to argue that missed prayers must be made up even during prohibited times, because the verse commands prayer upon remembrance. The Hanafi objects that this verse was addressed to Moses, hence it is not part of Islamic law. The author then gives three possible rebuttals. The first rebuttal: show that the verse's address is universal, not limited to Moses. The second: invoke the principle that the laws of previous prophets remain valid for us unless abrogated—a foundational principle in usul. The third: demonstrate that the verse is still operative in Islam, as the Prophet himself cited it when ruling on the missed prayer, proving it is not abrogated. The passage illustrates how dialectical strategy involves both substantive legal principles (like the continuity of previous laws) and textual evidence from the Sunnah.

Key Terms
شرع من قبلنا
نسخ
محكمة

Checkpoint

B) Invoking the principle that previous laws remain binding unless abrogated.
C) Demonstrating that the Prophet cited the verse for the same ruling.
D) Arguing that the Hanafi has misunderstood the verse's context.

Key Takeaway
Three rebuttals—universal address, continuity of previous laws, and prophetic precedent—can counter an objection that a verse is abrogated from earlier revelation.

Imagine an objector says it is from David's law and abrogated. Draft two rebuttals: one using the principle of continuity, and one showing that the Prophet or early Muslims used it.

The Qisas Example: Objecting that a Verse is from Abrogated Law and Its Rebuttal
Second example: dialectical exchange over qisas verse and its abrogation status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical exchange regarding the qisas verse and evaluate the strength of the rebuttal using the principle of shar'u man qablana.

Core Idea
A verse from a previous scripture remains binding unless explicit Islamic scripture nullifies it.

In this second example, we see the same objection applied to the qisas verse. The Hanafi claims the verse is from the Torah and thus abrogated. The proponent counters by reaffirming the principle that previous laws are binding unless abrogated, and provides prophetic practice as evidence. This lesson walks you through the argument structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions
Determine who the proponent (Shafi'i or Hanbali) and opponent (Hanafi) are. What is each claiming about the qisas verse?
The proponent uses the verse to argue for qisas equality between man and woman; the opponent objects that the verse is from the Torah and abrogated.
2
Extract the objection
The Hanafi argues that because the verse is introduced by 'We prescribed for them therein' (referring to the Torah), it is a report of Torah law, and the Torah is abrogated by the Quran. Therefore, the verse is no longer binding.
This objection relies on the premise that abrogation of the scripture cancels all its rulings.
3
Examine the rebuttal
The rebuttal reaffirms the usuli principle: laws of previous prophets (shar'u man qablana) are binding on us unless explicitly nullified in Islamic sources. Then it cites prophetic practice: the Prophet ruled on a tooth-pulling case using this same verse, proving it remains operative.
The rebuttal shifts from the verse's origin to its continued application by the Prophet.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
Evaluate whether the rebuttal successfully neutralizes the objection. It does so by providing direct evidence (prophetic action) that the verse is still in force, and by invoking a well-established principle.
The objection fails because it assumes automatic abrogation, while the rebuttal shows continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله كذلك: أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنبلي في إيجاب القصاص في الطرف بين الرجل والمرأة بقوله تعالى: ... وَالْجُرُوحَ قِصَاصٌ ... فيعترض الحنفي بأن هذا إخبار عن حكم من أحكام التوراة، إذ صدرت الآية بقوله تعالى: وَكَتَبْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ فِيهَا ... إلى قوله: ... وَالْجُرُوحَ قِصَاصٌ، والتوراة منسوخة بالقرآن كما هو معلوم. والجواب: أن يثبت المستدل أن شرع من قبلنا هو شرع لنا ما لم يرد في شريعتنا ما ينص صراحة على إلغائه، والدليل على أن شريعة القصاص باقية أيضًا في شريعتنا أن امرأة قلعت سن امرأة أخرى، فقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: (كتاب الله: القصاص ...) مشيرا إلى هذه الآية.
And likewise its example: That the Shafi'i or Hanbali argues for the obligation of retribution in (bodily) parts between a man and a woman by His statement, Exalted and Majestic: '... and wounds are retribution ...' So the Hanafi objects that this is a report of a ruling from the rulings of the Torah, since the verse begins with His statement: 'And We prescribed for them therein ...' up to His statement: '... and wounds are retribution,' and the Torah is abrogated by the Qur'an as is known. The answer: That the arguer establishes that the law of those before us is a law for us as long as there does not come in our law something that explicitly stipulates its nullification, and the evidence that the law of retribution remains also in our law is that a woman pulled out the tooth of another woman, so the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'The Book of Allah: retribution ...' pointing to this verse.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage continues the same dialectical theme from the previous lesson. The author provides a second example of the objection that a verse is from the law of a previous prophet and thus abrogated. Here, the Shafi'i or Hanbali relies on the verse about qisas (retribution) in Surah al-Ma'idah (5:45) to argue that a man and a woman are equal in qisas for bodily injuries. The Hanafi objects that this verse is part of the Torah (because the preceding verse says 'We prescribed for them therein'), and the Torah is abrogated by the Qur'an. The rebuttal is simpler: reaffirm the principle that the laws of previous prophets are binding unless explicitly nullified, and then cite evidence that the Prophet himself applied this rule in a case of a tooth-pulling, referencing the same verse. This shows the verse remains operative. The passage thus reinforces a key dialectical move: grounding an argument in established usul principles combined with prophetic practice.

D) The Hanafi agrees to the principle.

Key Takeaway
The qisas verse is not automatically abrogated despite its Torah context; it remains valid unless explicitly nullified, as demonstrated by prophetic practice.

Partial Abrogation Does Not Entail Total Abrogation
Author presents the fourth objection type: claiming universal abrogation from partial abrogation, with example and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the claimant's position, the objection, and the two responses in a jadal exchange about partial vs. total abrogation, and evaluate the strength of each response.

Core Idea
Partial abrogation does not entail total abrogation unless supported by independent evidence.

In jadal debates, it is common for an opponent to claim that if part of a verse is abrogated, the whole verse must be abrogated. This lesson examines that fallacy through a specific example from usul al-fiqh, where the Malikis use a verse about dower to argue that a free person's labor can be a dower. The objector says the verse is abrogated because it gives the dower to the guardian, which is no longer valid. The response offers two lines of defense: one interpretive, the other logical. By the end, you will see how careful distinction between part and whole protects a proof from overreach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's argument (mudda'i)
The Malikis claim that a free person's benefit (labor) can be a dower, citing Quran 28:27 where Prophet Shu'ayb offers his daughter in marriage on condition that Prophet Musa works for him. This implies that beneficial labor is valid as a dower.
The claimant uses the verse as a proof for a ruling in fiqh.
2
Identify the objector's challenge (mu'tarid)
The objector argues that the verse is abrogated because it gives the dower to the guardian (the father), whereas in Islamic law the dower is given to the woman, not her guardian. Therefore, the whole verse is abrogated and cannot be used as evidence.
The objector assumes that abrogation of one ruling abrogates the entire verse.
3
Analyze the first response (jawab 1)
The first response denies abrogation altogether: the verse can be interpreted as giving the dower to the father by right of guardianship, which was customary. As long as such an interpretation is possible, the claim of abrogation is not allowed.
This response avoids abrogation by providing a plausible non-abrogated reading.
4
Analyze the second response (jawab 2)
Even if the part about the guardian is abrogated, the rest of the verse remains valid unless there is specific evidence (dalil) proving that the entire verse is abrogated. Since no such evidence exists, the abrogation claim is insufficient.
This response concedes partial abrogation but denies it extends to the whole.
5
Evaluate the debate outcome
The claimant successfully defends the proof by showing that the objector's objection is insufficient. The objector would need to produce evidence that the abrogation covers all rulings in the verse, which they have not.
By jadal rules, the claimant's response is valid unless the objector can counter it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الرابع : أن يدعي نسخ جميع أحكام الآية بنسخ حكم من أحكامها ومثاله : استدلال بعض المالكية على أن منفعة الحر يجوز أن تكون صداقا، بقوله عز وجل : قَالَ إنّي أُرِيدُ أَنْ أُنكِحَكَ إحْدَى ابْنَى هَنتَيْنِ عَلَى أَن تَأْجُرَنِى ثَمَنى ، إذ تدل الآية على أن رعي الغنم، وهو منفعة، يجوز أن يُعتبر صداقا. واعترض عليه بأن هذه الآية منسوخة، بدليل أنه جعل الصداق في الآية للولي، ولا خلاف أن ذلك منسوخ ، إذ يُجعل الصداق للمرأة لا لوليها. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض يكون من أحد وجهين : أحدهما : أن يقول المستدل : ليس في الآية شيء منسوخ، لأنه يجوز أن يكون قد أضاف الصداق إلى أبيها بحق الولاية، وقد جرى استعماله عرفا، وما دام الاستعمال ممكنا لم يجز دعوى النسخ. والثاني : أنه لو صح : أنه لو صح أن ذلك منسوخ ، لم يجب أن ينسخ جميع ما في الآية من الأحكام إلا بدليل، وهو غير موجود، فلم تجز دعوى النسخ لذلك (1)
The fourth point: to claim abrogation of all rulings of a verse by abrogation of one of its rulings. Its example: some Malikis argue that the benefit of a free person can be a dower, by His statement: 'He said, "Indeed I wish to marry you one of these two daughters of mine on condition that you serve me for eight years." ' Indeed the verse indicates that shepherding sheep, which is a benefit, can be considered a dower. And it was objected that this verse is abrogated, evidenced by the fact that the dower in the verse is given to the guardian, and there is no disagreement that that is abrogated, since the dower is given to the woman, not her guardian. The answer to this objection is from two sides: One: that the arguer says: There is nothing abrogated in the verse, because it is possible that he attributed the dower to her father by right of guardianship, and this usage has occurred customarily, and as long as usage is possible, the claim of abrogation is not permitted. The second: that if it were correct that that is abrogated, it would not be necessary that all rulings in the verse are abrogated except by evidence, and it does not exist, so the claim of abrogation is not permitted.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth type of objection: claiming that abrogation of a single ruling in a verse entails abrogation of the entire verse. The example is a Maliki argument that a free person's labor can be a dower, based on the story of Prophet Musa and Shu'ayb. The objector says the verse is abrogated because it gives the dower to the guardian, which is no longer valid. The answer offers two responses: first, that the verse can be interpreted as giving the dower to the father by guardianship, so no abrogation needed; second, that even if that part is abrogated, it does not abrogate the entire verse without specific evidence.

دليل

B) Because abrogation of one ruling does not abrogate the whole verse without separate evidence.
C) Because the objector did not provide a valid alternative proof.
D) Because the Malikis reject the concept of abrogation.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation of a specific ruling in a verse does not automatically abrogate the entire verse; each ruling must be individually abrogated with evidence.

Two Objections Against Claims of Abrogation
Author presents two dialectical objections against claims of abrogation in a verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the structure of a dialectical objection to a claim of abrogation in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Abrogation must be proven beyond all valid interpretations.

This passage from a jadal manual teaches the rigorous standards required to claim abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law. The interlocutor (mustadill) presents two objections that force the opponent to provide definitive proof. The first objection invokes the principle that a plausible interpretation prevents abrogation. The second insists that each part of a verse requires separate evidence. Understanding these objections equips you to evaluate and construct arguments in usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's position and the opponent's stance
The passage does not explicitly state the claimant's position, but we infer that someone claims a verse has been abrogated. The mustadill opposes this claim. Note the phrase 'the interlocutor says: There is nothing abrogated in the verse.' This sets the stage for the objections.
In jadal, the mustadill is the one who brings evidence; here he defends the non-abrogation.
2
Extract the first objection
The first objection argues that a non-abrogating interpretation is possible: 'because it is possible that he added the dowry to her father by right of guardianship, and this usage has become customary.' The key principle is 'as long as interpretation is possible, the claim of abrogation is not permissible.'
This objection relies on the usuli principle of al-istiqrar (continuity).
3
Extract the second objection
The second objection uses a conditional: 'even if it were correct that that is abrogated, it would not be necessary to abrogate all the rulings in the verse except with evidence.' This shifts the burden to the claimant to prove each abrogation separately.
The phrase 'and such evidence does not exist' is the premise that the opponent lacks proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : أن يقول المستدل : ليس في الآية شيء منسوخ، لأنه يجوز أن يكون قد أضاف الصداق إلى أبيها بحق الولاية، وقد جرى استعماله عرفا، وما دام الاستعمال ممكنا لم يجز دعوى النسخ. والثاني : أنه لو صح أن ذلك منسوخ، لم يجب أن ينسخ جميع ما في الآية من الأحكام إلا بدليل، وهو غير موجود، فلم تجز دعوى النسخ لذلك
First: the interlocutor says: There is nothing abrogated in the verse, because it is possible that he added the dowry to her father by right of guardianship, and this usage has become customary, and as long as interpretation is possible, the claim of abrogation is not permissible. Second: even if it were correct that that is abrogated, it would not be necessary to abrogate all the rulings in the verse except with evidence, and such evidence does not exist, so the claim of abrogation is not permissible for that reason.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents two typical objections used in jadal against a claim of abrogation (naskh). The first objection argues that the verse can be interpreted in a way that does not require abrogation; as long as a valid interpretation exists, abrogation cannot be claimed. This is a common principle in usul al-fiqh: 'al-istiqrar al-isti'mal' (continuity of usage). The second objection argues that even if some part of the verse is abrogated, it does not entail abrogation of all rulings in it; each ruling requires separate evidence for abrogation. Since no such evidence is present, the claim is invalid. Both objections rely on the principle that abrogation is a serious claim that must be proven definitively.

Key Terms
نسخ
مستدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the verse is clear and unambiguous
B) Because a possible interpretation exists that avoids abrogation
C) Because the abrogation is proven by consensus
D) Because the verse is not part of the Quran

Key Takeaway
A claim of abrogation cannot succeed if a plausible non-abrogative interpretation exists, and each ruling requires separate evidence.
