Objection by Interpretation of Apparent Meaning
Author defines objection by interpretation (ta'wil) with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection by interpretation (ta'wil) in a juristic debate and explain why it is valid only if the alternative meaning is plausible and supported by evidence.

Core Idea
Objection by interpretation turns the opponent's own text against him by offering a plausible alternative meaning.

In dialectical debate, a powerful way to counter an opponent's evidence is not to deny it outright, but to accept it while offering a different plausible interpretation that undermines their conclusion. This technique, called ta'wil, requires the objector to provide evidence that the text can bear the alternative meaning. Let's examine how it works through classical examples from fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent meaning (zahir)
Read the text (verse or hadith) that the original debater (mustadill) uses. Determine its most obvious, literal meaning based on linguistic usage, custom, or context.
In the first example: the verse 'And give them from the wealth of Allah...' apparently commands giving, implying obligation.
2
Find the alternative interpretation
Look at what alternative meaning the objector (mu'tarid) proposes. The alternative must be a possible meaning that the wording can bear, not a forced or impossible reading.
The Hanafi objector interprets 'wealth of Allah' as zakat wealth, or the command as recommendation, not obligation.
3
Check the plausibility of the alternative
Assess whether the alternative is linguistically or contextually plausible. The objector often provides a clue (qarinah) that supports his interpretation.
The Hanafi notes that the phrase 'wealth of Allah' is used elsewhere for zakat, and he offers a reason for the recommendation interpretation.
4
Verify that the objector provides evidence
A valid ta'wil objection requires the objector to present a separate evidence (dalil) for his interpretation, not just assert it. Otherwise, it is mere speculation.
The author says 'but with evidence that the objector mentions' – this is crucial for the objection to be dialectically sound.
5
Evaluate the effect on the original argument
If the alternative interpretation is plausible and supported, the opponent's inference loses certainty. The burden shifts back to the original debater to defend his apparent meaning.
In the first example, the Shafi'i's claim of obligation is weakened; the debate continues with further evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس: الاعتراض من جهة التأويل. وهو نوعان: تأويل الظاهر، وتخصيص العموم. المطلب الأول: تأويل الظاهر. تأويل الظاهر معناه: صرف الكلام عن ظاهره إلى وجه يحتمله، ومعنى ذلك أن يكون الكلام يحتمل معنيين فزائدا إلا أن أحدهما أظهر في ذلك اللفظ إما لوضع أو استعمال أو عرف. وعليه فإن الاعتراض بتأويل الظاهر يكون بأن يحمل المعترض الآية على معنى آخر تحتمله غير المعنى الذي حملها عليه المستدل. مثاله؛ استدلال الشافعي أو الحنبلي في إيجاب الإيتاء في الكتابة، بقوله تعالى: (وآتوهم من مال الله الذي آتاكم). فيعترض الحنفي بأن يقول: إنما أراد به مال الزكاة ويشهد لذلك إضافته إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وقد يكون المراد حمل الغني على الإيتاء بطريق الاستحباب. لكن بدليل يذكره المعترض. ومثاله كذلك استدلال الحنفي في إيجاب غسل الثوب من المني، بما رواه علقمة والأسود أن رجلا نزل بعائشة فأصبح يغسل ثوبه، فقالت عائشة: «إنما كان يجزئك إن رأيته أن تغسل مكانه، فإن لم تر نضحت حوله، ولقد رأيتني أفركه من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فركا فيصلي فيه». فيعترض الشافعي على هذا الاستدلال بمنع إفادة الوجوب، بحمله على الاستحباب بدليل.
Sixth Topic: Objection from the Perspective of Interpretation. It is of two types: interpretation of the apparent meaning, and specification of the general. First Section: Interpretation of the Apparent Meaning. The meaning of interpretation of the apparent meaning is: turning the speech away from its apparent meaning to a meaning it can bear. And the meaning of that is that the speech can bear two or more meanings, except that one of them is more apparent in that wording, either due to linguistic usage, conventional usage, or customary practice. Accordingly, the objection by interpretation of the apparent meaning is that the objector carries the verse to another meaning it can bear, different from the meaning the interlocutor carried it to. Its example: the argument of al-Shafi'i or al-Hanbali regarding the obligation of giving in the contract of kitaba, by His statement: 'And give them from the wealth of Allah which He has given you.' So the Hanafi objects by saying: He only meant by it the wealth of zakat, and what testifies to that is its attribution to Allah the Exalted, and it may be that the intended meaning is to make the rich give by way of recommendation, but with evidence that the objector mentions. Another example of that is the argument of the Hanafi regarding the obligation of washing the garment from semen, by what 'Alqama and al-Aswad narrated that a man stayed with 'Aisha and in the morning he washed his garment, so 'Aisha said: 'It would have sufficed you, if you saw it, to wash its spot, and if you did not see, you would sprinkle around it, and I indeed used to rub it from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) by rubbing, and he would pray in it.' So al-Shafi'i objects to this argument by preventing the indication of obligation, by carrying it to recommendation with evidence.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines a key dialectical technique: objection by interpretation (tawil). The author first states that this objection is of two types: interpretation of the apparent meaning and specification of the general. The focus here is on the first type. The definition: the objector takes the opponent's evidence and interprets it in a different way that is still linguistically possible, thereby undermining the opponent's inference. The key is that the interpretation must be plausible (the word can bear that meaning), and it must be supported by evidence. The author provides two concrete examples from Islamic law. The first is the dispute between the Shafi'i/Hanbali and Hanafi schools regarding the obligation of giving to slaves writing a contract of emancipation (kitaba). The Shafi'i uses the verse 'And give them from the wealth of Allah...' to argue obligation. The Hanafi objects by interpreting 'wealth of Allah' as zakat wealth, or by interpreting the command as recommendation, not obligation. This is a classic example of tawil. The second example is the Hanafi's argument that a garment must be washed if it has semen, using the hadith of Aisha. The Shafi'i objects by interpreting Aisha's statement as indicating recommendation, not obligation. The objector must provide a separate evidence (dalil) for his interpretation. This passage illustrates how jadal works: the objector does not simply deny the evidence but offers a plausible alternative interpretation.

Key Terms
تأويل
ظاهر
تخصيص العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of ta'wil, what alternative interpretation does the Hanafi offer for the verse 'And give them from the wealth of Allah...'?
A) It refers to the wealth of the slave himself
B) It refers to the wealth of the state treasury
C) It refers to zakat wealth
D) It refers to the wealth of the guardian

Key Takeaway
Objection by interpretation (ta'wil) is a dialectical tool that challenges an opponent's inference by offering a plausible alternative meaning of the same text, provided it is supported by evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ta'wil al-Zahir: Definition and Kitaba Example
Defines Ta'wil al-Zahir with first example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a Ta'wil al-Zahir objection and evaluate its validity using the first fiqh example (kitaba).

Core Idea
Ta'wil al-Zahir challenges certainty of the apparent meaning.

In usul al-fiqh debates, a Ta'wil al-Zahir objection does not reject the text but offers an alternative plausible meaning, undermining the opponent's claim. This lesson dissects the first example: the Hanafi interpretation of the verse on kitaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's claim
The Shafi'i/Hanbali argues that the verse (24:33) commands giving from Allah's wealth, implying obligation in the kitaba contract.
2
Find the apparent meaning (zahir)
The apparent meaning is that the command is unconditional, indicating obligation.
3
Locate the objector's alternative interpretation
The Hanafi objector says the verse means zakat wealth, not general wealth, and the command is recommendatory.
The objector supports this by the attribution to Allah, suggesting special funds.
4
Assess the objector's proof
The objector provides a linguistic clue: 'wealth of Allah' often refers to zakat in Qur'anic usage.
5
Evaluate the objection's force
The alternative is plausible, so the apparent meaning loses its certainty. The objection is valid if the alternative is supported.

المبحث السادس: الاعتراض من جهة التأويل وهو نوعان : تأويل الظاهر، وتخصيص العموم. المطلب الأول : تأويل الظاهر. تأويل الظاهر معناه : صرف الكلام عن ظاهره إلى وجه يحتمله، ومعنى ذلك أن يكون الكلام يحتمل معنيين فزائدا إلا أن أحدهما أظهر في ذلك اللفظ إما لوضع أو استعمال أو عرف. وعليه فإن الاعتراض بتأويل الظاهر يكون بأن يحمل المعترض الآية على معنى آخر تحتمله غير المعنى الذي حملها عليه المستدل. مثاله؛ استدلال الشافعي أو الحنبلي في إيجاب الإيتاء في الكتابة، بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَآتُوهُم مِّن مَّالِ اللَّهِ الَّذِي آتَاكُمْ﴾. فيعترض الحنفي بأن يقول : إنما أراد به مال الزكاة ويشهد لذلك إضافته إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وقد يكون المراد حمل الغني على الإيتاء بطريق الاستحباب، لكن بدليل يذكره المعترض. ومثاله كذلك استدلال الحنفي في إيجاب غسل الثوب من المني، بما رواه علقمة والأسود أن رجلا نزل بعائشة فأصبح يغسل ثوبه، فقالت عائشة: «إنما كان يجزئك إن رأيته أن تغسل مكانه، فإن لم تر نضحت حوله، ولقد رأيتني أفركه من ثوب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فركا فيصلي فيه». فيعترض الشافعي على هذا الاستدلال بمنع إفادة الوجوب، بحمله على الاستحباب بدليل.
The sixth research topic: Objection from the perspective of interpretation. It is of two types: interpretation of the apparent meaning, and specification of the general. The first demand: Interpretation of the apparent meaning. The meaning of interpretation of the apparent meaning is: diverting speech from its apparent meaning to a meaning it can bear. That is, the speech can bear two or more meanings, except that one of them is more apparent in that wording either due to linguistic usage, common use, or custom. Therefore, the objection by interpretation of the apparent meaning is that the objector carries the verse to another meaning it can bear, different from the meaning to which the arguer carried it. Example of it: The argument of al-Shafi'i or al-Hanbali regarding the obligation of giving in the contract of kitaba (manumission by contract), by His saying the Exalted: 'And give them from the wealth of Allah which He has given you.' So the Hanafi objects by saying: He only meant by it the wealth of zakat, and this is supported by its attribution to Allah the Exalted. And it may be intended to encourage the rich to give by way of recommendation, but with a proof that the objector mentions. Another example: The argument of the Hanafi regarding the obligation of washing the garment from seminal fluid, based on what 'Alqamah and al-Aswad narrated: A man stayed with 'A'isha and in the morning he was washing his garment. 'A'isha said: 'It would have been sufficient for you if you saw it to wash that spot, and if you did not see it, you could sprinkle water around it. And I used to rub it from the garment of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and he would pray in it.' So al-Shafi'i objects to this argument by denying that it indicates obligation, carrying it to recommendation with a proof.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of objection from interpretation: Ta'wil al-Zahir (interpretation of the apparent meaning). The author clarifies that interpretation involves turning the apparent meaning of a text to another valid meaning it can bear, even if that other meaning is less apparent. The essence of this objection is that the objector claims the arguer's interpretation is not the only possible one; rather, the text can support another meaning. The author provides two examples from fiqh. First, the Shafi'i/Hanbali argue that the Qur'anic verse (24:33) commands giving from Allah's wealth, implying obligation in kitaba. The Hanafi objects by interpreting 'wealth of Allah' as zakat funds, not general wealth, and also suggests it is recommendatory rather than obligatory. Second, the Hanafi argues that washing the entire garment from semen is obligatory based on 'A'isha's practice. The Shafi'i objects by interpreting her statement as showing recommendation, not obligation, as she said 'it would have been sufficient' and described her own action as rubbing, not washing. Both objections rely on offering an alternative plausible meaning (recommendation) supported by some evidence. The student should note that the objection does not reject the text but challenges the claimed certainty of the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
تأويل الظاهر
اعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of Ta'wil al-Zahir, what alternative interpretation does the Hanafi objector offer for the verse on giving in kitaba?
A) It refers to obligatory zakat, not voluntary giving.
B) It is abrogated by another verse.
C) It applies only to men, not women.
D) It is a command that was specific to the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil al-Zahir is a valid objection that shifts the apparent meaning to a less apparent but plausible alternative, reducing the certainty of the arguer's proof.

Ta'wil al-Zahir: The Seminal Fluid Example
Second example of Ta'wil al-Zahir on seminal fluid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a Ta'wil al-Zahir objection in a hadith-based argument and evaluate the objector's alternative interpretation.

Core Idea
An action described in a hadith can be interpreted as recommendation, not obligation.

The second example demonstrates Ta'wil al-Zahir on a prophetic practice. The Hanafi uses 'A'isha's action to argue that washing the garment from semen is obligatory, but the Shafi'i objects by interpreting her statement as showing recommendation. This lesson dissects that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi's claim
The Hanafi argues that washing the entire garment from semen is obligatory, based on the man washing his garment and 'A'isha's statement about rubbing.
2
Find the apparent meaning (zahir) of the evidence
The apparent meaning is that the man's washing and 'A'isha's rubbing indicate the impurity must be removed by washing.
3
Locate the Shafi'i's alternative interpretation
The Shafi'i says 'A'isha's statement 'it would have been sufficient' shows the washing is recommended, not obligatory, and her rubbing demonstrates a permissible method.
4
Assess the objector's proof
The Shafi'i points to the permissive language ('it would have been sufficient') and 'A'isha's own practice of rubbing, which is less than washing.
5
Evaluate the objection's force
The alternative interpretation is plausible because 'A'isha did not command washing; she described a sufficient practice. The obligation claim weakens.

Responding to the Objection of Specification
Defines specification objection and gives examples with response strategy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection by specification of the general (takhṣīṣ al-ʿumūm) in a dialectical exchange and apply the correct response strategy.

Core Idea
The objection of specification shifts the burden to the objector's evidence, not the original proof.

This passage introduces a key move in jadal: the objection by takhṣīṣ al-ʿumūm (specification of the general). In usul al-fiqh, a general term covers all its individuals unless a separate proof restricts it. In dialectics, the objector may claim that the arguer's general proof is actually specified to a subset. The proper response is not to reject the specification outright but to scrutinize the objector's evidence. Let's walk through the mechanics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general term
Find the word or phrase in the arguer's proof that is claimed to be general (ʿāmm). For example, 'kill the polytheists' is general for all polytheists.
2
Locate the objector's specification claim
The objector asserts that the general term applies only to a subset, e.g., 'it is specified to exclude the elderly' or 'it applies only to men.'
The objector must provide a separate proof (dalīl) for this restriction.
3
Extract the objector's specifying proof
Look for the evidence the objector adduces for the specification. In the examples, a Hanafi might cite a sunna or a verse.
Without proof, the objection is invalid.
4
Examine the validity of the specifying proof
Does the objector's proof qualify as a valid mukhassis (specifier) in usul? Check its authenticity, strength, and relevance.
If the proof is weak (e.g., a solitary hadith contradicting a mutawātir), it may be rejected.
5
Formulate the arguer's response
If the specifying proof is invalid, refute it directly. If valid, engage it as a separate proof, and after defeating it, the original general proof stands unchallenged.
The response 'tekellam ʿalā al-dalīl' means to discuss that evidence as an independent argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : تخصيص العموم. التخصيص عند الأصوليين : إخراج بعض ما يتناوله اللفظ. والاعتراض بتخصيص العموم يكون بأن يستدل المستدل بعموم آية، فيحملها المعترض على بعض أفرادها فحسب، دون بقية الأفراد. مثاله ؛ استدلال الشافعي في قتل شيوخ المشركين بقوله عز وجل: ﴿فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ﴾. فيقول الحنفي معترضا هي مخصوصة في الشيوخ بدليل. وهنا لا جواب للمستدل إلا النظر في الدليل هل يصلح للتخصيص أو لا؟ فإن لم يصلح رده، وإن صلح تكلم عليه كلامه على الأدلة المبتدأة، فإذا تكلم عليه سلم له الظاهر والعموم أيضًا. ومنه أن يستدل الشافعي في قتل المرتدة بعموم قوله : (من بدل دينه فاقتلوه). فيعترضه الحنفي بأنه خاص بالرجال دون النساء، بدليل. والجواب : أن يتكلم الشافعي على الدليل الذي تأول به أو خص به ليسلم له الظاهر والعموم.
The second demand: Specification of the general. Specification according to the usul scholars is: removing some of what the word includes. And the objection by specification of the general is that the arguer uses the generality of a verse, so the objector carries it only to some of its individuals, excluding the rest. Example: The argument of al-Shafi'i regarding the killing of the elderly polytheists by His saying the Exalted: 'So kill the polytheists.' So the Hanafi says objecting: It is specified regarding the elderly with a proof. Here the arguer has no response except to examine the proof: does it qualify for specification or not? If it does not qualify, he refutes it; and if it qualifies, he discusses it as he discusses initial proofs. Then when he discusses it, the apparent meaning and generality are conceded to him. And from it is that al-Shafi'i argues for killing the apostate woman by the generality of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' So the Hanafi objects by saying it is specific to men excluding women, with a proof. The response: al-Shafi'i should discuss the proof by which he objected or specified to secure the apparent meaning and generality.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of objection from interpretation: Takhsis al-'Umum (specification of the general). The author first defines specification in usul al-fiqh as excluding some individuals from the scope of a general word. The objection arises when the arguer cites a general text, and the objector claims it applies only to a subset, not all. The author gives two examples. First, the Shafi'i argues that the verse 'kill the polytheists' (9:5) is general, including elderly polytheists. The Hanafi objects that the verse is specified to exclude the elderly (since the Prophet forbade killing old men in some narrations). The author then explains the proper response: the arguer must examine the objector's proof for specification. If the proof is invalid, he rejects it; if valid, he engages it as a separate proof. After addressing it, the original general meaning stands. The second example: the Shafi'i uses the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him' to argue that an apostate woman should be killed. The Hanafi objects that the hadith is specific to men, with a proof (e.g., the Sunnah indicates women apostates are imprisoned, not killed). The proper response is for the Shafi'i to rebut that proof. The student should understand that this objection technique requires the objector to provide evidence for the specification, not just claim it.

Key Terms
تخصيص العموم
عموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second example of specification, what is the Hanafi's basis for specifying the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him' to men only?
A) A verse in the Qur'an exempts women.
B) A specific proof from the Sunnah indicating women apostates are not killed.
C) Consensus (ijma') that female apostates are only imprisoned.
D) Analogy (qiyas) with the punishment for adultery.

Key Takeaway
The objection of specification does not defeat a general proof; it requires the arguer to critically examine the specifying evidence, and if that evidence fails, the general stands.

Takhsees al-‘Umum: Specification as a Dialectical Objection
Defines takhsees al-‘umum and illustrates its use as an objection with examples from jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an objection using takhsees al-‘umum in a dialectical exchange and explain the proper response to it.

Core Idea
Specification restricts a general proof without denying it – the opponent's move is to limit, not invalidate.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), the opponent often uses a technique called takhsees al-‘umum: arguing that a general proof verse or hadith is actually restricted to certain individuals. This does not reject the proof but challenges its universal applicability. The proponent must respond by engaging with the evidence for specification, not by ignoring it. Let's dissect how this works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of takhsees al-‘umum
The author defines it as 'excluding some of what the expression encompasses.' In jadal, this means the opponent claims the proponent's general scriptural proof does not cover all individuals it appears to cover.
2
Identify the proponent's general proof and the opponent's specification claim
In each example, first note the general verse or hadith the proponent (al-mustadill) uses. Then see how the opponent (al-mu‘tarid) restricts it to a subset.
3
Analyze the proponent's required response
The author states that the proponent must 'examine the proof' – i.e., evaluate the evidence brought forward for specification. If it is invalid, reject it; if valid, discuss it as one would any independent proof. Only after defeating the specification evidence is the general meaning conceded.
This is crucial: the proponent does not argue that the text is not general; rather, he argues that the specification evidence is weak or inapplicable.
4
Apply the pattern to the apostate woman example
Second example: al-Shafi‘i uses 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him' generally. The Hanafi objects it is specific to men. The response is the same: discuss the evidence for specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني: تخصيص العموم
التخصيص عند الأصوليين: إخراج بعض ما يتناوله اللفظ. والاعتراض بتخصيص العموم يكون بأن يستدل المستدل بعموم آية، فيحملها المعترض على بعض أفرادها فحسب، دون بقية الأفراد. مثاله: استدلال الشافعي في قتل شيوخ المشركين بقوله عز وجل: ... فَاقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ ... فيقول الحنفي معترضا: هي مخصوصة في الشيوخ بدليل. وهنا لا جواب للمستدل إلا النظر في الدليل هل يصلح للتخصيص أو لا؟ فإن لم يصلح رده، وإن صلح تكلم عليه كلامه على الأدلة المبتدأة، فإذا تكلم عليه سلم له الظاهر والعموم. ومنه أيضًا أن يستدل الشافعي في قتل المرتدة بعموم قوله: (من بدل دينه فاقتلوه) فيعترضه الحنفي بأنه خاص بالرجال دون النساء، بدليل. والجواب: أن يتكلم الشافعي على الدليل الذي تأول به أو خص به ليسلم له الظاهر والعموم.
The Second Demand: Specification of the General. Specification according to the usuliyyun is: the exclusion of some of what the expression encompasses. And the objection by specification of the general is that the proponent adduces the generality of a verse, so the opponent restricts it to only some of its individuals, excluding the rest. Its example: al-Shafi‘i's argument in killing the polytheist elders by His saying, the Exalted: '...then kill the polytheists...' So the Hanafi says, objecting: It is specified regarding the elders by a proof. And here there is no response for the proponent except to examine the proof: is it valid for specification or not? If it is not valid, he rejects it; and if it is valid, he discusses it as he discusses independent proofs. When he discusses it, the apparent meaning and generality are conceded to him. And also from that: al-Shafi‘i argues in killing the apostate woman by the generality of his saying: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' So the Hanafi objects to him that it is specific to men, not women, by a proof. And the answer: that al-Shafi‘i should discuss the proof that is used for interpretation or specification so that the apparent meaning and generality are conceded to him.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a definition and illustration of a dialectical objection known as 'specification of the general' (takhsees al-‘umum). The author first defines the term: specification is the exclusion of some individuals from the scope of a general expression. In jadal, the objection works by the opponent claiming that the proponent's proof (a general verse) is actually specified to only part of its potential individuals. Two examples are given. First, al-Shafi‘i uses the general command 'kill the polytheists' to argue for killing polytheist elders. The Hanafi opponent objects that this verse is specified with respect to elders by some evidence. The proponent's only recourse is to examine that evidence: if it is invalid, he rejects it; if valid, he discusses it as any other proof, and once he has argued against it, the general meaning of the verse is conceded to him. The second example concerns the hadith 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' Al-Shafi‘i uses its generality to argue for killing an apostate woman. The Hanafi objects that it is specific to men. Again, the response is that al-Shafi‘i should discuss the evidence used to specify the hadith. This section teaches how the takhsees objection operates and the proper response: engage with the specifying evidence directly, not ignore it.

Key Terms
تخصيص العموم
المستدل
المعترض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of al-Shafi‘i arguing from the verse 'kill the polytheists,' how does the Hanafi opponent object?
A) He denies the authenticity of the verse.
B) He claims the verse is abrogated.
C) He claims the verse is specified to exclude elders.
D) He argues from a different verse.

Key Takeaway
Takhsees al-‘umum is a dialectical strategy that restricts a general proof to a subset; the proponent must respond by critiquing the evidence for specification, not by insisting on the generality alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The opponent objects that it is specified to only pre-Islamic riba (riba al-jahiliyya). What should the proponent's response be according to the rules of jadal?

Dialectic -- Jadal
beginner

The Dialectical Objection of Contraversion (Mu‘arada)
Defines mu‘arada and introduces its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mu‘arada according to al-Baji, identify its two main types, and explain why the counter-proof must be equal or stronger.

Core Idea
A valid objection in debate must match or exceed the proof it counters.

Every dialectician needs to know how to properly object to an opponent's proof. The mu‘arada is a sophisticated tool: you do not simply deny the proof; you offer a counter-proof of equal or greater strength. Al-Baji's definition sets the standard, and this lesson unpacks its conditions and categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of mu‘arada
Locate al-Baji's definition in the passage: 'confronting the proponent with a proof like his proof or stronger than it.' Notice the condition of equality or strength.
The key phrase is 'مثل دليله أو بما هو أقوى منه'.
2
Understand the condition of strength
The passage states that a weaker counter-proof is not considered mu‘arada in jadal, even if it is linguistically a contraversion. This sets a high bar for objections.
Why would a weaker proof be invalid in debate? Because it does not refute the original proof.
3
Distinguish the two types of mu‘arada using the Qur'an
The passage divides mu‘arada in Qur'anic arguments into two: by explicit text (nass) and by cause ('illa). Note that these correspond to different ways of countering a proof.
This classification is specific to the source of the proof (nass or 'illa).
4
Apply the definition to a sample scenario
Imagine a proponent uses a Qur'anic verse to support a ruling. To make a valid mu‘arada, the opponent must bring another verse that is at least as strong in textual force. This will be the basis for the next lesson.
Strength can be measured by generality, clarity, or other factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع
الاعتراض بالمعارضة
المعارضة، كما عرفها الإمام أبو الوليد الباجي، هي: مقابلة الخصم للمستدل بمثل دليله أو بما هو أقوى منه. ومعنى ذلك أن يستدل المستدل بدليل فيسلم السائل صحته ويعارضه بدليل مثله أو أقوى منه، ولو عارضه بدليل أضعف من دليله لكان معارضًا من جهة اللغة، لكنها ليست المعارضة التي يريدها أهل الجدل.
وسؤال المعارضة في الاستدلال بالقرآن الكريم نوعان: معارضة بالنطق، ومعارضة بعلة:
المطلب الأول: المعارضة بالنطق
وذلك أن يستدل المستدل بآية في مسألة من المسائل، فيعترض عليه المعترض بآية أخرى تفيد نقيض ما تفيده الآية المستدل بها في المسألة ذاتها.
ولا يخلو الدليل المعترض به من القرآن من أحد ثلاثة أحوال، فهو إما أن يكون أخص من المستدل به، أو أعم منه، أو مثله في العموم.
أ - أن يكون الدليل المستدل به أخص من المعترض به عليه: ومثاله: أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنفي على صحة عقد البيع يوم الجمعة إذا قعد الإمام على المنبر وشرع في الخطبة، عملا بقوله تعالى: (وَأَحَلَّ اللهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا) فيعارضه المالكي بقوله تعالى: (... فَاسْعَوْا إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللَّهِ وَذَرُوا الْبَيْعَ) والنهي يقتضي الفساد، فوجب أن يكون البيع في ذلك الوقت فاسدا.
وربما أجاب بعض أصحاب أبي حنيفة على هذا الاعتراض بقولهم: إن الآية
The Seventh Section: Objection by Contraversion. Contraversion, as defined by Imam Abu al-Walid al-Baji, is: the opponent's confronting the proponent with a proof like his proof or stronger than it. The meaning of that is that the proponent adduces a proof, and the questioner concedes its soundness but counters it with a proof like it or stronger. And if he counters it with a proof weaker than his proof, he would be a contravener from the perspective of language, but it is not the contraversion intended by the people of jadal. And the issue of contraversion in using the noble Qur'an as proof is of two types: contraversion by explicit text (nass) and contraversion by cause ('illa). The First Demand: Contraversion by Explicit Text. That is that the proponent adduces a verse concerning a certain issue, so the opponent objects with another verse that implies the opposite of what the proponent's verse implies in that same issue. And the proof objected with from the Qur'an is not free from one of three states: either it is more specific than the proof adduced, or more general than it, or equal to it in generality. A - That the proof adduced is more specific than the proof objected with: Its example: that al-Shafi‘i or the Hanafi argues for the validity of a sale contract on Friday when the imam sits on the pulpit and begins the sermon, acting on His saying, the Exalted: 'And Allah has permitted sale and forbidden usury.' So the Maliki counters him with His saying, the Exalted: '... hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave sale.' And prohibition necessitates invalidity, so it must be that the sale at that time is invalid. And perhaps some of Abu Hanifa's followers answered this objection by saying: indeed the verse...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage introduces a key dialectical objection: mu‘arada (contraversion). The author cites the definition of al-Baji: the opponent counters the proponent's proof with an equal or stronger proof. If the counter-proof is weaker, it is not considered proper mu‘arada in jadal. The discussion then narrows to contraversion using the Qur'an, which is of two types: by explicit text (nass) and by cause ('illa). The first type is detailed here. In textual contraversion, the opponent brings a different verse that implies the opposite ruling on the same issue. The relationship between the two verses falls into three cases: the objecting verse may be more specific, more general, or equal in generality to the original verse. The passage then provides an example for the first case: a Hanafi or Shafi‘i argues that a sale on Friday during the sermon is valid based on the general permission of sale (al-Baqara 2:275). The Maliki opponent counters with the verse from Surat al-Jumu‘a (62:9) which commands leaving sale and hastening to the remembrance of Allah. Since the command implies prohibition, the sale would be invalid. The text ends with a possible Hanafi response, which is cut off but indicates that the Hanafi may argue that the verse is not an absolute prohibition. This passage teaches the mechanics of textual contraversion and emphasizes the importance of comparing the generality of the two verses.

النطق
العلة

Checkpoint
According to al-Baji, what is required for a valid mu‘arada (contraversion) in jadal?
A) The counter-proof must be from the Qur'an.
B) The counter-proof must be weaker than the original.
C) The counter-proof must be equal or stronger than the original proof.
D) The counter-proof must be more specific.

Key Takeaway
A valid mu‘arada requires the counter-proof to be equal or stronger; a weaker counter is not a proper objection in dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a proponent's proof from the Qur'an (e.g., '...do not approach prayer while you are intoxicated' (al-Nisa’ 4:43) used to argue that intoxication invalidates prayer), find a counter-verse that is equal or stronger in generality to make a valid mu‘arada.

Textual Contraversion: Comparing Generality
Exemplifies textual contraversion with the Friday sale debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three cases of textual contraversion (more specific, more general, equal) and evaluate whether a given textual mu‘arada is valid based on generality.

Core Idea
The strength of a textual contraversion depends on the relative generality of the contested verses.

Now that you understand the general concept of mu‘arada, this lesson dives into the specifics of textual contraversion. When two verses appear to conflict, you must analyze their generality. The passage outlines three possible relationships and gives a concrete example from the Friday sale debate. This is a practical skill for any student of usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases of textual mu‘arada
The passage states that the objecting verse is either more specific, more general, or equal in generality to the original verse. List these three cases.
Generality here refers to the scope of the ruling (al-‘umum).
2
Analyze the example: Friday sale debate
The Hanafi/Shafi‘i proponent uses 'And Allah has permitted sale' (general permission). The Maliki counters with 'leave sale' (command implying prohibition). Determine the generality relationship: the prohibiting verse is more specific because it applies only to the time of the Friday sermon.
The original verse is general in permitting all sales; the counter-verse is specific to Friday sermon time.
3
Evaluate the validity of the mu‘arada
Since the counter-verse is more specific, it does not necessarily negate the general permission; it may be an exception. The mu‘arada is valid as an objection, but the proponent can respond by restricting the general verse.
The Hanafi response might argue that the permission overrides the prohibition or that the command is not absolute.
4
Apply the analysis to other juristic examples
Think of another case: a proponent uses a general verse on inheritance, and the opponent cites a specific verse excluding certain relatives. Determine which case applies and what the outcome should be.
This skill is essential for reconciling contradictory textual evidence.

Mu'arada bi al-Nutq: Counter-Argument by Text in Jadal
Author defines mu'arada and explains three cases of counter-text in Qur'anic argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the relationship between two conflicting Qur'anic verses (more specific, more general, or equal) and apply the appropriate jadal response to resolve the objection.

Core Idea
A valid mu'arada must match or exceed the original proof in strength, not merely contradict.

In jadal (dialectical disputation), a key skill is responding to a proof by bringing a counter-proof from the same source — the Qur'an. But not every contradiction counts: the counter-proof must meet or exceed the original in strength. This lesson unpacks the concept of mu'arada bi al-nutq, focusing on how to evaluate the relative scope of conflicting verses and what responses each situation demands. We will walk through al-Baji's definition and the three cases the author presents, using the furnished examples to apply the rules yourself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proof and the counter-proof
Locate the initial verse used by the mustadill (proponent) and the opposing verse used by the mu'tarid (objector). In the examples, note the specific rulings at stake (e.g., validity of a sale on Friday).
Look for the verses cited and the issue under debate.
2
Determine the scope relationship (akhass vs a'amm vs equal)
Compare the two verses: Which one has a narrower application (specific) and which has a broader application (general)? If neither is clearly broader or narrower, they are equal in generality. Use the text's classification: original more specific, original more general, or equal.
The classification is based on the wording and context of the verses, not on the strength of the arguments.
3
Recall the jadal rule for each case
If the original is more specific than the counter, the more specific verse governs (takhasus). If the original is more general, the more specific counter-verse takes precedence. If equal, the proponent should harmonise or argue for one over the other based on agreed-upon criteria.
The author gives specific responses for each case; internalise them.
4
Apply the rule to the example and evaluate the response
Take the sale-on-Friday example: original (2:275) is general, counter (62:9) is specific. According to the rule, the specific should win, so the sale is invalid. The Hanafi response is to argue that the general is agreed upon and the specific disputed; evaluate whether this is a valid jadal move.
The Hanafi response is an alternative strategy, not the default rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع: الاعتراض بالمعارضة. المعارضة، كما عرفها الإمام أبو الوليد الباجي، هي: «مقابلة الخصم للمستدل بمثل دليله أو بما هو أقوى منه»... ومعنى ذلك أن يستدل المستدل بدليل فيسلم السائل صحته ويعارضه بدليل مثله أو أقوى منه ولو عارضه بدليل أضعف من دليله لكان معارضًا من جهة اللغة، لكنها ليست المعارضة التي يريدها أهل الجدل. وسؤال المعارضة في الاستدلال بالقرآن الكريم نوعان: معارضة بالنطق، ومعارضة بعلة. المطلب الأول: المعارضة بالنطق. وذلك أن يستدل المستدل بآية في مسألة من المسائل، فيعترض عليه المعترض بآية أخرى تفيد نقيض ما تفيده الآية المستدل بها في المسألة ذاتها. ولا يخلو الدليل المعتَرَض به من القرآن من أحد ثلاثة أحوال، فهو إما أن يكون أخص من المستدل به، أو أعم منه، أو مثله في العموم. أ- أن يكون الدليل المستدل به أخص من المعترض به عليه: ومثاله: أن يستدل الشافعي أو الحنفي على صحة عقد البيع يوم الجمعة إذا قعد الإمام على المنبر وشرع في الخطبة، عملا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَحَلَّ اللهُ الْبَيْعَ وَحَرَّمَ الرِّبَا﴾، فيعارضه المالكي بقوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِذَا نُودِيَ لِلصَّلَاةِ مِنْ يَوْمِ الْجُمُعَةِ فَاسْعَوْا إِلَى ذِكْرِ اللَّهِ وَذَرُوا الْبَيْعَ﴾ والنهي يقتضي الفساد، فوجب أن يكون البيع في ذلك الوقت فاسدا. وربما أجاب بعض أصحاب أبي حنيفة على هذا الاعتراض بقولهم: إن الآية التي استدللنا بها متفق عليها أما الآية التي اعترضتم بها فمختلف فيها، والعام المتفق عليه أولى من الخاص المختلف فيه. ب- أن يكون الدليل المستدل به أعم من المعترض به عليه: ومثاله: أن يستدل المالكي على تحريم وطء الحائض قبل الغسل بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْتَزِلُوا النِّسَاءَ فِي الْمَحِيضِ وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ﴾. فيعارضه الحنفي بعدم التحريم، بقوله تعالى: ﴿نِسَاؤُكُمْ حَرْثٌ لَكُمْ فَأْتُوا حَرْثَكُمْ أَنَّى شِئْتُمْ﴾. وجواب المالكي على هذا الاعتراض أن يقول: إن الآية التي استدللنا بها يُقضى بها على الآية التي اعترضتم بها، لأن ما استدللنا به يتناول موضع الخلاف على وجه خاص وما اعترضتم به يتناوله بعمومه على وجه محتمل، فتحمل الآية المعترض بها على غير الحائض. ج- أن يكون الدليل المستدل به مثل الدليل المعترض به عليه: ولا يخلو هذا النوع من ثلاثة أحوال: فهما إما أن يكونا عامين، أو خاصين، أو كل واحد منهما خاص من وجه عام من وجه. فأما كونهما عامين فمثل أن يستدل المالكي في جواز المن والفداء بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِمَّا مَنَّا بَعْدُ وَإِمَّا فِدَاءً حَتَّى تَضَعَ الْحَرْبُ أَوْزَارَهَا﴾. فيعارضه الحنفي بقوله تعالى: ﴿مَا كَانَ لِنَبِيٍّ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُ أَسْرَى حَتَّى يُثْخِنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا وَاللَّهُ يُرِيدُ الْآخِرَةَ﴾. ويمكن للمالكي أن يجيب على هذا الاعتراض بأحد وجوه: أحدها: أن يقول: أنا أجمع بين الآيتين فأحمل آيتكم على أن ذلك حكمه إذا رأى المصلحة فيه وأحمل آيتنا على أن ذلك حكمه إذا رأى المصلحة في المن والفداء، والجمع بين الآيتين واستعمالهما أولى من إسقاط إحداهما.
The seventh section: Objection by counter-argument (mu'arada). Mu'arada, as defined by Imam Abu al-Walid al-Baji, is: 'The opponent's confrontation of the one who brings the proof with a proof like his proof or with one stronger than it.' ... The meaning of that is that the one who brings the proof uses a proof, and the questioner acknowledges its validity and counters it with a proof like it or stronger than it. If he countered it with a proof weaker than his proof, he would be a counter-arguer in terms of language, but that is not the mu'arada intended by the people of jadal. The question of mu'arada in inference by the Noble Qur'an is of two types: mu'arada by text (nutq) and mu'arada by cause ('illa). The first sub-section: Mu'arada by text. That is, that the one who brings the proof uses a verse concerning some issue, and then the objector objects to him with another verse that conveys the opposite of what the verse used as proof conveys concerning that same issue. The proof objected with from the Qur'an does not depart from one of three conditions: it is either more specific (akhass) than the proof used, or more general (a'amm) than it, or like it in generality. A. The case where the proof used is more specific than the proof objected with: Example: that the Shafi'i or Hanafi infers the validity of a sale contract on Friday when the imam has sat on the pulpit and commenced the sermon, acting on the Almighty's saying: 'And Allah has permitted trade and forbidden usury' (2:275). Then the Maliki objects to him with the Almighty's saying: 'O you who have believed, when the call is made for prayer on the day of congregation, then hasten to the remembrance of Allah and leave off trade' (62:9), and prohibition necessitates invalidity, so the sale at that time must be invalid. Perhaps some of Abu Hanifa's followers answered this objection by saying: the verse we used as proof is agreed upon, while the verse with which you objected is disputed; the general that is agreed upon is more worthy than the specific that is disputed. B. The case where the proof used is more general than the proof objected with: Example: that the Maliki infers the prohibition of intercourse with a menstruating woman before bathing from the Almighty's saying: 'So keep away from women during menstruation and do not approach them until they are pure' (2:222). Then the Hanafi objects to him with lack of prohibition, by the Almighty's saying: 'Your wives are a tilth for you, so come to your tilth however you will' (2:223). The Maliki's response to this objection is to say: the verse we used as proof takes precedence over the verse with which you objected, because what we used as proof addresses the point of dispute in a specific manner, and what you objected with addresses it in a general manner that is ambiguous, so the objected verse is interpreted as referring to other than the menstruating woman. C. The case where the proof used is like the proof objected with: This type does not depart from three conditions: either both are general, both are specific, or each one is specific in one respect and general in another. As for their being both general, an example is that the Maliki infers the permissibility of manumission or ransom from the Almighty's saying: 'Then either a favor afterward or ransom until the war lays down its burdens' (47:4). Then the Hanafi objects with the Almighty's saying: 'It is not for a prophet to have captives until he inflicts a massacre in the land. You desire the goods of this world while Allah desires the Hereafter' (8:67). The Maliki can answer this objection in one of several ways: one of them is to say: I combine between the two verses: I interpret your verse as referring to the ruling when he sees expediency in that, and I interpret our verse as referring to the ruling when he sees expediency in manumission and ransom, and combining the two verses and using them is more appropriate than dropping one of them.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is from a treatise on jadal (dialectical disputation). It defines and explains the concept of 'mu'arada' (counter-argument), specifically the type known as 'mu'arada bi al-nutq' (counter-argument by text), where a Qur'anic proof is opposed by another Qur'anic verse. The author begins by quoting al-Baji's definition of mu'arada: it is not any contradiction, but a response that meets or exceeds the original proof in strength. He then notes that counter-argument with the Qur'an is of two kinds: by explicit text (nutq) or by cause ('illa). The first kind, mu'arada bi al-nutq, is discussed here in detail. The objector brings a verse that implies the opposite of the original verse's implication on the same issue. The relationship between the original proof (al-mustadall bih) and the countering proof (al-mu'tarad bih) falls into three cases: the original is more specific than the counter, the original is more general, or they are equal in generality. For the first case, the author gives the example of a Shafi'i or Hanafi using the general permission of trade (2:275) to argue that a sale during Friday sermon is valid. The Maliki objects with the specific command to leave trade at that time (62:9), which implies prohibition and thus invalidity. A Hanafi response is that the general verse is agreed upon, while the specific verse is disputed, so the agreed-upon general takes precedence. For the second case, the original is more general: the Maliki uses the specific prohibition of approaching menstruating women (2:222) to forbid intercourse before washing; the Hanafi counters with the general permission 'come to your tilth however you will' (2:223). The Maliki's response is that the specific verse governs the general one because it addresses the point of controversy specifically. The third case deals with both proofs equal in generality, either both general, both specific, or mixed. For both general, the example is the Maliki's use of the verse permitting manumission or ransom of captives (47:4) versus the Hanafi's use of the verse prohibiting taking captives for worldly gain (8:67). The Maliki can resolve the apparent contradiction by harmonizing: the prohibition applies when there is no expediency in freeing or ransoming, while the permission applies when there is expediency. Thus, the passage teaches a crucial jadal skill: how to handle textual objections by understanding the relative scope of the verses and using interpretative principles to reconcile or prioritize them.

Key Terms
المعارضة
النطق
الأخص
الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The original proof (2:275) is more specific than the counter-proof (62:9).
B) The original proof is more general than the counter-proof.
C) Both proofs are equal in generality.
D) The counter-proof is weaker than the original proof.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada bi al-nutq requires the counter-verse to be equal or stronger in scope; the proper response depends on whether it is more specific, more general, or equal, with specific texts typically taking precedence over general ones.

When Evidence is More General than the Objection
Al-Baji explains the priority of specific evidence over general objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an arguer's evidence is more specific than the objector's evidence and explain why the specific takes precedence in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
Specific evidence trumps general objection in jadal.

In juristic debate (jadal), not all evidence is equal. Al-Baji teaches a key principle: if your evidence directly addresses the disputed point in a specific way, and the opponent's objection is general and merely probable, your evidence takes priority. This lesson unpacks that principle with a real fiqhi example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
In the Maliki-Hanafi example, the point of contention is whether intercourse with a menstruating woman before bath is forbidden. Identify the exact issue.
2
Identify the Maliki's evidence
The Maliki uses Qur'an 2:222, which commands withdrawal during menses and prohibits approach until purification. Note how it specifically addresses menstruation.
3
Identify the Hanafi's objection
The Hanafi objects with Qur'an 2:223, which gives general permission to approach wives. This verse does not specifically mention menstruation.
4
Apply the principle
The Maliki claims his verse takes precedence because it is specific (khāṣṣ) to the disputed situation, while the opponent's verse is general (ʿāmm) and thus probable. The specific overrides the general.
5
Conclude with the rule
The rule is that specific evidence is preferred over general probable evidence in jadal. This ensures that direct textual rulings are not undermined by vague generalities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - أن يكون الدليل المستدل به أعم من المعترض به عليه : ومثاله : أن يستدل المالكي على تحريم وطء الحائض قبل الغسل بقوله تعالى : فَاعْتَزِلُوا النِّسَاء فِي الْمَحِيضِ وَلَا نَقْرَبُوهُنَّ حَتَّى يَطْهُرْن . . . ) (2) . فيعارضه الحنفي بعدم التحريم، بقوله تعالى: ﴿ نِسَاؤُكُمْ حَرْثٌ لَكُمْ فَأْتُوا حَرْثَكُمْ أَنَّى ، وجواب المالكي على هذا الاعتراض أن يقول : إن الآية التي استدللنا بها يُقضى بها على الآية التي اعترضتم بها، لأن ما استدللنا به يتناول موضع الخلاف على وجه خاص وما اعترضتم به يتناوله بعمومه على وجه محتمل، فتحمل الآية المعترض بها على غير الحائض
B - That the evidence used is more general than the objection against it: And its example: The Maliki argues for the prohibition of intercourse with a menstruating woman before the bath by His saying, 'So keep away from women during menstruation and do not approach them until they are pure' (2). The Hanafi objects with the absence of prohibition, by His saying, 'Your wives are a tilth for you, so go to your tilth as you will' (3). And the Maliki's answer to this objection is to say: The verse we used takes precedence over the verse you objected with, because what we used covers the point of dispute in a specific manner, and what you objected with covers it in a general manner that is probable, so the objected verse is interpreted as referring to other than the menstruating woman.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition from al-Baji's Minhaj on how to handle objections (i'tirad) in juristic debate. The author defines the situation where the evidence (dalil) presented by the original arguer is more general than the objection (mu'tarad bih) raised by the opponent. He illustrates with a fiqhi example: The Maliki argues that intercourse with a menstruating woman before ritual purification is forbidden, citing the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Baqarah (2:222) which commands withdrawal during menses and prohibits approach until purification. The Hanafi objects using the verse (2:223) which gives permission to approach wives (the 'tilth'). The Maliki's rejoinder is that his own verse specifically addresses the disputed situation (menstruation), while the opponent's verse is general and thus can be interpreted restrictively to exclude the menstruating woman. The principle is that the specific ruling is preferred over the general probable one. This follows the usuli maxim that 'the specific takes precedence over the general'.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, when the original arguer's evidence is specific to the dispute and the objector's evidence is general and probable, the specific evidence takes precedence.

Resolving Conflicting General Texts: Jam', Naskh, and Tarjih
Al-Baji details the first subcase when conflicting texts are both general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three dialectical responses (jam', naskh, tarjih) that a Maliki scholar can use when a Hanafi objects to a general textual proof, and evaluate which response is preferred in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Reconciliation (jam') is always preferable to discarding a contradictory text.

In Islamic legal jadal, when two pieces of evidence conflict, the first instinct is to reconcile them rather than reject one. Al-Baji presents a classic case: the Maliki proof for mann/fida' from Surah Muhammad and the Hanafi counterproof from Surah al-Anfal. The Maliki has three dialectical moves: jam', naskh, or tarjih. This lesson walks you through each, preparing you to use these strategies in actual usul discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting proofs and their generality
Note that both verses are 'amm (general) in their commands. The Maliki cites 47:4; the Hanafi cites 8:67. Both are general in wording, so they fall under the first subcase.
Generality means the command applies to all instances without exception.
2
Understand the Maliki's first response: jam' (reconciliation)
The Maliki claims the verses apply to different situations based on maslaha (public interest). This is reconciliation by contextual specification (ta'yin). It preserves both texts.
Jam' is the preferred method; dropping one text is a last resort.
3
Understand the Maliki's second response: naskh (abrogation)
The Maliki argues his verse is later (revealed after Badr) and thus abrogates the Hanafi's verse. This requires establishing chronological order and that naskh is possible.
Naskh is only valid if the later verse is definitely later and abrogation is accepted.
4
Understand the Maliki's third response: tarjih (preference)
The Maliki gives preference to his verse by external criteria (e.g., stronger chain, more specific context). This is a fallback if jam' and naskh are not possible.
Tarjih involves weighing evidence; the criteria vary among usulis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - أن يكون الدليل المستدل به مثل الدليل المعترض به عليه : ولا يخلو هذا النوع من ثلاثة أحوال : فهما إما أن يكونا عامين، أو خاصين، أو كل واحد منهما خاص من وجه عام من وجه. فأما كونهما عامين فمثل أن يستدل المالكي في جواز المن والفداء بقوله تعالى : لله ... فَإما مَنَا بَعْدُ وَإِمَّا فِدَاءً حَتَّى تَضَعَ الْحَرْبُ أَوْزَارَهَا .. فيعارضه الحنفي بقوله تعالى : مَا كَانَ لِنَبِي أَن يَكُونَ لَهُ أَسْرَى حَتَّى يُشْخِنَ فِي الْأَرْضِ تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا وَاللهُ يُرِيدُ الآخِرَةُ ... (6) ويمكن للمالكي أن يجيب على هذا الاعتراض بأحد وجوه : أحدها: أن يقول : أنا أجمع بين الآيتين فأحمل آيتكم على أن ذلك حكمه إذا رأى المصلحة فيه وأحمل آيتنا على أن ذلك حكمه إذا رأى المصلحة في المن والفداء، والجمع بين الآيتين واستعمالهما أولى من إسقاط إحداهما. والثاني : أن يقول بنسخ الآية الأولى بالثانية بأن يقول : آيتنا متأخرة وآيتكم متقدمة، وذلك أن آيتكم نزلت في بدر وآيتنا نزلت بعدها . والثالث : أن يرجح استعمال آيته على آيتهم ببعض أنواع الترجيح، إن وجد إلى ذلك سيلا
J - That the evidence used is equal to the objection against it: And this type is not free from three states: They are either both general, or both specific, or each one is specific from one aspect and general from another. As for them being both general, an example is that the Maliki argues for the permissibility of man (gratuitous release) and fida' (ransom) by His saying '...then either a favour after that or ransom until the war lays down its burdens...' (4), and the Hanafi objects by His saying 'It is not for a prophet to have captives until he has made a slaughter in the land; you desire the goods of this world while Allah desires the Hereafter...' (6). And the Maliki can answer this objection in one of three ways: One: to say 'I reconcile between the two verses, so I interpret your verse as applying when he sees maslaha in that, and I interpret our verse as applying when he sees maslaha in man and fida'. Reconciliation and using both is better than dropping one.' Second: to say that the first verse is abrogated by the second, by saying our verse is later and your verse is earlier, as your verse was revealed at Badr and our verse after it. Third: to give preference to using his verse over their verse by some type of tarjih, if that is possible.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage continues al-Baji's exposition of types of objection when evidence and objection are equal in generality. He first states three possible states: both general, both specific, or each general from one aspect and specific from another. Then he expands on the first state (both general). Using a historical fiqhi example: the permissibility of mann (freeing) and fida' (ransoming) captives. The Maliki cites Surah Muhammad (47:4) which allows either mann or fida after the battle; the Hanafi objects with Surah al-Anfal (8:67) which prohibits taking captives until victory. The Maliki has three possible responses: 1) Harmonization (jam'): interpret each verse for a different context (when maslaha dictates). 2) Abrogation (naskh): claim the Maliki's verse is later (post-Badr) and thus abrogates the earlier one. 3) Preference (tarjih): give his verse preference by some criterion (e.g., stronger chain, context). The principle emphasized is that reconciliation of both is superior to dropping one entirely.

جمع
نسخ
ترجيح

Key Takeaway
The first and best dialectical response when two general texts conflict is to reconcile them by interpreting each as addressing a different context, rather than abrogating or preferring one.

Resolving Conflict Between Two Specific Texts
Al-Baji explains the rare case of two specific texts conflicting and the resolution through naskh or tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two specific texts conflict and apply the correct resolution method (naskh or tarjih) based on historical knowledge or preference criteria.

Core Idea
When two specific texts conflict, the later abrogates the earlier; if unknown, prefer the stronger.

In Islamic legal theory, it is rare for two specific texts (khass) to directly conflict, but when they do, the scholar must have a method to resolve the apparent contradiction. This passage from Al-Baji explains the two-step approach: first check for historical order to apply naskh, and if that is unknown, resort to tarjih (preference) using established criteria. Understanding this method is crucial for any student of usul al-fiqh or jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting specific texts
Look for two textual evidences that both apply to the same particular case but yield different rulings. Confirm that both are specific (khass) in scope.
If one is general, this case doesn't apply.
2
Check for known chronological order
If you know which text was revealed later, the later text abrogates (naskh) the earlier one. That resolves the conflict.
Naskh requires certain conditions, like the later text being revealed after the earlier.
3
If chronology is unknown, apply tarjih
Use preference criteria to choose one ruling over the other. Common criteria include stronger chain of transmission, more specific context, or agreement with other principles.
Tarjih is a fallback when naskh is not possible.
4
Apply the resolution to a real usul scenario
Practice by taking a hypothetical conflict between two specific hadiths and walk through the steps.
This mirrors the actual work of a faqih or muhaddith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كانا خاصين لا يمكن استعمالهما معا، ورغم أن هذا النوع نادر جدا، إلا أن الطريق في الجواب عليه إن وجد أن ينسخ الدليل الأول بالآخر منهما، وإن لم يعرف التاريخ رجح الحكم بأحدهما على الحكم بالآخر بوجه من وجوه الترجيح .
If they are both specific and cannot be used together, and although this type is very rare, the way to answer it, if possible, is to abrogate the first evidence with the other, and if the chronology is not known, prefer the ruling of one over the other by some type of preference.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a brief treatment of the second subcase where both the evidence and the objection are specific (khass) in scope, meaning each text addresses a particular situation without general applicability. Al-Baji notes this situation is very rare. The solution is similar to the previous: if historical order is known, the later text abrogates the earlier. If not, one must resort to preference (tarjih) based on some criterion, such as stronger transmitter or more specific context. This reflects the usuli principle that specific-to-specific conflicts are resolved through naskh or tarjih.

Key Takeaway
When two specific texts conflict, resolve by either naskh (if chronology known) or tarjih (if not).

Resolving Conflicts Between Partially Overlapping Texts
Al-Baji explains the third subcase where texts are partly general and partly specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of tarjih (preference) to resolve a conflict between two texts that are each general from one aspect and specific from another, using the criterion of the text's intended purpose (maqṣad).

Core Idea
When texts overlap in generality and specificity, the one intended to clarify the disputed rule takes precedence.

This lesson examines a dialectical scenario from al-Baji's work on usul al-fiqh. When two legal texts are each general in one aspect and specific in another, neither can simply be restricted by the other. The resolution lies in preferring the text whose purpose is to address the specific ruling in dispute. This method is foundational for advanced juristic debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting texts and their aspects of generality/specificity
Read the example: the Maliki cites Quran 4:23 (forbidding combining two sisters) which is general to all forms of marriage but specific to sisters. The opponent cites Quran 4:36 (permitting what right hands possess) which is general to all women but specific to possession.
Note the phrasing 'general from one aspect, specific from another' – both texts have a scope that is broad in one dimension and narrow in another.
2
Recognize the stalemate caused by reciprocal restriction
If the Maliki restricts the opponent's verse to non-sisters, the opponent can equally restrict the Maliki's verse to non-possession. This shows that simple restriction is not decisive.
This stalemate is the problem al-Baji addresses: neither side can unilaterally narrow the other's text without symmetry.
3
Introduce the criterion of maqṣad (purpose)
Al-Baji argues that the Maliki's verse was revealed to clarify the specific ruling about combining sisters in marriage – its purpose (maqṣad) is directly about the disputed issue. The opponent's verse was about a general permission for marrying slave women, not about the specific issue of combining sisters.
The key is not mere generality or specificity, but what the text was intended to address.
4
Apply tarjih to prefer the more relevant text
The Maliki gives preference (tarjih) to his own verse because it is more directly relevant to the disputed ruling. The verse whose purpose is to clarify the matter under debate takes precedence over a verse that addresses a different context.
Tarjih here is based on relevance and purpose, not on external factors like authenticity or chronology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - 3 – وإن كان كل واحد منهما عامًا من وجه خاصا من وجه وذلك مثل أن يستدل المالكي على أنه لا يجمع بين الأختين بملك اليمين بقوله تعالى: ... وَأَن تَجْمَعُوا بَيْنَ الأُختين . . . ) (1) ، ولم يفرق بين النكاح وبين اليمين . وهنا يعارضه المخالف بقوله تعالى : .. وَمَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَنُكُمْ ... (2) ولم يفرق بين الأختين وغيرهما، فليس للمستدل أن يحمل آية المعترض على غير الأختين بأن آيته خاصة في الأختين، إلا وللمعترض أن يحمل آية المستدل على غير ملك اليمين بأن آيته خاصة في ملك اليمين والطريق في الجواب عن مثل هذا الاعتراض، إن وقع أن يرجح المستدل استعمال آيته على آية المعترض، وذلك بأن يقول : في هذا الموضع استعمال آيتنا أولى لأنه قصد بها بيان ما أبيح جمعه في النكاح وما منع منه ، وآيتكم إنما قصد بها بيان الحالة التي أجيز فيها نكاح الإماء، والآية التي قصد بها بيان الحكم المختلف فيه أولى من الآية التي لم يقصد بها ذلك
J-3 – And if each one is general from one aspect and specific from another, such as the Maliki arguing that it is not permissible to combine between two sisters by right hand possession (milk al-yamin) by His saying '...and that you combine between two sisters...' (1), and he did not differentiate between marriage and right hand possession. Here the opponent objects by His saying '...and what your right hands possess...' (2), and he did not differentiate between sisters and others. So it is not for the arguer to interpret the opponent's verse as applying to other than sisters, saying his verse is specific to sisters, without the opponent interpreting the arguer's verse as applying to other than right hand possession, saying his verse is specific to right hand possession. The way to answer such an objection, if it occurs, is that the arguer gives preference to using his verse over the opponent's verse, by saying: In this situation using our verse is preferable because it was intended to clarify what is permitted to combine in marriage and what is prohibited, while your verse was only intended to clarify the case in which marriage of slave women is permitted. The verse that was intended to clarify the disputed ruling is more worthy than the verse that was not intended for that.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage discusses the third subcase where each of the conflicting texts is general from one perspective and specific from another. Al-Baji gives a fiqhi example: The Maliki argues that combining two sisters in marriage through right hand possession (milk al-yamin) is prohibited, citing Surah al-Nisa (4:23) which forbids 'combining two sisters' without distinguishing between marriage and possession. The opponent uses Surah al-Nisa (4:36) which states 'what your right hands possess' without excluding sisters. The arguer cannot restrict the opponent's verse to non-sisters by claiming specificity, because the opponent can similarly restrict the arguer's verse to non-possession. The solution is for the arguer to give preference (tarjih) to his own verse by arguing that his verse's purpose was to clarify the specific ruling in dispute (combining sisters), whereas the opponent's verse was general about permissibility in a different context (marrying slave women). Thus the verse intended to address the issue at hand takes precedence.

Key Terms
عام من وجه خاص من وجه
ترجيح
مقصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of combining sisters by right hand possession, why does the Maliki claim his verse is preferable?
A) His verse is more authentic.
B) His verse was revealed later.
C) His verse was intended to clarify the specific disputed ruling about combining sisters.
D) His verse uses more emphatic language.

Key Takeaway
When two texts are each general from one aspect and specific from another, the text whose purpose (maqṣad) is directly about the disputed ruling is given preference (tarjih) over the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider a scenario: Verse A says 'Do not marry your mothers' (specific to marriage) but general to all mothers. Verse B says 'What your right hands possess is permissible' (general to all women) but specific to possession. Which verse should take precedence when determining if one's slave mother is permissible to marry? Use al-Baji's criterion of purpose to decide and explain your reasoning.
?
Task 2: Construct a similar dialectical exchange between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i using a different example (e.g., the ruling on wine versus nabidh) where each side cites a text that is general in one aspect and specific in another, and show how tarjih by purpose resolves it.
